Every line is one of the daily operations. By the time these are second nature, the rest of the course's code becomes readable on first pass.
Module 1 · Chapter 1.4

Modules — ESM, Imports & Exports

Chapter 0.2 wrote your first two-file project. This chapter is the full picture of how Node modules work — named exports, default exports, namespace imports, dynamic imports, and the older CommonJS style you will see in legacy code. Modules are how Node code is organised at any scale beyond one file.

Lesson 1.4How Node Code Is Organised

Concept Overview

A module is a single file with its own scope. Variables and functions declared in a module are private to it unless explicitly exported. Other modules import the exports to use them. Node supports two module systems. ESM — ES Modules, the modern standard — uses import and export; files have the .mjs extension or live in a project with "type": "module". CommonJS (CJS) — the older Node-specific system — uses require() and module.exports; files have .cjs or live in a project without the type flag. Modern code uses ESM; you will still see CJS in older libraries. ESM supports named exports (many per file), a default export (one per file), namespace imports (everything as one object), and dynamic import() (load a module on demand, returning a promise).

Plain English Explanation

Think of each .mjs file as a sealed box. Inside the box you write code that is private — nothing outside the file can see it. When you want something from the box to be available outside, you stick a label on it: export function greet() {...} says "this function is available." Another file then says import { greet } from './x.mjs' — it opens the box and takes a labelled item out.

There are three export shapes, used for different situations. A named export is the everyday one: export function greet() {...} — you can have many per file, and importers pull them out by name with curly braces: import { greet, farewell } from './x.mjs'. A default export is "the one main thing this file is about": export default class User {...} — only one default per file, and you import it without curly braces, picking any name you like: import User from './user.mjs'. A namespace import grabs everything at once as a single object: import * as math from './math.mjs' — useful when you want a clear prefix.

Older Node code uses a different syntax — const x = require('./x') and module.exports = .... That is CommonJS, the original Node module system. It still works, you will see it in libraries that have not migrated, and the two systems are mostly interchangeable — but ESM is the modern default and what this course uses. Worth recognising both when you see them.

Why It Matters

Every Node project beyond a hundred lines is split into modules. Knowing the four shapes of import — named, default, namespace, dynamic — and how to choose between them is the difference between code that reads naturally and code that fights its own structure. The choice between a default export and several named exports is not arbitrary: it shapes how callers write your library's name throughout their codebase. Module design is a deliberate engineering decision, made early in any project's life.

Real-World Example

A team writes auth.mjs exporting three functions: hashPassword, verifyPassword, createSession. They use named exports — three peers, no single "main" thing. A consumer writes import { hashPassword, createSession } from './auth.mjs' and the intent is plain. The same team writes UserRepository.mjs exporting one class: export default class UserRepository {...} — one main concept per file, default export. Consumers write import UserRepository from './UserRepository.mjs'. Both styles, chosen by intent: "many peer utilities" → named exports; "one primary thing" → default export. Mixing badly produces awkward call sites forever.

Enterprise Example

Enterprise codebases impose conventions on module shape, often via linter rules. A common convention: no default exports — only named — because named exports are refactor-friendly (tools can rename across a codebase reliably) while default exports can be imported under any name, making global rename harder. Another convention: barrel files (index.mjs that re-exports everything from a folder) so consumers can write import { x, y } from '../auth' instead of the full file path. Both are real trade-offs, and the conventions matter because at scale even a small piece of awkwardness multiplies across thousands of import sites. We will lay out a recommended convention in Module 5.

Step-by-Step Breakdown

How a module load resolves:

  1. Node sees import { greet } from './x.mjs'. It resolves ./x.mjs relative to the importing file's path.
  2. It reads that file, finds its exports, and binds them to the names requested in the import.
  3. If ./x.mjs itself has imports, those are resolved first, recursively, until the whole dependency graph is loaded.
  4. The module's top-level code runs once; subsequent imports of the same module return the same already-evaluated exports.
  5. For import * as math from './math.mjs', Node creates a namespace object holding every named export.
  6. For const m = await import('./x.mjs') — the dynamic form — the module is loaded on demand at runtime, returning a promise. This is useful for conditional loading, code splitting, and reducing startup cost.

Code Example

code
math.mjs — named exports, default export, all in one file// named exports
export function add(a, b) { return a + b; }
export function subtract(a, b) { return a - b; }
// a default export — one per file
export default function multiply(a, b) { return a * b; }
js
index.mjs — four ways to import from math.mjsimport multiply, { add, subtract } from './math.mjs';
import * as math from './math.mjs';

console.log('add(2,3):', add(2, 3));
console.log('subtract(10,4):', subtract(10, 4));
console.log('multiply(6,7):', multiply(6, 7));
console.log('math.add:', math.add(1, 1));

// Dynamic import — returns a promise, only loaded when needed.
// Useful for conditional or lazy-loaded modules.
const dynamic = await import('./math.mjs');
console.log('dynamic.add:', dynamic.add(5, 5));

The import multiply, { add, subtract } line combines a default import (no braces) with named imports (in braces) — both from the same file in one statement. The * as math form is occasionally useful; the dynamic import() at the bottom returns a promise so the module can be loaded conditionally.

JS & TS Together

TypeScript modules use the same syntax with optional type annotations on the exported functions:

code
math.tsexport function add(a: number, b: number): number { return a + b; }

TypeScript also adds export type and export interface for sharing types between modules — types are imported and exported the same way values are. Module 3.4 covers this in full.

Common Mistakes

Common Mistake

Default-exporting many things from one file. A file with ten "small" defaults is impossible to navigate. Default exports are for "this file is one main thing." Use named exports for collections of utilities.

Common Mistake

Circular imports. If a.mjs imports b.mjs which imports a.mjs, one of them sees the other as half-initialised. Either restructure so the dependency goes one way, or extract the shared part into a third file both can import.

Common Mistake

Mixing CJS and ESM without care. ESM can import CJS, but only the default; CJS importing ESM has to use dynamic import() because require() cannot load ESM directly. New code: stick to ESM end-to-end.

Principal Engineer Notes

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

A principal engineer prefers named exports by default. Named exports survive renames across a codebase: changing greet to greetUser propagates with confidence, because there is only one name for the import. Default exports can be imported under any name (import x from './greet.mjs') so a rename misses anywhere a developer chose a different local name. This is a small thing in a 10-file project and a large thing in a 1000-file codebase.

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

They keep modules small. A module that exports thirty things is doing too many jobs; split it. The cost of having more files is far less than the cost of a 1500-line module whose surface area cannot be summarised. The CSS course's "ownership boundary" applies here too: a module owns one coherent thing and exposes a minimal surface.

Exercises & Solutions

Practice — Modules
  1. Create math.mjs exactly as shown. From a separate index.mjs, import only add and use it. Confirm subtract and multiply are not defined in index.mjs.
  2. Convert the default export multiply to a named export. Update index.mjs to import it correctly. Notice the default-vs-named distinction is purely about syntax — the function does the same thing.
  3. Add a third file logger.mjs that exports a function log(msg) printing with a timestamp. Use it from index.mjs. Confirm the import order is visible — Node loads dependencies depth-first before running the importing file.
Solution & What to Check
  1. import { add } from './math.mjs'; imports only add; the other names are not in scope in index.mjs. Referring to subtract there would error.
  2. Remove default from the export (export function multiply), update the import to import { multiply } from './math.mjs'; — braces around multiply because it is now a named export. The function behaves identically.
  3. If logger.mjs contains console.log('logger module loaded') at top level, you see that line appear before any code in index.mjs runs — because Node loads dependencies first. This is why top-level code in a module runs exactly once, when first imported.

Architecture Thinking

Module boundaries are encapsulation boundaries, the same idea the CSS course taught for components. A module hides its internals and exposes a deliberate, minimal interface; everything inside the file is implementation detail, free to be refactored without anyone outside noticing. This is the unit of code reuse and the unit of code change. The discipline of small, focused modules with named exports — modules that own one concept and own nothing else — is what makes a 500-file codebase navigable. The same instinct ("bounded units, composed") that produced the CSS course's ownership boundary is the right instinct here: each module owns its inside, the codebase composes them deliberately.

Debugging Tips

  • "Cannot find module './x.mjs'" — wrong path, wrong extension, or wrong working directory. The error message shows the path Node looked for; check it against the actual file.
  • "x is not exported" — the name is wrong, or you imported a default as a named import (or vice versa).
  • Circular-import surprises. If one of two mutually-importing modules sees undefined for the other's exports, the cycle is the cause; extract the shared piece into a third module.
  • Use node --trace-warnings to see the full stack trace for module-related warnings.

Performance Considerations

Performance

Every import at the top of a file is resolved at startup — Node loads the file, runs its top-level code, and caches the result. Importing a heavy module you only use sometimes makes startup slower than necessary. Dynamic import() defers the load to first use, which is the right tool for rarely-used code paths in a long-running service or any tool whose startup time matters. For everyday application code, prefer static imports — they are clearer and Node optimises them well. Module 3.5 covers package bundling and tree-shaking strategies.

Build This — Mini-Project
Math Utilities With Three Import Styles

A two-file project that uses named exports, a default export, and a namespace import in one place — so you can see the syntax of all three side by side, in working code.

css
package.json{ "name": "esm-demo", "version": "1.0.0", "type": "module" }
code
math.mjsexport function add(a, b) { return a + b; }
export function subtract(a, b) { return a - b; }
export default function multiply(a, b) { return a * b; }
css
index.mjsimport multiply, { add, subtract } from './math.mjs';
import * as math from './math.mjs';

console.log('add(2,3):', add(2, 3));
console.log('subtract(10,4):', subtract(10, 4));
console.log('multiply(6,7):', multiply(6, 7));
console.log('math.add:', math.add(1, 1));
Expected result:
code

subtract(10,4): 6
multiply(6,7): 42
math.add: 2
Four lines, three import styles working together. This is the full module vocabulary the rest of the course speaks.

Bonus: The Same Project in All Four Module Forms

The rest of this course uses ESM (.mjs for JS, .mts for TS) because that is the 2026 default for new Node code. But you will join codebases — some of them very large — that still use CommonJS (.cjs, .cts), the older module system Node was born with. They differ at the syntax layer, not the engineering layer: same project, same exports, same imports — just a different vocabulary for how files reference each other.

Below is the same two-file math project rendered four ways. The expected output is identical for all four. Read this once as a Rosetta stone; you will recognise both forms in the wild forever after.

code
math.cjs — CommonJS JavaScriptfunction add(a, b) { return a + b; }
function subtract(a, b) { return a - b; }
function multiply(a, b) { return a * b; }
module.exports = { add, subtract, multiply, default: multiply };
js
index.cjs — the callerconst math = require('./math.cjs');
const { add, subtract } = math;
const multiply = math.default;      // mirrors ESM `import multiply from ...`

console.log('add(2,3):', add(2, 3));
console.log('subtract(10,4):', subtract(10, 4));
console.log('multiply(6,7):', multiply(6, 7));
console.log('math.add:', math.add(1, 1));

The TypeScript variants — .mts for ESM, .cts for CommonJS — annotate the same functions with parameter and return types. Node 24+ runs both directly via node --experimental-strip-types, no separate build step:

code
math.mts — ESM TypeScriptexport function add(a: number, b: number): number { return a + b; }
export function subtract(a: number, b: number): number { return a - b; }
export default function multiply(a: number, b: number): number { return a * b; }
code
math.cts — CommonJS TypeScript (strip-friendly form)function add(a: number, b: number): number { return a + b; }
function subtract(a: number, b: number): number { return a - b; }
function multiply(a: number, b: number): number { return a * b; }
module.exports = { add, subtract, multiply, default: multiply };
A subtle note on CommonJS + TypeScript

The TS-native CJS forms — export = {...} on the producer side and import x = require(...) on the consumer side — are what tsc understands and what production codebases use. They are not, however, supported by Node's --experimental-strip-types mode because they require syntactic rewriting, not just type removal. To run a .cts file under Node's stripper without a build step, use the plainer form shown above (module.exports = ... + const x = require(...) with a manual type). In a tsc-built project, prefer export = / import =.

All four files print the same four lines: add(2,3): 5, subtract(10,4): 6, multiply(6,7): 42, math.add: 2. The engineering is identical; only the module-system syntax differs. Once you can read both, you can read every Node codebase you will ever join.

Module 1 · Chapter 1.5

Errors, Throwing & Result Patterns

Things go wrong: an input is malformed, a file is missing, the database rejected a query. JavaScript has two complementary ways to handle failure — throwing an error and catching it, or returning a result that says success-or-failure. Knowing when to use each is one of the most important judgement calls in backend code.

Lesson 1.5Handling What Goes Wrong

Concept Overview

A throw sends an error up the call stack until a try { ... } catch (err) { ... } catches it; uncaught errors crash the program (or, in async code, become rejected promises that Node reports loudly). The standard Error class carries a message and a stack; custom error classes (class ValidationError extends Error) let you carry your own fields and let callers err instanceof X to handle different kinds differently. The Result pattern is the alternative: instead of throwing, return an object like { ok: true, value } or { ok: false, error }. The caller checks result.ok rather than wrapping the call in try/catch. Real Node code uses both — throwing for genuinely exceptional failures, Result for predictable validation outcomes.

Plain English Explanation

Throwing is the panic button: code says "I cannot continue, something is wrong" by calling throw new Error('reason'), and JavaScript unwinds the running function — and the function that called it, and the function that called that — until it finds a try/catch ready to handle it. If nothing catches it, the program crashes. The try { ... } catch (err) { ... } block is how you say "for this section of code, I am prepared to handle anything that goes wrong."

Throwing is the right shape when the failure is genuinely exceptional — a database connection lost, a configuration missing, a developer mistake. But many "failures" are not exceptional — they are expected, predictable, and routine. "The user entered an invalid email" is not exceptional; it happens every day. Wrapping every input validation in try/catch is overkill and obscures intent.

For those routine cases, the Result pattern reads more cleanly. A function returns { ok: true, value: 30 } on success and { ok: false, error: 'not a number' } on failure, and the caller writes if (result.ok) { use(result.value) } else { handle(result.error) }. No exceptions thrown, no try/catch needed. The flow of failure is right there in the function signature, not hidden behind a throw two functions deep.

Finally, custom error classes let you tag thrown errors with their kind, so a single catch can route them: "if it's a ValidationError return 400; if it's a NotFoundError return 404; otherwise log and return 500." Without custom classes, all thrown errors look the same.

Why It Matters

Error handling is where Node code reveals its quality. Code that consistently distinguishes "exceptional → throw" from "expected → Result" is easy to read: function signatures advertise their expected failures, and try/catch blocks are reserved for genuine surprises. Code that throws on every kind of failure becomes a forest of try/catches with no signal about what is actually exceptional. Module 4 (HTTP) and Module 5 (databases) both depend on this discipline — they live or die on whether validation failures are surfaced cleanly versus thrown indiscriminately.

Real-World Example

A signup handler receives a JSON body with an email and password. Two kinds of failure can happen. The email might be malformed — that is a predictable, routine failure: the handler validates the input and either continues or returns 400 to the user. The database might be down when the handler tries to insert the new user — that is a genuinely exceptional failure: catch it at a top-level error handler, log it, and return 500. Two failures, two shapes: the email validation uses Result-style returns; the database call's failure is thrown and caught by middleware. The mental model — predictable vs exceptional — drives the shape choice at every layer.

Enterprise Example

Enterprise Node services often standardise this with two patterns. First, a small hierarchy of custom errors: ValidationError, NotFoundError, ConflictError, UnauthorizedError, each extending Error. A single error-handling middleware maps each subclass to its HTTP status. Second, a Result-style return for domain operations that can routinely fail in known ways — a payments function returns { ok: true, paymentId } | { ok: false, code: 'insufficient_funds' }, not a thrown error. Callers handle the failure modes explicitly. Together, these conventions make error handling a deliberate architecture decision rather than scattered try/catches.

Step-by-Step Breakdown

How to choose:

  1. Is this failure expected and routine? Validation failures, "not found" results, "user input was bad." Use the Result pattern: return success-or-failure as data.
  2. Is this failure exceptional? Database unreachable, file system error, programming bug, contract violated. Throw a custom error that names the kind.
  3. Catch at the right layer. Generally, low-level functions throw; top-level handlers catch. Catching too low means swallowing errors and losing context; catching too high means crashes.
  4. Re-throw if you cannot handle. A catch that does not know how to recover should rethrow — possibly wrapping with more context: throw new Error('failed to load user', { cause: err }).
  5. Never swallow errors silently. An empty catch { } hides bugs and is one of the most common causes of mysterious behaviour.
  6. For async code: try/catch works with await; Module 2.3 covers async error handling fully.

Code Example

code
errors.mjs — three patterns side by side// 1. throw an error, catch it, recover
function divide(a, b) {
  if (b === 0) throw new Error('divide by zero');
  return a / b;
}

try {
  console.log('10/2 =', divide(10, 2));
  console.log('10/0 =', divide(10, 0));
} catch (err) {
  console.log('caught:', err.message);
}

// 2. Result pattern — return success or failure instead of throwing
function parseAge(input) {
  const n = Number(input);
  if (Number.isNaN(n)) return { ok: false, error: 'not a number' };
  if (n < 0)            return { ok: false, error: 'negative age' };
  return { ok: true, value: n };
}

const r1 = parseAge('30');
const r2 = parseAge('abc');
console.log('r1:', r1);
console.log('r2:', r2);

// 3. custom error subclass — carry your own kind
class ValidationError extends Error {
  constructor(field, message) {
    super(message);
    this.name = 'ValidationError';
    this.field = field;
  }
}

try {
  throw new ValidationError('email', 'must contain @');
} catch (err) {
  if (err instanceof ValidationError) {
    console.log('validation:', err.field, '-', err.message);
  }
}

JS & TS Together

The Result pattern is especially good in TypeScript, because the union type makes the failure case explicit to the compiler:

css
errors.tstype Result<T> = { ok: true; value: T } | { ok: false; error: string };

function parseAge(input: string): Result<number> {
  // ... same body
}

With this signature, TypeScript will refuse to use result.value until you have checked result.ok first — the failure case is impossible to forget.

Common Mistakes

Common Mistake

Empty catch blocks. catch (err) { } swallows every error silently. Even if you cannot handle it, at least log it. Silent catches are the most common cause of "the system did nothing and I cannot tell why."

Common Mistake

Throwing plain strings. throw 'something broke' produces an error without a stack trace and no message property. Always throw new Error('...'); better, throw a custom subclass that names the kind.

Common Mistake

Catching too low. Wrapping every internal call in try/catch and converting errors to null or defaults loses context. Catch where you can actually do something useful — usually at the route handler or top-level worker.

Principal Engineer Notes

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

A principal engineer treats the choice between throwing and Result as a design decision per function, made deliberately. Their default: predictable domain failures return a Result (the function signature names what can go wrong); genuinely exceptional or programmer-error situations throw with a custom error class. The result is code where you can read a function's signature and know what failures it expects, separate from what surprises could happen.

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

They preserve error context with the cause option, added in modern JavaScript: throw new Error('failed to load user', { cause: originalError }). This chains errors so the top-level handler sees both the high-level intent and the low-level root cause — every link in the chain visible in the stack trace. Lost context is lost time at 3 AM debugging production.

Exercises & Solutions

Practice — Errors & Results
  1. Write a parseEmail(input) function using the Result pattern: return { ok: true, value } if the input is a string containing @, otherwise { ok: false, error: 'invalid email' }.
  2. Create a NotFoundError custom class and throw it from a mock findUser(id) function when the id is not 1. Catch it and check err instanceof NotFoundError.
  3. Write a chained throw: a high-level function catches an inner error, wraps it with { cause: err }, and rethrows. Log the outer error's cause property and confirm the chain is preserved.
Solution & What to Check
  1. function parseEmail(input) { if (typeof input !== 'string' || !input.includes('@')) return { ok: false, error: 'invalid email' }; return { ok: true, value: input }; }. The signature alone tells the caller what failures to expect.
  2. class NotFoundError extends Error { constructor(id) { super(`not found: ${id}`); this.name = 'NotFoundError'; this.id = id; } } — and err instanceof NotFoundError reliably distinguishes it from any other thrown error.
  3. catch (err) { throw new Error('loading user failed', { cause: err }); }. The outer error's cause property holds the original, and Node prints both in the stack trace automatically since modern versions.

Architecture Thinking

The choice between throwing and returning a Result is an instance of a general principle: make the failure mode of every function explicit. Throws are an out-of-band channel — they can come from anywhere — so use them for genuinely exceptional surprises. Result returns are in-band — the signature names the failure case — so use them for failures you expect. A codebase that follows this consistently is one where you can read any function's signature and know what can go wrong without reading its body. That property is what makes a system approachable to a new engineer and debuggable in production at 3 AM. It is the same architectural instinct as making types explicit: contracts visible, surprises minimised.

Debugging Tips

  • Always log the stack, not just the message. console.error(err) prints the stack; console.error(err.message) alone loses where it came from.
  • Use the cause property for wrapped errors so you keep both the outer intent and the inner cause in the trace.
  • If a function "did nothing," grep for empty catch blocks first — a swallowed error is the most common cause.
  • Unhandled promise rejections print loudly in modern Node; if you see one in logs, find the missing await or .catch(). Module 2.3 covers async errors fully.

Performance Considerations

Performance

Throwing an error is expensive: V8 has to capture the stack trace, which involves walking the call stack and resolving source positions. For a single error per request this is invisible; for code that throws inside tight loops it becomes measurable. This is one of the reasons the Result pattern is preferred for routine, expected failures — a returned object is essentially free, while a thrown error costs microseconds of stack capture each time. Reserve throws for genuine exceptions and the performance question never arises.

Build This — Mini-Project
Three Error Patterns in One Script

One script demonstrating throwing, the Result pattern, and a custom error subclass — so you have all three shapes side by side and have run each one once.

code
errors.mjs// 1. throw an error, catch it, recover
function divide(a, b) {
  if (b === 0) throw new Error('divide by zero');
  return a / b;
}
try {
  console.log('10/2 =', divide(10, 2));
  console.log('10/0 =', divide(10, 0));
} catch (err) { console.log('caught:', err.message); }

// 2. Result pattern — return success or failure
function parseAge(input) {
  const n = Number(input);
  if (Number.isNaN(n)) return { ok: false, error: 'not a number' };
  if (n < 0)            return { ok: false, error: 'negative age' };
  return { ok: true, value: n };
}
console.log('r1:', parseAge('30'));
console.log('r2:', parseAge('abc'));

// 3. custom error subclass — carry your own kind
class ValidationError extends Error {
  constructor(field, message) {
    super(message);
    this.name = 'ValidationError';
    this.field = field;
  }
}
try { throw new ValidationError('email', 'must contain @'); }
catch (err) {
  if (err instanceof ValidationError) {
    console.log('validation:', err.field, '-', err.message);
  }
}
Expected result:
code

caught: divide by zero

r2: { ok: false, error: 'not a number' }
validation: email - must contain @
Three patterns, one screen. From now on, every Node function you write chooses between these three shapes — and the choice is a design decision, not a coincidence.

How Node Runs

Before any HTTP server or database client makes sense, you must understand the machine that runs your code. This module opens Node's runtime: the process, the event loop, the async model, and the concurrency patterns that make Node handle thousands of connections without ever using multiple threads for its application code. Get this mental model right and every later module follows; get it wrong and async code feels like magic.

Module 2 · Chapter 2.1

The Runtime: V8, libuv & the Process

"Node" is shorthand for a specific stack of programs running together: Google's V8 JavaScript engine, the C library libuv handling input/output and timers, and a wrapper that binds them with a JavaScript API. Knowing what each piece does is what makes Node's behaviour predictable.

Lesson 2.1What Node Actually Is

Concept Overview

Node.js is a runtime built from three main pieces. V8 — Google's JavaScript engine, the same one inside Chrome — is the part that actually executes your JavaScript code. libuv is a C library that gives Node its asynchronous I/O — reading files, network sockets, timers, child processes. The Node bindings are the JavaScript surface that lets you call all of it from your code: console, process, fs, http, and the rest. When you run node script.mjs, an instance of this stack starts up, exposes a global process object representing the running instance, evaluates your script, and exits — either when the script finishes and there is nothing pending, or when something calls process.exit().

Plain English Explanation

Think of Node like a small car. V8 is the engine — it burns the fuel (your JavaScript) and turns it into motion. libuv is the rest of the chassis — the tyres, the steering, the gauges — the parts that let the engine actually interact with the world: read files, open network connections, count seconds. The Node API is the dashboard — the buttons and labelled controls (fs.readFile, http.createServer, setTimeout) you press from JavaScript to make any of it happen.

The process object is the most important piece of the dashboard. It is your running instance of Node, made available to your code as a global. Through it you read your script's arguments (the words after node on the command line, in process.argv), your environment variables (process.env.NODE_ENV), your current working directory (process.cwd()), and you control how the program ends (process.exit(0) for success, any non-zero for failure — that "exit code" is how every Unix tool reports success or failure). The most important property might be process.version and process.platform — they tell you exactly which Node and which operating system your code is running on.

Two things to understand about how Node ends. First, a script ends naturally when there is nothing left to do — no pending timers, no open network connections, no unfinished file reads. Second, an exit code of 0 means success; anything else means failure. Every shell, CI system, and orchestration tool reads that number to decide whether your script worked.

Why It Matters

A surprising amount of Node debugging comes down to "I do not understand what Node is doing right now." Knowing that V8 runs your JavaScript single- threaded but libuv can do I/O on a thread pool — and that the process object exposes everything about the running instance — is the difference between "Node behaved strangely" and "of course, that is how the runtime works." Every Module 4 HTTP server, every Module 5 database client, every Module 7 worker — they all sit on this stack. Understanding the stack makes the higher layers predictable.

Real-World Example

An engineer's CI pipeline fails mysteriously: their tests pass locally but the deployment script reports failure. Tracing through, the engineer finds their test runner script does not call process.exit() — it just falls off the end. Locally that worked because there was nothing pending and Node exited cleanly with code 0. In CI, the tests opened a database connection that was never closed, so Node kept running, the deployment script timed out, and the CI system marked it as failure. The fix: close the connection (or call process.exit(0) explicitly once tests pass). The bug was not in the code's logic; it was in how the script ended. Knowing the runtime's exit rules turns a mysterious CI failure into a five-minute fix.

Enterprise Example

In production every long-running Node service uses process extensively. Configuration comes from process.env (the twelve-factor app convention says configuration lives in environment variables, never in code). Graceful shutdown is wired up via process.on('SIGTERM', ...) — when the orchestrator (Kubernetes, a load balancer) tells the service to stop, the service finishes its in-flight requests, closes its database pool, and only then exits. Unhandled errors are logged via process.on('uncaughtException', ...) and process.on('unhandledRejection', ...) so a crash leaves forensic evidence. Module 8 builds all of these out properly. The point for now: process is not a beginner detail — it is the runtime surface every serious Node program manipulates.

Step-by-Step Breakdown

What happens when you run node script.mjs:

  1. The operating system launches the node binary, passing script.mjs as an argument.
  2. Node initialises V8 (the JavaScript engine), libuv (the I/O layer), and its standard library bindings.
  3. Node creates the global process object, populates it with argv, env, platform, version, and other facts about this run.
  4. Node resolves and loads script.mjs — running its top-level code through V8. Any imports in it are loaded first, depth-first.
  5. If the script schedules anything async (a timer, a promise, an I/O call), Node keeps the process alive until all of it completes.
  6. When there is nothing left to do, Node exits with code 0. If your code called process.exit(N) earlier, it exits with N immediately, skipping any pending work.

Code Example

code
process.mjs — inspecting the running Node instance// Node hands every script a `process` object: the running instance.
console.log('Node version:', process.version);
console.log('Platform:', process.platform);
console.log('PID:', process.pid);
console.log('Args:', process.argv.slice(2));   // skip [node, script-path]
console.log('Env GREETING:', process.env.GREETING ?? '(unset)');

// An exit code: 0 means success; non-zero is the universal "failed" signal.
if (process.argv.includes('--fail')) {
  console.error('Failing on purpose.');
  process.exit(1);
}
console.log('OK');
code
terminal — running the script several ways$ node process.mjs
Node version: v22.11.0
Platform: darwin
PID: 90073
Args: []
Env GREETING: (unset)
OK

$ GREETING=hi node process.mjs one two
# Args: [ 'one', 'two' ]    Env GREETING: hi

$ node process.mjs --fail; echo "exit was $?"
# Failing on purpose.
# exit was 1

Three small but important things to notice. process.argv is an array; positions 0 and 1 are Node and your script, so the first real argument is at index 2 — hence the .slice(2). Environment variables come pre-loaded from the shell — GREETING=hi node ... sets GREETING for just this command. And $? in the shell holds the previous command's exit code — that is how every shell script reads success/failure.

JS & TS Together

In TypeScript, process is fully typed — process.argv is string[], process.env is NodeJS.ProcessEnv (effectively Record<string, string | undefined>). That last detail matters: every environment variable is potentially undefined, so TypeScript will force you to handle the missing case — exactly what you want for configuration loading. We will validate env vars properly in Module 8.3.

Common Mistakes

Common Mistake

Reading process.argv[0] as the first argument. It is the path to Node. argv[1] is your script. Real arguments start at argv[2].

Common Mistake

Trusting process.env.X exists. Environment variables are strings or undefined. Always default explicitly: process.env.PORT ?? '3000' — and validate them at startup.

Common Mistake

Calling process.exit() in the middle of async work. It exits immediately, abandoning any pending I/O — half-written files, half-sent HTTP responses. For shutdown, signal the work to wind down and let Node exit naturally; reserve process.exit() for "fatal and now."

Principal Engineer Notes

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

A principal engineer treats the process object as the boundary between their code and the operating system. Configuration enters through process.env; success or failure leaves through the exit code; OS lifecycle signals (SIGTERM, SIGINT) come in via process.on(...). Code that crosses that boundary deliberately — instead of pretending the OS does not exist — is code that survives in production.

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

They validate environment variables at startup, not at first use. A service that boots, runs for two hours, then crashes because a missing env var was finally accessed is a worse failure than one that refuses to boot in the first place. Read every required variable, validate it, fail loudly if it is missing — before any HTTP listener starts.

Exercises & Solutions

Practice — The Process
  1. Write a script that prints process.cwd(), process.platform, and the first five characters of process.version.
  2. Make a script that exits with code 2 if no command-line argument is given, and code 0 otherwise. Run it both ways and check the exit code with echo $? on macOS/Linux or echo %ERRORLEVEL% on Windows.
  3. Read an environment variable PORT with a fallback to 3000. Print the chosen value. Run the script with PORT=8080 node script.mjs and again with no env set.
Solution & What to Check
  1. console.log(process.cwd(), process.platform, process.version.slice(0, 5)); prints your folder, your OS code (darwin, linux, win32), and something like v22.1.
  2. if (process.argv.length < 3) process.exit(2); followed by a normal end. Running with no arg gives exit code 2; with one argument it is 0. The shell's $? / %ERRORLEVEL% reads the last command's exit code.
  3. const port = Number(process.env.PORT ?? 3000);. With PORT=8080 set, port is 8080; without it, 3000. Notice the Number() — env vars are always strings, even when they look like numbers.

Architecture Thinking

The process object is your program's system interface — the deliberate, narrow line where your application talks to the operating system that is hosting it. Treating that interface as a real architectural boundary, rather than a grab-bag of globals to use anywhere, is what makes a service portable. Configuration through env lets the same binary run in development, staging, and production. Exit codes let any orchestrator know what happened. Signal handlers let any operations team restart your service gracefully. None of this is Node-specific; it is the Unix tradition of small, well-behaved programs, and Node embraces it. Build with that boundary in mind from the start.

Debugging Tips

  • "My script exits before async work finishes" — you probably called process.exit() too early. Remove the explicit exit and let Node leave naturally when work is done.
  • "My script hangs forever" — the opposite: something (a timer, an open server, a database client) is keeping Node alive. Use process._getActiveHandles() in a REPL to inspect what is still active.
  • Read exit codes from CI logs. Most failures show a non-zero exit code in the orchestrator's output; that number is searchable and often documented per tool.

Performance Considerations

Performance

Node's startup time is a real performance concern for CLI tools and serverless functions, where a fresh runtime starts per invocation. The cost has two main contributors: V8 initialisation (essentially fixed) and loading modules (proportional to the size of your import graph). Cold starts on a fast machine are tens of milliseconds; on a constrained serverless platform they can be hundreds. Keep top-level imports lean, consider dynamic imports for rare code paths, and Module 9 covers startup-optimisation techniques in depth.

Build This — Mini-Project
Inspecting the Running Process

Write a single script that prints every important fact about its own running instance — version, platform, PID, command-line arguments, environment variables — and can fail on demand via an exit code. This is the smallest tour of the runtime surface and the foundation for every operations-aware script.

  console.error('Failing on purpose.');
  process.exit(1);

Run it three ways:

Three lines of inspection plus your message — the runtime surface every operations-aware Node program sits on top of.
Module 2 · Chapter 2.2

The Event Loop, Step by Step

Node's superpower — handling thousands of concurrent connections on a single thread — comes from one mechanism: the event loop. This is the mental model that makes async code stop being mysterious. Read this chapter carefully; every later Node concept rests on it.

Lesson 2.2How Node Schedules Work

Concept Overview

Node runs JavaScript on one thread — V8's main thread. That thread has a call stack (the in-progress function calls) and a queue of pending work. The event loop is the simple algorithm Node runs over and over: while the call stack is non-empty, keep executing; when it empties, take the next pending task from the queue and start it. There are several queues in priority order: microtasks (promise callbacks, queueMicrotask) run as soon as the current task finishes; I/O callbacks (filesystem, network) run when libuv reports their completion; timers (setTimeout) run when their time has elapsed; setImmediate runs once per loop iteration. Everything async in Node is "schedule a task, return immediately, the event loop will run it later."

Plain English Explanation

Picture a single chef in a kitchen. Orders come in on a counter. The chef picks one up, cooks it start-to-finish (the synchronous code), and only then picks up the next order. This is JavaScript's call stack — one function running at a time, top to bottom, never interrupted.

Now picture the chef can hand off the slow bits — boiling water, waiting for the oven — to assistants. When the chef wants water boiled, they put a note on the assistant's board ("tell me when this is done") and immediately go back to other orders. When the water boils, the assistant puts a completion note in a tray on the chef's counter. The chef finishes the current order, glances at the tray, picks up the note, and finishes the waiting order. The chef is V8's main thread; the assistants are libuv's thread pool (for I/O) and the operating system (for network and timers); the tray of completion notes is the event queue. The chef cycling through "finish current, check tray, pick up next" is the event loop.

The crucial consequence: the chef never multitasks. If one order takes five minutes of pure chef work — slicing onions by hand, no assistant possible — every other order waits. That is blocking the event loop, and it is the single most important thing to understand about Node. Heavy synchronous work blocks everything: every other HTTP request, every other timer, every other operation. Async work (anything that hands off to an assistant) does not block; the chef returns immediately. This is why Node can handle thousands of connections: each connection's CPU work is small and the slow parts (database queries, network calls) are handed off.

There are also priority levels for the tray. Notes from promises (the microtask queue) are read before notes from timers; that is why a Promise.resolve().then(...) scheduled before a setTimeout(..., 0) always runs first. You will write code that depends on this ordering; the next chapter is where knowing it pays off.

Why It Matters

Almost every Node performance issue, deadlock, or "why did my code run in that order" question is answered by the event loop. A server that "stops responding" under load is usually a server with a CPU-heavy synchronous function blocking the loop. A unit test that fails "randomly" is usually a test that assumed the wrong scheduling order. Knowing the loop precisely is what lets you predict — not guess — when each piece of your code will run.

Real-World Example

A Node service that handles thousands of requests per second suddenly starts dropping requests under load. The engineer profiles and finds one function doing JSON parsing on a 50MB request body — synchronously, on the main thread. While that parse runs (300ms of pure CPU), every other incoming request waits in the queue, builds up, and times out. The fix is not to make the parse faster; it is to not block the event loop: either reject oversized bodies upfront, or offload heavy parsing to a worker thread (Module 9). The deeper lesson is the chapter's: in Node, long synchronous functions are not just slow for the user running them — they freeze every other user too. The event loop is shared.

Enterprise Example

Enterprise Node services monitor event loop lag — the delay between when a timer should fire and when it actually does. If a synchronous task is hogging the thread, timers fire late and the lag metric rises. Tools like Node's built-in perf_hooks.monitorEventLoopDelay() and observability platforms (Datadog, New Relic) track this number continuously, alerting when it crosses a threshold (commonly 100ms). High event-loop lag means users are waiting; consistently low lag means the architecture is respecting the single-threaded model. The discipline of "no blocking functions on the main thread" is built into review processes and CI checks at every serious Node team.

Step-by-Step Breakdown

The order Node runs things, simplified:

  1. Run the synchronous code at the top of your script to completion. The call stack must be empty before the loop checks any queue.
  2. Drain the microtask queue. Every pending promise callback (.then, await continuations) and every queueMicrotask() runs, in order, until the queue is empty.
  3. Pull one task from the next ready phase of the loop: a timer that has elapsed, an I/O callback that completed, a setImmediate, etc.
  4. Run that task to completion. If it schedules more microtasks, drain them before picking the next task.
  5. Repeat. The loop continues until no scheduled tasks remain — then Node exits.
  6. The hard rule: only one thing runs at a time. If a step takes 500ms of pure CPU, nothing else happens for 500ms.

Code Example

code
loop.mjs — predicting Node's scheduling order// Demonstrates how Node orders synchronous code, microtasks, and timers.
console.log('1: sync start');

setTimeout(() => console.log('5: setTimeout (timer)'), 0);

Promise.resolve().then(() => console.log('3: promise microtask'));

queueMicrotask(() => console.log('4: queueMicrotask'));

console.log('2: sync end');
// Order: all sync first, then microtasks, then timers.

Output (deterministically):

code
terminal1: sync start
2: sync end
3: promise microtask
4: queueMicrotask
5: setTimeout (timer)

Read that output as the event loop's algorithm in action. Lines 1 and 2 ran because Node always runs all synchronous code first. Lines 3 and 4 are the microtask queue draining — even though the timer was scheduled first, microtasks are higher priority. Line 5 is the timer firing on the next iteration. The order is not arbitrary; it follows the rules.

JS & TS Together

TypeScript adds no special handling here — the event loop is the runtime, not the type system. But TypeScript will catch one related class of bug: forgetting to await a promise. If a function returns Promise<User> and you write const user = getUser(id), TypeScript will warn that user is a Promise, not a User. JS gives you no such warning and the bug is harder to spot. Chapter 2.3 covers async patterns in depth.

Common Mistakes

Common Mistake

Blocking the event loop with synchronous CPU work. Heavy JSON parsing, regex on huge strings, image processing, password hashing on the main thread — all freeze the entire server. Use streams (Module 3.1), workers (Module 9), or smaller chunks.

Common Mistake

Assuming setTimeout(fn, 0) means "immediately." It means "after at least 0ms, on the next loop iteration." Microtasks run first. For "as soon as possible after now," use queueMicrotask for the very next opportunity, or accept the small delay.

Common Mistake

Confusing setImmediate with setTimeout(fn, 0). They run in different loop phases and the order between them depends on context. Module 9 explains the difference; for now, prefer queueMicrotask when you need deferred-but-soon, and timers for delays you mean.

Principal Engineer Notes

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

A principal engineer keeps the main thread "boring" — only fast, async- flavoured work. CPU-heavy operations get offloaded (to workers, to other services, to streaming) so the event loop stays responsive under load. They monitor event-loop lag in production the way a CSS engineer monitors layout cost: as a first-class operational signal, not an afterthought.

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

They know precisely when each kind of work fires — microtask before timer before setImmediate — because that ordering shows up in tests, in race conditions, and in the "why did my code run in that order" question that gets asked at code review. The loop is not magic; it has rules, and the rules are predictable.

Exercises & Solutions

Practice — The Event Loop
  1. Predict the output, then run, the four-line script: console.log('a'); setTimeout(() => console.log('b'), 0); Promise.resolve().then(() => console.log('c')); console.log('d');.
  2. Write a script that blocks the event loop for 2 seconds with a busy while loop. Schedule a setTimeout for 100ms before the block. Watch the timer fire 2 seconds late.
  3. Schedule a microtask from inside another microtask. Confirm both run before any pending timer — microtasks are drained completely each time.
Solution & What to Check
  1. Order: a, d, c, b. Sync first (a, d), then the microtask (c), then the timer (b). Microtasks beat timers even when the timer was scheduled first.
  2. The setTimeout fires after the busy loop ends — 2+ seconds late. The event loop could not even check the timer queue while the synchronous loop was running. This is what blocking looks like.
  3. Adding a queueMicrotask from inside a microtask appends to the same queue, and the loop drains the queue completely before moving on. Timers wait until the whole microtask queue is empty.

Architecture Thinking

The event loop is a specific instance of a much larger architectural choice: cooperative concurrency. Many languages give each connection its own OS thread; Node gives them all one thread and rotates cooperatively as each piece of work voluntarily yields by awaiting I/O. The win is enormous when most of the time is spent waiting for the network or disk — one machine handles tens of thousands of simultaneous connections, because none of them actually use CPU most of the time. The cost is that a CPU-bound task on the main thread freezes everyone. This is the trade Node makes, and the patterns of the rest of the course are all about staying on the winning side of it: keep work async, offload what is genuinely CPU-bound, never make the loop wait.

Debugging Tips

  • Suspect blocking when responsiveness dies under load. If your server's p99 latency spikes while p50 stays low, something is occasionally blocking. Profile with --cpu-prof.
  • Measure loop lag. const h = perf_hooks.monitorEventLoopDelay(); h.enable(); starts measuring; h.mean and h.p99 show delay in nanoseconds.
  • Read scheduling-order bugs as event-loop puzzles. When "this happened in the wrong order," draw the loop's queues and step through. The bug is almost always microtask-versus-timer-versus- immediate ordering.

Performance Considerations

Performance

The performance discipline of Node is "everything async, never block the loop." File I/O uses async APIs (fs.promises.readFile, not fs.readFileSync). Database calls return promises and are awaited. CPU-heavy work runs in worker threads or child processes. The event loop should be visiting the next queued task quickly and constantly; anything that holds it for more than a few milliseconds is a candidate for offloading. Module 9 builds the full performance picture; this chapter is the foundation.

Build This — Mini-Project
Predicting Scheduling Order

Write the script that prints lines 1 through 5 in the exact order the event loop runs them. Predict the order before you run it, then verify. Once this output is intuitive, you have internalised Node's scheduling model.

code
loop.mjsconsole.log('1: sync start');
setTimeout(() => console.log('5: setTimeout (timer)'), 0);
Promise.resolve().then(() => console.log('3: promise microtask'));
queueMicrotask(() => console.log('4: queueMicrotask'));
console.log('2: sync end');
Expected result:
code
1: sync start
2: sync end
3: promise microtask
4: queueMicrotask
5: setTimeout (timer)
Sync code first; microtasks drain next; timers fire last. Run the script five times — the order is deterministic, every time. That determinism is the gift of the event loop.
Module 2 · Chapter 2.3

Callbacks, Promises & async/await

Three syntaxes for the same idea — "do this when the async work finishes" — in order of historical age. Callbacks are the original Node convention. Promises are the modern primitive. async/await is the readable form built on promises. Every Node file you read or write uses at least one; understand all three.

Lesson 2.3Three Ways to Wait

Concept Overview

A callback is a function you pass to another function so it can call you back when its async work finishes; Node's classic convention is (err, result) => as the first two arguments of every callback. A Promise is an object representing a future value: it is either pending, then fulfilled with a value, or rejected with an error. You read it with .then(onValue, onError) or chain with .catch(). async/await is syntactic sugar for promises: an async function always returns a promise, and inside it await promise pauses the function until the promise settles, producing the value or throwing the error. Modern Node code uses async/await almost everywhere; promises underneath are still what is doing the work; callbacks remain in older APIs and legacy code.

Plain English Explanation

Imagine you order a coffee. There are three ways the barista can tell you it is ready.

Callback: you hand the barista a small slip of paper that says "call me when it is done." When the coffee is ready, the barista picks up your paper and calls. In Node, that paper is a function: readFile('path', (err, data) => { ... }) — when the file read is done, Node calls your function with either an error or the data. The "err first" convention exists because errors in Node are common and explicit; if there is an error you handle it, otherwise the data is the second argument.

Promise: the barista hands you a ticket — a small object that represents "your future coffee." You attach instructions to the ticket: "when it is ready, do this; if it spills, do that." readFile('path').then(data => {...}).catch(err => {...}). The ticket itself is the promise; you do not block waiting for the coffee, you describe what to do when it shows up.

async/await: the readable form. You still get a ticket from the barista, but instead of attaching instructions, you write const coffee = await ticket — and JavaScript pauses your function (without blocking the whole thread!) until the ticket resolves. From the inside, it reads like sequential code; from the outside, it is still asynchronous. try { const data = await readFile('path') } catch (err) { ... }. This is what 95% of modern Node code looks like.

Two important facts about async/await. First, an async function always returns a promise — even if its body looks like it returns a plain value, the runtime wraps it. Second, await only works inside an async function or at the top level of an ES module (top-level await is what made Chapter 1.4's dynamic import example legal). Otherwise you must wrap your code in async function main() { ... } and call main().

Why It Matters

Every Node file longer than ten lines deals with async work. The shape you choose determines whether the code reads cleanly or descends into "callback hell" — the deeply-nested mess that made older Node infamous. async/await is the modern default because it makes async code read like sync code: one line per step, errors caught with try/catch, no deep nesting. But understanding the promise underneath — and recognising callbacks in legacy code — is essential, because the moment you need to combine async operations, return early, or work with libraries, you are back at the promise level.

Real-World Example

An HTTP handler needs to load a user from the database, then their organisation, then their billing record. In callback style this is three levels of nesting, with error handling repeated in each level. In promise chain it is db.getUser(id).then(u => db.getOrg(u.orgId)).then(o => db.getBilling(o.id)), with a single .catch() at the end — better, but the variables are awkward to share between .thens. In async/await it is three plain lines: const user = await db.getUser(id); const org = await db.getOrg(user.orgId); const billing = await db.getBilling(org.id);. Errors at any step bubble to a single try/catch; the code reads top-to-bottom like sync code; refactoring is trivial. This is why modern Node lives in async/await.

Enterprise Example

At scale, the promise model unlocks parallel composition: const [user, org] = await Promise.all([db.getUser(id), db.getOrg(orgId)]) kicks off both calls simultaneously and waits for both — turning a 200ms sequential pair into a 100ms parallel pair. Promise.allSettled waits for all to finish even if some fail. Promise.race resolves with the first to finish — useful for timeouts. Promise.any resolves with the first success, useful for fallback strategies. These are the building blocks of Module 2.4's concurrency patterns and of any serious backend's performance story. Callbacks have no clean equivalent; async/await reads through them all easily.

Step-by-Step Breakdown

The lifecycle of a promise:

  1. You call an async function. It synchronously returns a promise in the pending state.
  2. The promise's internal work proceeds — probably scheduled on libuv for I/O, the timer queue for delays, or the microtask queue for immediate continuation.
  3. When that work finishes, the promise transitions to fulfilled (with a value) or rejected (with a reason). A promise can settle exactly once; further attempts are ignored.
  4. Any .then(), .catch(), or await waiting on the promise is scheduled as a microtask and runs after the current synchronous code.
  5. An await pauses the surrounding async function — saves its state, releases the call stack to the event loop, and resumes the function when the awaited promise settles.
  6. An unhandled rejection (no .catch(), no try/catch) becomes an unhandledRejection event on the process; in modern Node this terminates the process by default.

Code Example

code
async.mjs — three syntaxes, same operation// 1. Callback style — the older Node convention.
function readFakeCallback(name, cb) {
  setTimeout(() => cb(null, 'data:' + name), 30);
}
readFakeCallback('a', (err, data) => {
  if (err) return console.error(err);
  console.log('callback:', data);
});

// 2. Promise style — the modern primitive.
function readFakePromise(name) {
  return new Promise(resolve => setTimeout(() => resolve('data:' + name), 30));
}
readFakePromise('b').then(data => console.log('promise:', data));

// 3. async/await — the readable form built on promises.
async function main() {
  const data = await readFakePromise('c');
  console.log('await:', data);

  // try/catch works exactly as it does with sync code:
  try {
    await Promise.reject(new Error('boom'));
  } catch (err) {
    console.log('caught:', err.message);
  }
}
await main();

The three blocks do the same job; the third is what you write 95% of the time. Notice try/catch around await — async errors are caught exactly like sync errors, which is the entire reason async/await exists.

JS & TS Together

TypeScript's types for promises are pleasant. An async function that returns a number has type () => Promise<number>; awaiting it yields a number. The compiler refuses to use a promise as if it were its resolved value — the most common async bug in plain JS (forgetting await) is caught at compile time.

code
async.tsasync function readFake(name: string): Promise<string> {
  return new Promise(r => setTimeout(() => r('data:' + name), 30));
}

Common Mistakes

Common Mistake

Forgetting await. const user = getUser(id) without await assigns the promise to user, not the value. Subsequent code operates on the wrong type. TypeScript catches this; in plain JS, it is the most common async bug.

Common Mistake

Awaiting inside .forEach. arr.forEach(async x => { await ... }) fires off all the async work but does not wait for any of it — forEach does not understand promises. Use a for...of loop with await inside, or Promise.all(arr.map(...)).

Common Mistake

Unhandled rejections. A promise that rejects with no .catch() or try/catch crashes modern Node. The fix is always: somewhere up the chain, handle the rejection.

Principal Engineer Notes

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

A principal engineer writes async code in async/await by default, drops to .then() for clean parallel composition (Promise.all, Promise.race), and recognises callback APIs when they meet them but wraps them in promises (util.promisify) so the rest of the codebase stays uniform. Mixing styles in one function is a small mess that compounds over time.

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

They treat async functions as functions that return promises — and propagate them deliberately. If doX() calls doY() and forgets to await, the error from doY() escapes doX's try/catch and becomes unhandled. A discipline of always await every async call, or explicitly chain it, eliminates a whole class of subtle bug.

Exercises & Solutions

Practice — Async Shapes
  1. Convert a callback-style function — say, setTimeout(callback, ms) — into a promise-returning function called delay(ms). Use it with await delay(500).
  2. Write an async function that returns the number 42. Call it and inspect what comes back without await. Confirm it is a Promise.
  3. Write a function that awaits two operations in parallel using Promise.all. Compare its total time to the sequential version. Module 2.4 builds this pattern out in full.
Solution & What to Check
  1. const delay = ms => new Promise(r => setTimeout(r, ms)); — three short lines. await delay(500) pauses for 500ms.
  2. async function answer() { return 42; }. Calling answer() without await yields Promise { 42 } in Node's REPL — proving an async function always returns a promise, even when its body looks synchronous.
  3. Sequential awaits of two 100ms operations take 200ms. Promise.all([op1(), op2()]) takes 100ms — both run in parallel and the await waits for the slowest. For independent operations, always parallel.

Architecture Thinking

Async functions are a continuation mechanism: they record where they paused (the await point) and resume there when the result arrives. Continuations are how many languages handle concurrency without threads; in Node, they sit on the event loop, and the result is the same: tens of thousands of "concurrent" operations sharing one thread, each one only on the thread while it has actual work to do. Once you see await as "yield to the loop until ready," every async pattern in the rest of the course is a variation on that one idea.

Debugging Tips

  • Stack traces lose information across await boundaries in older Node; modern Node preserves them, but named functions help. Prefer async function name() {} over anonymous arrows for the top of async call chains.
  • Use util.promisify to wrap callback APIs into promise APIs so your code stays uniformly async/await.
  • If a promise "never resolves," the work was never actually scheduled — usually because a callback was lost or a resolve() was forgotten in a manual new Promise(...).

Performance Considerations

Performance

Awaiting an already-resolved promise is essentially free — microseconds. The performance trap is unnecessary serialisation: awaiting two independent operations one after the other when they could have run in parallel doubles the latency for no reason. Whenever you see const a = await foo(); const b = await bar(); and foo and bar are independent, change to const [a, b] = await Promise.all([foo(), bar()]);. Module 2.4 makes this discipline explicit.

Build This — Mini-Project
Three Async Syntaxes

One script doing the same operation three ways — callback, promise, async/await — plus a try/catch around an awaited rejection. Read the script, then run it, and you have the full async vocabulary in one place.

css

  setTimeout(() => cb(null, 'data:' + name), 30);
}
readFakeCallback('a', (err, data) => {
  if (err) return console.error(err);
  console.log('callback:', data);
});

  return new Promise(r => setTimeout(() => r('data:' + name), 30));
}
readFakePromise('b').then(d => console.log('promise:', d));

async function main() {
  const data = await readFakePromise('c');
  console.log('await:', data);
  try { await Promise.reject(new Error('boom')); }
  catch (err) { console.log('caught:', err.message); }
}

Expected result:
code
