promise: data:b
await: data:c
caught: boom
The three syntaxes side by side. Use the third by default in your own code; recognise the first two when you read other people's.
Module 2 · Chapter 2.4

Concurrency Patterns — Parallel, Sequential, Limited

Once you can await one async operation, the next question is how to handle many. Three patterns cover almost every case: sequential — one after another; parallel — all at once; limited — N at a time. Choosing the right one is one of the most common performance decisions in Node.

Lesson 2.4Many Things at Once

Concept Overview

Sequential means awaiting one operation, then the next: total time is the sum. Parallel means firing all operations at once and awaiting them together with Promise.all: total time is roughly the slowest. The catch is that "parallel" in JavaScript still runs your code on one thread — parallelism only helps when each operation is mostly waiting on I/O. Limited concurrency means running N operations at a time, neither all-at-once (which can overwhelm a downstream service) nor one-at-a-time (which leaves capacity unused) — controlled with a small worker-pool pattern. Choosing between these three is mostly a question of dependency (does B need A's result?) and capacity (can the downstream handle 1000 requests at once?).

Plain English Explanation

Think about hanging laundry. You have ten shirts to wash, dry, and fold.

Sequential: wash shirt 1, dry shirt 1, fold shirt 1; then start shirt 2. Slow but ordered, and the dryer is empty most of the time. This is what for (const x of items) { await process(x); } gives you: each operation waits for the last. Use it when each step genuinely depends on the previous step's result, or when the downstream service explicitly requires one-at-a-time.

Parallel: throw all ten shirts in ten washing machines at once. Total time is the time of the slowest machine. In code: await Promise.all(items.map(x => process(x))) — fires every async call simultaneously and waits for all. Spectacular speedup for independent operations; useless when each one depends on the previous. The catch: if the downstream is a database or HTTP API, firing a thousand requests at once may overload it.

Limited concurrency: three washing machines, ten shirts — as soon as one finishes, start the next, keeping all three busy. Faster than sequential, gentler than full-parallel, controlled. This is the pattern for "process 10,000 records, but never more than 20 at a time." A small worker-pool function (we will write one) keeps the downstream within its capacity while staying as fast as possible.

One additional primitive worth knowing: Promise.allSettled waits for all promises to finish, returning an array of each result with success or failure — useful when "I want all of them; some may fail and that is okay." Promise.all rejects as soon as any input rejects; allSettled never rejects.

Why It Matters

Pattern choice is often the single largest performance lever in a Node backend. A handler that loads 20 related records sequentially is twenty times slower than the same handler running them in parallel — and the parallel version is one line. A bulk-import job that fires 10,000 database inserts in parallel may melt the database; the same job at concurrency 20 finishes in a reasonable time and keeps the database alive. These are not edge cases; they are the everyday performance decisions every backend engineer makes.

Real-World Example

An endpoint loads a dashboard with three independent panels — recent orders, top customers, revenue summary. Each panel is a database query taking about 80ms. Sequential: 240ms total. Parallel with Promise.all: 80ms total. The fix is one line. The same endpoint also loads each order's line items — and there are forty orders. Forty parallel queries would spike the database; one-at-a-time would take three seconds. Limited concurrency at, say, 8 queries in-flight balances throughput with database load and finishes in about 400ms. Three pattern choices on one endpoint, three different reasons.

Enterprise Example

Enterprise integrations are almost entirely about concurrency control. A backfill script that re-syncs millions of records against an external API has rate limits to respect (often 100 requests per second), retries to handle, and partial failures to record. The shape is always limited concurrency with bounded retries and progress tracking — never raw Promise.all. Production teams either build a small worker-pool helper or use a tested library like p-limit or p-queue. The discipline of "what is the right concurrency for this downstream?" is part of every integration's design.

Step-by-Step Breakdown

Choosing the pattern:

  1. Does each step need the previous step's result? Yes → sequential. No → consider parallel.
  2. How many independent operations are there? A handful (under 10) → Promise.all is usually fine. Hundreds or thousands → use limited concurrency.
  3. Can the downstream handle full parallelism? If yes (in-process work, generous API limits) → parallel. If no (databases, rate-limited APIs) → limited concurrency at a tuned number.
  4. Do you need all results, or just some? Promise.all for "all or none" — rejects on first failure. Promise.allSettled for "all, even partial failures." Promise.race for "the first to finish."
  5. Implement, then measure. If a request handler uses sequential awaits for independent calls, your p95 latency is paying for it. The fix is usually one line.

Code Example

code
concur.mjs — three concurrency shapes// Three concurrency shapes for async work.
const fetchItem = (id, ms) =>
  new Promise(resolve => setTimeout(() => resolve(`item${id}`), ms));

// 1. Sequential — one after another, slow but ordered.
async function sequential() {
  const start = Date.now();
  const a = await fetchItem(1, 80);
  const b = await fetchItem(2, 80);
  const c = await fetchItem(3, 80);
  console.log('sequential:', [a, b, c], `(${Date.now() - start}ms)`);
}

// 2. Parallel — all at once, as fast as the slowest.
async function parallel() {
  const start = Date.now();
  const [a, b, c] = await Promise.all([
    fetchItem(1, 80),
    fetchItem(2, 80),
    fetchItem(3, 80),
  ]);
  console.log('parallel:', [a, b, c], `(${Date.now() - start}ms)`);
}

// 3. Limited — N at a time, controlled concurrency.
async function limited(items, n, fn) {
  const results = [];
  const queue = [...items];
  const workers = Array.from({ length: n }, async () => {
    while (queue.length) {
      const item = queue.shift();
      results.push(await fn(item));
    }
  });
  await Promise.all(workers);
  return results;
}

await sequential();
await parallel();
const r = await limited([1, 2, 3, 4, 5], 2, id => fetchItem(id, 40));
console.log('limited(2):', r);

Notice the limited-concurrency function: it creates n worker promises that each loop pulling items from a shared queue. When the queue empties, all workers finish, and Promise.all(workers) resolves. It is a small, self-contained pattern worth understanding because libraries that do the same thing all work this way underneath.

JS & TS Together

TypeScript types this elegantly:

code
concur.tsasync function limited<T, R>(
  items: T[],
  n: number,
  fn: (item: T) => Promise<R>
): Promise<R[]> { /* ... */ }

Generics make the function reusable for any item and result types — the compiler enforces that the function passed in returns the right promise. Module 3.4 covers generics properly.

Common Mistakes

Common Mistake

Sequential awaits when parallel would work. const a = await foo(); const b = await bar(); when foo and bar are independent is needless sequencing. The fix is const [a, b] = await Promise.all([foo(), bar()]).

Common Mistake

Unbounded parallelism over a list. await Promise.all(thousands.map(id => api.get(id))) sends all of them at once and can overload the API or hit rate limits. Use limited concurrency for any list of meaningful size.

Common Mistake

Forgetting that Promise.all rejects on first failure. If you have 100 calls and one fails, the other 99 may still be running but you have already exited the await with an error. Use Promise.allSettled when you need to see all results.

Principal Engineer Notes

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

A principal engineer treats "what is the right concurrency here?" as a conscious design decision, made deliberately. The default is parallel with Promise.all for small handful of independent operations, limited concurrency for any larger list, sequential only when ordering or dependency demands it. They tune the limit based on the downstream's known capacity, and they prefer the small worker-pool pattern (or a tested library) over hand-rolled rate-limiting.

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

They distinguish the cost of waiting from the cost of doing. JavaScript "parallel" is genuinely parallel for I/O-bound operations (network, disk, child processes) because the main thread is free while waiting; for CPU-bound work, parallel is a lie on one thread and a worker thread is needed (Module 9). Knowing which kind each operation is keeps the concurrency model honest.

Exercises & Solutions

Practice — Concurrency
  1. Write a function slow(label, ms) that resolves after ms milliseconds. Run three calls sequentially and time it. Then run them with Promise.all and time it. Confirm the ratio matches.
  2. Take a list of 20 items and process them with the limited(items, 4, fn) pattern. Confirm the elapsed time is roughly (total work / 4), not the sequential total.
  3. Make one of the 20 operations fail. Use Promise.allSettled instead of Promise.all: confirm you get back an array of {status, value | reason} for every item — including successes that ran after the failure.
Solution & What to Check
  1. Sequential of three 100ms calls: ~300ms. Promise.all of the same three: ~100ms. The ratio is the number of operations — for I/O-bound work, parallel wins hugely.
  2. Twenty 100ms operations at concurrency 4 take roughly 500ms (5 batches of 4). Compared to 2 seconds sequential, that is a 4× speedup — exactly what the concurrency limit promised.
  3. Promise.allSettled returns [{status:'fulfilled', value}, {status:'rejected', reason}, ...]. Successful calls remain visible even though one failed; you can log every failure and still process the successes.

Architecture Thinking

Concurrency control is an architectural concern, not just a micro-optimisation. The choice between sequential, parallel, and limited is also the choice between "this service overloads its downstream" and "this service is a polite citizen." A backfill that fires 10,000 parallel requests is technically faster but takes down the API it depends on; the same backfill at concurrency 20 is barely slower and never causes incidents. Concurrency limits are a form of backpressure — letting the slower part of the system govern the faster — and good Node services apply them deliberately at every boundary where they talk to something with limits.

Debugging Tips

  • Slow handler? Look for sequential awaits. Open the handler and trace which awaits depend on which previous values; ones that do not depend should be parallelised.
  • Database timeouts under load? Probably unbounded parallelism. Cap the concurrency at the database's connection pool size or less.
  • Use a Promise inspection helper for chains. Tagging promises with labels (a custom wrapper that logs start and end times) makes it easy to see what ran when in a complex parallel set.

Performance Considerations

Performance

The single highest-impact performance move in most Node code is parallelising independent awaits. Sequential awaits accumulate latency linearly; Promise.all collapses them to the slowest. The single most common performance disaster is unbounded parallelism over a long list — it sometimes "works" in development with 5 items and dies in production with 50,000. Limited concurrency, tuned to the downstream's capacity, is the production default for any list bigger than a handful. Module 9 covers performance investigation in depth; this chapter is the everyday discipline.

Build This — Mini-Project
Three Concurrency Patterns Side by Side

One script demonstrating sequential, parallel, and limited concurrency — with timing for each — so the difference is concrete. Once you have run this and seen the time numbers, choosing between the three becomes automatic.

code
concur.mjsconst fetchItem = (id, ms) =>
  new Promise(r => setTimeout(() => r(`item${id}`), ms));

async function sequential() {
  const start = Date.now();
  const a = await fetchItem(1, 80);
  const b = await fetchItem(2, 80);
  const c = await fetchItem(3, 80);
  console.log('sequential:', [a, b, c], `(${Date.now() - start}ms)`);
}

async function parallel() {
  const start = Date.now();
  const [a, b, c] = await Promise.all([
    fetchItem(1, 80), fetchItem(2, 80), fetchItem(3, 80)
  ]);
  console.log('parallel:', [a, b, c], `(${Date.now() - start}ms)`);
}

async function limited(items, n, fn) {
  const results = [], queue = [...items];
  const workers = Array.from({ length: n }, async () => {
    while (queue.length) results.push(await fn(queue.shift()));
  });
  await Promise.all(workers);
  return results;
}

await sequential();
await parallel();
const r = await limited([1, 2, 3, 4, 5], 2, id => fetchItem(id, 40));
console.log('limited(2):', r);
Expected result (your ms numbers will vary slightly):
code
sequential: [ 'item1', 'item2', 'item3' ] (243ms)
parallel: [ 'item1', 'item2', 'item3' ] (81ms)
limited(2): [ 'item1', 'item2', 'item3', 'item4', 'item5' ]
Three patterns, three timing profiles. The exact numbers will vary but the ratio is the lesson: sequential is the sum of work; parallel is the slowest single work; limited is somewhere in between, tuned by the limit. Choose by intent, every time.

Node ships with a complete standard library: filesystem, networking, crypto, URLs, processes, streams, binary data. Knowing what is built in saves you from installing packages for everything. This module tours the parts you will use daily, introduces TypeScript as the modern way to write Node, and finishes with npm and dependency hygiene — the disciplines that keep a project healthy as it grows.

Module 3 · Chapter 3.1

Filesystem, Paths & Streams

Reading and writing files is one of the oldest things a Node program does, and it is also where the difference between blocking and async, and between loading and streaming, becomes concrete. The same file-read can be a one-liner or a streaming pipeline; choosing the right shape is most of the chapter.

Lesson 3.1Files, Paths, and Reading Without Loading

Concept Overview

The node:fs module is Node's filesystem API, and it comes in three flavours. fs.promises (the modern default) returns promises and integrates naturally with async/await. Callback-style fs is the older convention. Sync versions (fs.readFileSync, fs.writeFileSync) block the event loop and are reserved for one-shot scripts. Paths come from the node:path module — path.join(), path.resolve(), path.dirname() — for working with platform-correct path separators. For files too large to load into memory, streams let you process them chunk by chunk: createReadStream(file) emits buffers as they are read, which you consume with for await (const chunk of stream) or pipe to another stream.

Plain English Explanation

Think of a file three ways. As a small piece of text — say a config file, a few kilobytes — you read the whole thing into a variable and work with it. That is readFile: simple, fast for small files, and almost everything fits this shape.

As a huge file — a multi-gigabyte log, a CSV with millions of rows — you do not want to load it into memory all at once. Imagine trying to read a thousand-page book by photographing every page first; or imagine reading it page by page instead. That second way is a stream: a sequence of small chunks arriving as Node reads them off disk. You process each chunk, then forget it. Memory usage stays tiny no matter how big the file is.

Paths are the other small chapter. On macOS and Linux the separator is /; on Windows it is \. If you write 'data/users.json' as a string it works on Mac and Linux but breaks on Windows. path.join('data', 'users.json') uses the right separator for the running OS — and it normalises away duplicate slashes and resolves .. sensibly. path.resolve() does the same but also makes the path absolute (anchored at the current working directory). Use these instead of concatenating strings.

One sharp edge worth knowing: the sync versions (readFileSync) block the event loop. In a script that runs and exits, that is fine. In a long-running server, calling readFileSync on a large file pauses every other request while it runs (Chapter 2.2). Default to the promise-based API; reach for sync only for one-shot scripts that have nothing else to do.

Why It Matters

Backend services read configuration files at startup, write logs, parse uploads, generate exports. Knowing the right shape — load the whole file, or stream it — is the difference between a service that handles a 5GB upload in 200MB of memory and one that crashes with "out of memory." Knowing node:path is the difference between code that works on every developer's laptop and code that breaks for the one teammate on Windows. These are tiny disciplines with large consequences.

Real-World Example

A service generates monthly reports — each one a CSV summarising millions of database rows. The first implementation loads all the rows into memory, builds one giant string, and writes it. With a small customer that works; with a large one, Node runs out of memory. The streaming version queries the database row by row, formats each row, and pipes the output into a write stream. Memory usage stays under 100MB regardless of how many rows there are. Same job, very different runtime profile — and the only difference is "load it all" versus "stream it through."

Enterprise Example

Enterprise file handling is often streaming end to end: readStream from disk → pipeline() through a decompressor → through a CSV parser → through a database batch inserter → finish. Errors at any stage propagate; memory stays flat. The node:stream/promises module exposes pipeline(), which composes streams safely with error handling — preferred over piping manually because it cleans up if any stage fails. The mental model is "the file flows through a pipe of transformations" — exactly the Unix tradition.

Step-by-Step Breakdown

Choosing the shape:

  1. Is the file small and known in size? readFile() / writeFile(). One line, all in memory. Fine for config, small JSON, source files.
  2. Is the file potentially huge? Stream it. Use createReadStream() / createWriteStream(), consume chunks with for await, or compose with pipeline().
  3. Is this one-shot CLI work that does nothing else? The sync versions are acceptable — there is no event loop to block.
  4. Is this a long-running service? Never sync. fs.promises everything.
  5. Are you building paths? Always path.join(), never string concatenation. For import-relative paths use new URL('./file', import.meta.url) (modern ESM idiom).

Code Example

css
fs.mjs — reading, writing, and streamingimport { readFile, writeFile, mkdir } from 'node:fs/promises';
import { createReadStream } from 'node:fs';
import { join } from 'node:path';

const dir = './tmp-fs-demo';
await mkdir(dir, { recursive: true });
const file = join(dir, 'hello.txt');

// Promise-based fs — the modern Node API
await writeFile(file, 'Hello, filesystem!\nLine two.\n', 'utf8');
const text = await readFile(file, 'utf8');
console.log('read:', text.trim());

// Streaming — process a file chunk by chunk, never holding it all in memory
let total = 0;
for await (const chunk of createReadStream(file)) {
  total += chunk.length;
}
console.log('streamed bytes:', total);

Three details worth noticing. First, the imports use the node: prefix — that is the modern, explicit way to import Node built-ins, recommended over the unprefixed form. Second, readFile(file, 'utf8') with an encoding returns a string; without an encoding it returns a Buffer (Chapter 3.3). Third, the streaming loop uses for await (const chunk of stream) — every Node stream is also an async iterable, so this clean syntax just works.

JS & TS Together

TypeScript types the promises and the stream chunks. readFile with an encoding returns Promise<string>; without one, Promise<Buffer>. The stream chunk in for await is typed Buffer by default. Module 3.4 covers TypeScript proper; the API itself is identical.

Common Mistakes

Common Mistake

Using readFileSync in a server. Blocks the event loop while the file is read — every other request waits. Always use the promise version in long-running services.

Common Mistake

Concatenating paths with +. './data/' + name works on Mac but breaks on Windows when name already has a leading slash. Always path.join().

Common Mistake

Loading huge files with readFile. Reading a 5GB file into a string crashes Node. Anything potentially large gets streamed.

Principal Engineer Notes

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

A principal engineer asks "what is the worst-case size of this file?" before choosing an API. Anything bounded and small — readFile. Anything possibly unbounded — stream. The decision is made before the bug appears in production with a customer's unusually large file.

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

They prefer pipeline() from node:stream/promises over manual piping. It handles errors and cleanup; manual piping leaks streams on failure. The composition reads cleanly: await pipeline(source, transform, destination).

Exercises & Solutions

Practice — Filesystem
  1. Write a script that creates a folder, writes three text files into it with different content, then reads each one back and prints its length. Use only fs.promises and path.join.
  2. Generate a 10MB text file (a loop of long strings written to a createWriteStream). Then read it back with createReadStream and count the total bytes. Memory usage should stay flat — print process.memoryUsage().heapUsed before and after.
  3. Try the same 10MB file with readFile instead of streaming. Note the memory jump. This is the difference the chapter taught.
Solution & What to Check
  1. await mkdir(dir, { recursive: true }); await writeFile(join(dir, 'a.txt'), '...') three times; await readFile(join(dir, 'a.txt'), 'utf8'); .length is the character count.
  2. Streaming version keeps heapUsed roughly constant regardless of file size — chunks are processed and released. The total bytes printed equals the file size.
  3. readFile of a 10MB file pushes heap up by about 10MB. With a 1GB file it pushes by 1GB; with 5GB Node crashes. Streaming is unbounded-safe.

Architecture Thinking

Streams are backpressure-aware pipelines — the fast end naturally waits for the slow end, so memory stays bounded. This is a general architectural pattern: instead of "load it all, then process," you build a chain of small transformations that data flows through. The same shape applies to message queues, log processors, and ETL — anywhere data is too large or unbounded to fit in memory. Once you see "stream through transformations" as a pattern, the next time you face a large-data problem, you reach for it instinctively.

Debugging Tips

  • "ENOENT: no such file or directory" — wrong path, usually because process.cwd() is not what you expected. Print process.cwd() alongside the path you tried.
  • "EACCES" — permission denied. The Node process does not have read or write access to that path.
  • Stream "never ends" — you probably consumed it twice, or its writer never closed. Streams emit 'end' when the source is exhausted.

Performance Considerations

Performance

The throughput rule: small files — readFile is faster because it is one syscall; large or unknown-size files — streams are essential because they keep memory bounded. The crossover is around a few MB on a modern machine. For very high-throughput workloads, watch out for fs.promises overhead and consider fs.openSync with manual reads, but only with profiling — most services never need that level of tuning.

Build This — Mini-Project
Write, Read, and Stream a File

One script that creates a folder, writes a file with the promise API, reads it back, then reads it again as a stream — counting bytes chunk by chunk. The two read shapes side by side make the choice concrete.

css
fs.mjsimport { readFile, writeFile, mkdir } from 'node:fs/promises';
import { createReadStream } from 'node:fs';
import { join } from 'node:path';

const dir = './tmp-fs-demo';
await mkdir(dir, { recursive: true });
const file = join(dir, 'hello.txt');

await writeFile(file, 'Hello, filesystem!\nLine two.\n', 'utf8');
const text = await readFile(file, 'utf8');
console.log('read:', text.trim());

let total = 0;
for await (const chunk of createReadStream(file)) {
  total += chunk.length;
}
console.log('streamed bytes:', total);
Expected result:
code
read: Hello, filesystem!
Line two.
streamed bytes: 29
Same file, two reads. The first loaded it whole; the second streamed it. For a 29-byte file the difference is academic; for a 29GB file it is the difference between success and out-of-memory.
Module 3 · Chapter 3.2

Process, OS, Crypto, URL

Four small standard-library modules you will use everywhere: node:os for facts about the machine, node:crypto for random IDs and hashes, the global URL for parsing addresses, plus the rest of process from Chapter 2.1 in real use. Knowing these saves dependencies and saves bugs — every one of them replaces a third-party library people install by reflex.

Lesson 3.2The Built-in Toolkit

Concept Overview

node:os exposes facts about the host machine — hostname(), platform(), cpus(), totalmem(), freemem(), uptime(), homedir(). node:crypto provides the cryptographic primitives — randomUUID(), randomBytes(n), createHash('sha256'), createHmac(), key generation, and the scryptSync/scrypt password hashes for Module 6. The global URL class parses and constructs URLs correctly and exposes a searchParams for query strings — replacing every "split the string on ?" trick from old code. Together these are the daily backend toolkit.

Plain English Explanation

node:os is "what machine am I running on?" — hostname, OS, CPU count, memory, uptime. Use it for health endpoints, for sensible defaults (default worker count to number of CPUs), and for diagnostics in logs.

node:crypto is the right place for anything related to randomness or hashing. Never roll your own. randomUUID() gives you a standard v4 UUID — perfect for request IDs, idempotency keys, and most "unique identifier" needs. randomBytes(16).toString('hex') gives a hex random string of any length. createHash('sha256').update(data).digest('hex') is the hash function you reach for to fingerprint a file or compute a content-addressable key. For password hashing — covered in Module 6 — use scrypt or argon2; never raw sha256.

The URL class parses URLs correctly. Given new URL('https://example.com/path?q=node&page=2'), you get a structured object: .host, .pathname, .searchParams (a URLSearchParams with .get() / .set()). Manual string-splitting on ? and & is bug-prone — escaping, encoding, fragments, default ports — and the URL class handles all of it. Always use it.

Why It Matters

Most beginner Node projects install a UUID library, a URL parser, and an environment-variable reader, none of which they need — Node ships all three. Knowing the standard library means shorter dependency lists, smaller bundles, fewer security audits, and code that works without npm install. The principle generalises: always check what is built in before installing.

Real-World Example

A handler needs a request ID, a content hash for caching, and parsing of the incoming URL's query string. Without the standard library, the engineer installs uuid, crypto-js, and query-string. With it, three lines: const id = randomUUID(); const hash = createHash('sha256').update(body).digest('hex'); const page = new URL(req.url, 'http://x').searchParams.get('page');. Zero new dependencies, zero supply-chain risk, faster cold start. The standard library is usually enough.

Enterprise Example

At scale, dependency hygiene is a real concern: every package added is a future security audit, a supply-chain risk, and a startup-time cost. Enterprise Node teams have explicit policies to prefer the standard library — and node:os, node:crypto, and the URL class cover most of what general-purpose packages do. The win is not just "fewer dependencies" — it is fewer things that can break, fewer things to keep up to date, and fewer surfaces a supply-chain attack can use.

Step-by-Step Breakdown

Common tasks and the right tool:

  1. Generate a unique ID → randomUUID(). Never Math.random() — not cryptographically random.
  2. Generate a random token → randomBytes(n).toString('base64url'). Cryptographically strong.
  3. Hash data for fingerprinting → createHash('sha256').update(data).digest('hex'). For passwords use a slow hash (Module 6).
  4. Sign or verify a message → createHmac('sha256', secret).update(message).digest('hex'). Use a constant-time comparison for verification.
  5. Parse a URL → new URL(input). Build query strings with URLSearchParams; never concatenate.
  6. Pick worker count → os.cpus().length. Memory sizing → os.totalmem().

Code Example

css
stdlib.mjs — four built-ins in real useimport { hostname, platform, cpus, totalmem } from 'node:os';
import { randomUUID, createHash } from 'node:crypto';

console.log('host:', hostname());
console.log('platform:', platform());
console.log('cpu count:', cpus().length);
console.log('total memory (MB):', Math.round(totalmem() / 1024 / 1024));

// crypto — random IDs and hashes (use these, never roll your own)
console.log('uuid:', randomUUID());
console.log('sha256(hello):', createHash('sha256').update('hello').digest('hex').slice(0, 16) + '...');

// URL — the WHATWG URL class, no string-splitting
const u = new URL('https://example.com/path?q=node&page=2');
console.log('host:', u.host);
console.log('search q:', u.searchParams.get('q'));

JS & TS Together

All four modules are fully typed in TypeScript — randomUUID() returns a template literal type narrowing to UUID format, new URL(...) returns a fully typed URL with helpful method completions. Module 3.4 covers the setup.

Common Mistakes

Common Mistake

Using Math.random() for security purposes. Predictable. Use crypto.randomUUID() or crypto.randomBytes() for any value that an attacker should not guess.

Common Mistake

Manual URL parsing. Strings like url.split('?')[1].split('&') miss encoded characters, fragments, multiple values. Use URL and URLSearchParams.

Common Mistake

Comparing hashes with ===. For HMACs and signatures, use crypto.timingSafeEqual() to avoid timing-attack leaks. Plain === is fine for non-secret fingerprints.

Principal Engineer Notes

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

A principal engineer reaches for node: built-ins first and installs a package only when the built-in is genuinely insufficient. Every dependency added is a future maintenance burden and a security surface; the standard library is patched by Node and trusted by default.

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

They use randomUUID() universally for IDs — for HTTP request correlation, idempotency keys, job IDs, file names. Unique, unpredictable, free. Database primary keys are an exception (use the DB's own ID generation for clustering reasons), but for in-app IDs, UUID is the default.

Exercises & Solutions

Practice — Built-ins
  1. Write a script that prints the hostname, OS platform, CPU count, and total memory in GB.
  2. Write a function fingerprint(text) that returns the first 16 hex characters of a SHA-256 hash. Use it to fingerprint two strings; confirm the fingerprints differ.
  3. Given the URL 'https://api.example.com/v1/users?limit=10&page=3', extract the host, the path, and both query parameters using URL and URLSearchParams.
Solution & What to Check
  1. console.log(hostname(), platform(), cpus().length, Math.round(totalmem() / 1024**3)); — four facts about the machine, four built-in calls.
  2. createHash('sha256').update(text).digest('hex').slice(0, 16). Two different strings produce different fingerprints (essentially always — collisions are vanishingly unlikely).
  3. const u = new URL(...); u.host is 'api.example.com'; u.pathname is '/v1/users'; u.searchParams.get('limit') is the string '10' (always a string — Number() if you need a number).

Architecture Thinking

Choosing the standard library over a package is an instance of a deeper architectural value: prefer the platform. Every layer of your stack (Node, the OS, the database, the HTTP spec) has primitives, and building on the primitives directly leaves you fewer abstractions to debug. The packages worth installing are the ones that genuinely add capability — not those that wrap a built-in for a slightly different API.

Debugging Tips

  • randomUUID() is collision-free for practical purposes — if two of yours collide, something else is wrong (caching, mistaken reuse).
  • If a URL parses wrong — print u.href and JSON.stringify(u) to see every component. Most surprises are encoded characters or default ports.
  • For hash mismatches, verify input encoding — 'hello' as utf8 vs binary hashes to different values.

Performance Considerations

Performance

SHA-256 in Node is implemented in C and is fast — millions of operations per second on small inputs. randomUUID() uses the kernel's CSPRNG and is similarly fast. new URL() parses in microseconds. None of these are bottlenecks at any normal scale; reach for them freely.

Build This — Mini-Project
A Tour of os, crypto, and URL

One script that touches each of the four modules and prints a fact from each. Once you have run it, the standard-library shape is in your hands — and you will reach for these built-ins before installing equivalents.

css
stdlib.mjsimport { hostname, platform, cpus, totalmem } from 'node:os';
import { randomUUID, createHash } from 'node:crypto';

console.log('host:', hostname());
console.log('platform:', platform());
console.log('cpu count:', cpus().length);
console.log('total memory (MB):', Math.round(totalmem() / 1024 / 1024));
console.log('uuid:', randomUUID());
console.log('sha256(hello):', createHash('sha256').update('hello').digest('hex').slice(0, 16) + '...');

const u = new URL('https://example.com/path?q=node&page=2');
console.log('host:', u.host);
console.log('search q:', u.searchParams.get('q'));
Expected result (your machine's facts will differ):
code
host: my-laptop
platform: darwin
cpu count: 8
total memory (MB): 16384
uuid: 60ece87f-4bdd-4a87-82da-d340c05d7898
sha256(hello): 2cf24dba5fb0a30e...
host: example.com
search q: node
Four built-in modules, eight lines of code, zero dependencies. This is what Node's standard library is for.
Module 3 · Chapter 3.3

Buffers & Binary Data

JavaScript's string is for text. Node's Buffer is for raw bytes — the language of files, network packets, encrypted data, and image bytes. You will see Buffers returned from filesystem and network APIs every day; understanding them turns "what is this weird thing?" into "exactly the right tool."

Lesson 3.3Working With Raw Bytes

Concept Overview

A Buffer is Node's representation of a fixed-size sequence of bytes — raw binary data, outside V8's normal heap. It is a subclass of Uint8Array, so it works with every modern binary API, but it also has Node-specific conveniences: Buffer.from(string, 'utf8') to encode text; buf.toString('hex' | 'base64' | 'utf8') to decode; Buffer.alloc(n) to make a zeroed buffer of a given size; Buffer.concat([a, b]) to join several. Buffers are how the filesystem hands you a file's bytes, how the network gives you a packet, and how crypto produces digests. Strings and Buffers are not interchangeable — converting between them requires an encoding.

Plain English Explanation

A string is a sequence of characters — humans think of it as letters. A Buffer is a sequence of bytes — the computer thinks of it as numbers between 0 and 255. The two are related: any string can be turned into bytes (you have to choose an encoding, almost always 'utf8' for text), and any bytes can be displayed as a string (using the same encoding) — but they are not the same thing.

Most of the time in application code, you work with strings and never notice Buffers exist. The moment you read a file without specifying an encoding, you get a Buffer back — because Node does not know how to decode the bytes without your instruction. The moment you receive an HTTP request body or a raw network message, you get a Buffer. The moment you hash data, you get a Buffer (then call .digest('hex') to turn it back into a string). Buffers are the binary substrate; strings are one way to interpret them.

Three Buffer operations you will use constantly. Buffer.from('text', 'utf8') creates bytes from a string — the encoding is the rule for the conversion. buf.toString(encoding, start, end) reads bytes back into a string; common encodings are 'utf8' (text), 'hex' (each byte as two hex characters — great for hashes), and 'base64' (compact text representation — great for binary in JSON or HTTP headers). And Buffer.concat([a, b, c]) joins multiple Buffers into one — a common pattern when reading chunks from a stream and assembling them.

Worth knowing: Buffer in Node is a thin layer over the standard Uint8Array — newer code (especially web-platform code that runs in browsers and Deno too) often uses Uint8Array directly. They are largely interchangeable; Buffer adds the encoding helpers and some performance tricks.

Why It Matters

Every file you read without specifying encoding is a Buffer. Every HTTP body, every WebSocket message, every cryptographic operation traffics in Buffers. You cannot avoid them; you can only fail to understand them. Engineers who treat Buffers as "weird strings" produce bugs around encoding ("why does my UTF-8 file appear corrupted?"). Engineers who hold the model — bytes underneath, strings on top, encoding the bridge — produce code that handles binary correctly the first time.

Real-World Example

A handler accepts file uploads. The bytes arrive as a series of Buffer chunks from the network. The handler concatenates them with Buffer.concat(chunks), then either writes them to disk (Buffer-friendly) or computes a SHA-256 hash for content addressing — crypto operates on Buffers natively. Trying to convert the chunks to strings first would corrupt binary uploads. Knowing that Buffers are the right shape end to end keeps the implementation honest.

Enterprise Example

Enterprise services dealing with binary protocols — image processing, document conversion, custom wire formats, IoT — live entirely in Buffers. Performance-critical paths sometimes write bytes by hand: buf.writeUInt32BE(value, offset) writes a four-byte big-endian unsigned integer at a precise position; the corresponding buf.readUInt32BE(offset) reads it back. These primitives are how you build or parse a custom binary protocol — and the same skill applies when reading file headers, network packets, or certificate ASN.1 structures.

Step-by-Step Breakdown

Common Buffer tasks:

  1. Make a Buffer from a string → Buffer.from('text', 'utf8'). The encoding matters; for text default to 'utf8'.
  2. Read a Buffer as a string → buf.toString('utf8') or buf.toString('hex') for binary inspection.
  3. Allocate an empty Buffer → Buffer.alloc(n) (zeroed) or Buffer.allocUnsafe(n) (random previous-memory contents, use only if you will overwrite immediately).
  4. Combine many Buffers → Buffer.concat([a, b, c]). For streams, accumulate chunks then concat.
  5. Read or write specific binary values → buf.readUInt32BE(0), buf.writeFloatLE(3.14, 4), and friends. Big-endian (BE) for most network protocols, little-endian (LE) for most file formats.
  6. Inspect bytes → buf.toString('hex') is the universal debugger view; every byte appears as two hex digits.

Code Example

code
buffer.mjs — three Buffer operations in real use// Strings are text; Buffers are raw bytes. Many Node APIs hand you Buffers.

// 1. Create from a string and inspect bytes
const buf = Buffer.from('Héllo', 'utf8');
console.log('length (bytes):', buf.length);    // 6 — é is 2 bytes in utf8
console.log('hex:', buf.toString('hex'));      // 48c3a96c6c6f
console.log('back to string:', buf.toString('utf8'));

// 2. Allocate a zeroed buffer and write into it
const out = Buffer.alloc(4);
out.writeUInt32BE(1234567890, 0);   // 32-bit unsigned, big-endian, at offset 0
console.log('uint32 BE bytes:', out.toString('hex'));   // 499602d2
console.log('read back:', out.readUInt32BE(0));

// 3. Concat — combine many buffers efficiently
const joined = Buffer.concat([Buffer.from('foo:'), Buffer.from(' bar')]);
console.log('joined:', joined.toString());

The detail that surprises beginners: 'Héllo' is 5 characters but 6 bytes — the é is encoded as two bytes (c3 a9) in UTF-8. Always distinguish character count from byte count when working with binary.

JS & TS Together

In TypeScript, Buffer.from(string) returns Buffer; the read/write methods are typed by which numeric kind they handle (readUInt32BE returns number). Modern web-platform-compatible code prefers Uint8Array for cross-runtime portability, but in Node codebases Buffer remains the daily default.

Common Mistakes

Common Mistake

Calling .toString() on binary data without thinking. If the Buffer holds binary (not text), .toString('utf8') produces a corrupted string. Use .toString('hex') or .toString('base64').

Common Mistake

Confusing characters and bytes. A string's .length is character count; Buffer.byteLength(str, 'utf8') is byte count. For protocols that count bytes (Content-Length), use the byte count.

Common Mistake

Using Buffer.allocUnsafe and forgetting to overwrite it. Its initial contents are whatever was in memory — potentially sensitive. Unless you immediately fill the buffer, use Buffer.alloc.

Principal Engineer Notes

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

A principal engineer holds the strict distinction: Buffers are bytes, strings are characters, encodings bridge them. They never let binary accidentally become text. When debugging, they reach for .toString('hex') to see exact bytes — surprises are almost always character-vs-byte confusion.

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

They prefer streams to large Buffers. A 1GB file as one Buffer is a memory disaster; the same file streamed in 64KB chunks is fine. Chapter 3.1 covered this; the discipline of "size-bounded chunks" applies to networks too.

Exercises & Solutions

Practice — Buffers
  1. Print Buffer.from('café', 'utf8').length. Predict the number first.
  2. Encode a small string as base64, decode it back, confirm equality.
  3. Write a 4-byte Buffer containing the number 305419896 as a 32-bit big-endian integer, then read it back as little-endian and observe the byte-reversal.
Solution & What to Check
  1. café in UTF-8 is 5 bytes — c, a, f, then é as two bytes. Always longer than the character count for non-ASCII text.
  2. Buffer.from('hello').toString('base64') → 'aGVsbG8='; Buffer.from('aGVsbG8=', 'base64').toString('utf8') → 'hello'.
  3. Big-endian 305419896 is hex 12345678. Read with little-endian → 2018915346 (hex 78563412). Byte order matters; choose deliberately.

Architecture Thinking

Buffers are where Node meets the world outside JavaScript — disks, networks, OS APIs. The discipline of treating them as bytes-not-strings is the same discipline as treating types precisely in Chapter 1.1: respecting what a thing actually is, never blurring it for convenience. That respect becomes load-bearing the moment binary protocols, file formats, or low-level integrations enter the picture.

Debugging Tips

  • Print buf.toString('hex') to see exact bytes. Strings hide invisible characters; hex does not.
  • If decoded text is "mojibake" (garbled), the encoding used is wrong. Common culprits: assuming UTF-8 when the source is Latin-1 or Windows-1252.
  • Out-of-range reads/writes throw — Node's read/write methods refuse to go past the buffer's length.

Performance Considerations

Performance

Buffers are allocated outside V8's heap, which means very large Buffers do not pressure JavaScript's garbage collector. Allocation itself has a small cost; reuse Buffers in tight loops if profiling shows it. Buffer.concat on many small chunks allocates a new big Buffer — for stream pipelines, prefer to write each chunk to a destination rather than buffering them all.

Build This — Mini-Project
Three Buffer Operations

One script that creates a Buffer from a non-ASCII string, allocates a Buffer and writes a 32-bit integer to it, and concatenates two Buffers. After running it, the gap between strings and bytes is concrete in your head.

code
buffer.mjsconst buf = Buffer.from('Héllo', 'utf8');
console.log('length (bytes):', buf.length);
console.log('hex:', buf.toString('hex'));
console.log('back to string:', buf.toString('utf8'));

const out = Buffer.alloc(4);
out.writeUInt32BE(1234567890, 0);
console.log('uint32 BE bytes:', out.toString('hex'));
console.log('read back:', out.readUInt32BE(0));

const joined = Buffer.concat([Buffer.from('foo:'), Buffer.from(' bar')]);
console.log('joined:', joined.toString());
Expected result:
code
length (bytes): 6
hex: 48c3a96c6c6f
back to string: Héllo
uint32 BE bytes: 499602d2
read back: 1234567890
joined: foo: bar
Strings, bytes, and the encoding that bridges them. Carry this model into every binary-touching chapter.
Module 3 · Chapter 3.4

Introducing TypeScript

The moment to add types. TypeScript is JavaScript with a type system layered on top — same runtime, same syntax mostly, but with a build-time checker that catches whole categories of bugs before they ship. Modern Node 22+ can run TypeScript files directly. From here on, the course shows both flavours.

Lesson 3.4Types as a Build-Time Contract

Concept Overview

TypeScript is a superset of JavaScript: every valid JS program is a valid TS program. What TS adds is a static type system that verifies, before runtime, that your code uses values consistently. Annotations like function add(a: number, b: number): number declare the contract; the compiler refuses to build code that violates it. At runtime, TS code is identical to JS — types are erased — but the compile-time enforcement eliminates a whole class of "X is undefined" bugs. Modern Node (22+) ships with type-stripping built in — run node --experimental-strip-types file.ts and Node runs the file directly, stripping types as it goes — so a small project does not even need a separate build step.

Plain English Explanation

JavaScript lets a variable hold anything. TypeScript lets you say "this variable holds a number," and from then on the compiler refuses any line of code that tries to put a string in it. It is a layer of promises about your code that the computer can verify before you run a single line. Most bugs new engineers write — passing a number where a string was expected, forgetting that a value might be undefined, typing the wrong property name — are caught instantly by a type-aware editor and a TypeScript compile pass.

Three patterns are enough to start. Annotations on parameters and return types: function greet(user: User): string. Type aliases or interfaces for shaping objects: type User = { id: number; name: string }; or interface User { id: number; name: string; }. Generics for functions that work for any type: function first<T>(arr: T[]): T | undefined — the T stands for any type and is inferred from each call site. That is 80% of practical TypeScript; the rest is variations and utility types.

For setup, two options. Modern Node native strip: write .ts files, run with node --experimental-strip-types or node --no-warnings --experimental-strip-types. No compile step, no tsconfig.json needed. Good for small projects and the rest of this course. Full TypeScript build: install typescript, run tsc to compile to .js, ship the compiled output. Necessary for advanced features (decorators, complex configs), production builds with type checking in CI, and editor integration. Module 8 covers the full production build flow.

Why It Matters

Almost every serious Node codebase in 2026 is TypeScript. Two reasons: first, the type-checker catches a category of bugs that take hours to find at runtime — "undefined is not a function," "X is missing field Y." Second, types are documentation: a function signature shows what it takes and returns without reading the body. New engineers join a codebase and navigate by types. Skipping TypeScript on a production Node project today is a choice that costs the team more later than it saves now.

Real-World Example

A handler signature changes: getUser used to return User and now returns User | null (because the lookup can fail). In JavaScript, this change is invisible — every caller still compiles and runs, but every caller now has a latent bug when the lookup misses. In TypeScript, the compiler flags every caller that did not handle null. Fix them, ship, done. Same change, two completely different stakes. Types make refactoring safe.

Enterprise Example

Enterprise codebases lean on TypeScript at every layer. API request/response types are shared between server and client (often via a shared types package or code generation from an OpenAPI spec). Database row types are generated from the schema (Module 5). Background job payloads are typed end to end. The result is a system where changing a field name in one place fails to compile everywhere else — the change becomes "follow the compiler errors until they are gone." A large codebase without types is one where the same change is days of grep, runtime surprises, and incident reviews.

Step-by-Step Breakdown

Adopting TypeScript:

  1. Use .ts extension for files with type annotations. (.mts for explicit ESM; both run.)
  2. Run with modern Node: node --experimental-strip-types file.ts. Types are stripped at runtime — the .ts file runs directly.
  3. For full type checking, install typescript globally or in the project and run tsc --noEmit to check without emitting JS. CI runs this to fail the build on type errors.
  4. Annotate parameters and return types of public functions. Inside, let TypeScript infer.
  5. Shape objects with type or interface — equivalent for most purposes. Convention: interface for object shapes that may be extended; type for unions, computed types, and aliases.
  6. Use generics for functions that operate over multiple types — but introduce them only when needed; over-generic code is hard to read.

Code Example

js
typed.ts — types, generics, and run with node --experimental-strip-types// Plain JS with type annotations. Node 22+ can strip types directly.
type User = { id: number; name: string; email?: string };

function greet(user: User): string {
  return `Hello, ${user.name}! (#${user.id})`;
}

const u: User = { id: 7, name: 'Billy' };
console.log(greet(u));

// generics — a function that works for any type T
function first<T>(arr: T[]): T | undefined {
  return arr[0];
}
console.log('first number:', first([10, 20, 30]));
console.log('first string:', first(['a', 'b', 'c']));
code
terminal$ node --experimental-strip-types typed.ts
Hello, Billy! (#7)
first number: 10
first string: a

Three things worth noticing. The email?: string field is optional (the question mark). T | undefined means "either a T or undefined" — a union type, which TypeScript forces you to handle. And the first function's T is inferred from each call site — first([10, 20, 30]) picks T = number automatically.

JS & TS Together

From this chapter on, every later Code Example carries both forms when they differ. Conventionally the JS comes first (so beginners see the runtime clearly); the TS upgrade follows, often adding only annotations.

Common Mistakes

Common Mistake

Reaching for any to silence the compiler. any disables type-checking for that value and infects everywhere it flows. Use unknown if you genuinely do not know the shape, and narrow it with a type guard before use.

Common Mistake

Treating TypeScript as a runtime check. Types are erased at runtime; they do not validate incoming data. For that you need a runtime validator (zod, valibot) — Module 4.4 covers this.

Common Mistake

Over-annotating internals. Annotate function signatures and exports; let TypeScript infer everything inside. Excess annotation is noise.

Principal Engineer Notes

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

A principal engineer enables strict mode from day one — no implicit any, null checks on, strict function types. The compiler is on your side, but only if it has enough information to catch bugs; loose mode is half a type system.

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

They never use as to assert a type unless they have already runtime-checked the value. as User tells the compiler "trust me, this is a User" — and if it is not, the bug appears at runtime in a place far from the assertion. Validate at the boundary; the compiler enforces from there.

Exercises & Solutions

Practice — TypeScript
  1. Write a User type, a greet function, and run them with node --experimental-strip-types file.ts.
  2. Make the email field optional. Write a describeUser function that includes the email if present, "(no email)" if not. The TypeScript compiler should require you to handle both cases.
  3. Write a generic last<T>(arr: T[]): T | undefined. Call it with a string array and a number array; confirm the inferred return type is correct in your editor.
Solution & What to Check
  1. The file runs and prints the greeting. If you write an annotation incorrectly, Node reports a syntax error; if you use a type incorrectly, run tsc --noEmit to see the type error.
  2. The function uses user.email ?? '(no email)' or an if (user.email) narrowing. TypeScript treats user.email as string | undefined until you check.
  3. last([10, 20]) has return type number | undefined; last(['a', 'b']) has return type string | undefined — the same function, different inferred type each call.

Architecture Thinking

Types are contracts: a function's signature is a promise about what it consumes and produces, and contracts compose. A typed codebase becomes a graph of contracts whose consistency the compiler verifies. This is the same architectural value as schemas in databases or interfaces in object-oriented design: making the connections between parts explicit and machine-checked. Once you have lived in a typed codebase for a quarter, going back feels like debugging blindfolded.

Debugging Tips

  • Editor squiggles are the fastest feedback loop. A good editor (VS Code with the TypeScript extension) shows type errors as you type. Use them.
  • Hover for types. Hovering any variable in VS Code shows its inferred type — a great way to confirm your mental model matches what the compiler thinks.
  • tsc --noEmit --pretty is the build- server check: type-check the whole project, do not produce output, format errors readably.

Performance Considerations

Performance

TypeScript adds zero runtime cost — types are erased. The cost is at compile time: a large codebase can take seconds to type-check. Run type-checking in CI; locally, the editor's incremental checker is fast enough to ignore. Node's --experimental-strip-types is faster than full compilation because it does not type-check — fine for development, but pair it with tsc --noEmit in CI to catch real type errors.

Build This — Mini-Project
A Typed Greeting

Write a .ts file with a type, a function, and a generic. Run it with Node's native type stripping. Once this works, you have crossed into TypeScript and the rest of the course's TS examples are runnable on your machine.

css
typed.tstype User = { id: number; name: string; email?: string };

  return `Hello, ${user.name}! (#${user.id})`;
}
const u: User = { id: 7, name: 'Billy' };
console.log(greet(u));

function first<T>(arr: T[]): T | undefined {
  return arr[0];
}
console.log('first number:', first([10, 20, 30]));
console.log('first string:', first(['a', 'b', 'c']));
code
terminal$ node --experimental-strip-types typed.ts
Expected result:
code

first number: 10
first string: a
Three lines of output, three TypeScript features applied — type annotations, an interface, and a generic. From here, you are a TypeScript engineer.
Module 3 · Chapter 3.5

Packages, npm & Dependency Hygiene

The npm ecosystem is what makes Node practical for real work — a database driver, an HTTP client, a job queue all install with a single command. But every dependency is a trust decision. This chapter covers how packages work, how to manage them well, and how to keep your project from drowning in node_modules.

Lesson 3.5Installing, Versioning, Locking

Concept Overview

A package is a published module — code plus a package.json manifest — distributed via the npm registry. Your project's package.json lists its dependencies by name and version range; running npm install resolves and downloads them into a node_modules folder. The resolved versions are pinned in a lock file (package-lock.json) so every developer and every deploy gets the exact same versions. Scripts in package.json are short commands you run via npm run name — the convention for starting the app, running tests, building. Dependencies come in two flavours: dependencies (needed at runtime in production) and devDependencies (build tools, test frameworks, types). Modern alternatives to npm — pnpm and yarn — use the same package.json with faster, more efficient installs.

Plain English Explanation

npm is a giant public library of reusable Node code. When your project needs to talk to PostgreSQL, you do not write a PostgreSQL driver from scratch — you npm install pg and use the one thousands of teams already use. The driver lives in node_modules in your project folder; your code imports from it like any other module.

Three things to understand. Semver ranges: a dependency listed as "pg": "^8.11.0" means "version 8.11.0 or any compatible newer one — the same major version." The lock file records the exact version actually installed (say 8.11.3) so everyone gets the same thing. Scripts in package.json let you write "start": "node index.mjs" and run it with npm start — common ones are start, dev, test, build.

Every dependency is a trust decision. The package might be excellent, abandoned, or compromised. Modern hygiene: check downloads (popular packages are more trustworthy than unknowns); check the last release (active maintenance matters); prefer packages with few transitive dependencies (a tiny package with 200 dependencies is 200 trust decisions, not one); and audit periodically with npm audit. The CSS course taught "fewer dependencies, deeply understood" — the same applies here, doubled.

On the tooling itself: npm ships with Node and is fine. pnpm is faster, uses far less disk space (one global store linked into projects), and has stricter dependency resolution that catches phantom imports. yarn is another modern alternative. Pick one for the team and stick with it; all three read the same package.json.

Why It Matters

Dependencies are how you go from "writing every byte yourself" to "shipping working software next week." But the same mechanism is the single biggest source of operational pain in Node projects — supply- chain attacks, version conflicts, abandoned libraries, "works on my machine" caused by missing lock files. Knowing how to install sensibly, lock deterministically, audit regularly, and remove ruthlessly is a daily discipline.

Real-World Example

A team's CI breaks on Monday morning. Investigation reveals a transitive dependency was republished with a breaking change over the weekend, and because their package-lock.json was not committed, every fresh install gets the new version. Fix: commit the lock file, use npm ci (which respects the lock strictly) in CI, and the breakage stops. The lock file is not optional; it is the contract that makes "Mac, Linux, CI" all run the same code.

Enterprise Example

Enterprise dependency management adds layers. Private registries (Verdaccio, GitHub Packages, Nexus) host the company's own packages and proxy the public registry. Audit gates in CI fail the build on known CVEs. License compliance tooling enforces approved license types. Renovate or Dependabot open automatic PRs for safe updates. The discipline is constant: every dependency is justified, locked, scanned, and updated on a schedule.

Step-by-Step Breakdown

Daily npm flow:

  1. Start a project → npm init -y creates package.json.
  2. Install a runtime dependency → npm install nanoid (adds to dependencies).
  3. Install a dev dependency → npm install --save-dev typescript (adds to devDependencies).
  4. Commit both package.json and package-lock.json to git. The lock file is essential.
  5. In CI, use npm ci (clean install) — respects the lock strictly, fails on mismatch. Faster and more reproducible than npm install.
  6. Run scripts with npm run name (or just npm start for the conventional start script).
  7. Audit regularly → npm audit reports known vulnerabilities; npm outdated shows what is behind.

Code Example

css
package.json — manifest with deps and scripts{
  "name": "pkg-demo",
  "version": "1.0.0",
  "type": "module",
  "scripts": {
    "start": "node index.mjs",
    "say": "node -e \"console.log('via npm script')\""
  },
  "dependencies": {
    "nanoid": "^5.0.0"
  }
}
css
index.mjs — using an installed packageimport { nanoid } from 'nanoid';
console.log('npm package loaded:', typeof nanoid === 'function');
console.log('id:', nanoid(10));
console.log('id:', nanoid(10));
code
terminal$ npm install
$ npm start
> pkg-demo@1.0.0 start
> node index.mjs

npm package loaded: true
id: aeW6XbzQPl
id: tt3rUU-Ais

$ npm run say
via npm script

Notice three things. The import from 'nanoid' — no ./ prefix — is how Node knows to look in node_modules, not relative to the current file. The scripts field lets you give long commands short names — npm start is the convention for "run the app." And npm install writes both node_modules/ (the actual code) and package-lock.json (the resolved versions). Commit the lock, ignore node_modules.

JS & TS Together

Most packages ship TypeScript types — either bundled (the package contains .d.ts declaration files) or via a separate @types/foo package on npm. TypeScript finds them automatically. If a package has no types, write a tiny declarations.d.ts with declare module 'foo'; as a stopgap. We will use typed packages from Module 4 onward.

Common Mistakes

Common Mistake

Not committing the lock file. Without it, every install can pull a slightly different version. Bugs that "appear in CI but not locally" are usually this.

Common Mistake

Installing without justifying. Every new dependency is a trust commitment. For one-liners (UUID, hashing), check whether node: has the built-in first (Chapter 3.2).

Common Mistake

Confusing dependencies and devDependencies. Runtime code goes in dependencies; build/test tools in devDependencies. In production npm install --omit=dev skips dev deps — wrong classification breaks the production install.

Principal Engineer Notes

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

A principal engineer treats every dependency as borrowed code that must be kept up to date and removed when no longer needed. They run npm outdated and npm audit regularly — not as panic responses to incidents but as routine maintenance — and they push back on PRs that add a dependency for a trivial reason.

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

They prefer pnpm in 2026 for new projects — its strict dependency resolution catches "phantom imports" (importing a transitive dependency you did not declare) that npm permits, and its disk efficiency adds up across many projects on a developer's machine. The team chooses one tool and the lock file is committed; switching casually creates churn.

Exercises & Solutions

Practice — Packages & npm
  1. Create a project, install nanoid, write a script that imports it and prints two IDs. Commit package.json and package-lock.json but ignore node_modules.
  2. Add a scripts entry called start that runs your script. Confirm npm start runs it.
  3. Run npm outdated and npm audit in the project. Read what they report — these are the routine maintenance checks.
Solution & What to Check
  1. npm install nanoid writes nanoid under dependencies and creates the lock file. Your script imports from 'nanoid' (no ./).
  2. The scripts.start entry has its command as a string; npm start finds and runs it. Output is the two IDs.
  3. npm outdated lists packages with newer versions available; npm audit reports known vulnerabilities. Both empty is the ideal state.

Architecture Thinking

The npm ecosystem is the largest source of leverage in Node and the largest source of operational risk. The same package that lets you ship a feature in an afternoon could disappear, break, or be compromised. The discipline of fewer dependencies, deeply understood, kept current — the same instinct the CSS course taught for component libraries — keeps the upside without the downside. A small, curated set of trusted dependencies in a well-locked project is a long-lived asset; an unbounded pile of random installs is a liability.

Debugging Tips

  • "Cannot find module 'X'" — either the package is not installed (npm install X), the name is misspelled, or you forgot the ./ for a local file.
  • "Different behaviour in CI" — your lock file is missing, outdated, or not being respected. Use npm ci in CI.
  • "It worked yesterday" — somebody published a new version of a transitive dependency that broke compatibility. Roll back to a known-good lock file commit and investigate.

Performance Considerations

Performance

node_modules can become huge — gigabytes in a large project. This affects install time, CI time, and container image size. pnpm cuts this dramatically with its content-addressed global store. For production containers, multi-stage builds and npm install --omit=dev trim the image; Module 8.3 covers deployment in detail.

Build This — Mini-Project
A Project With a Real Package

Create your first project that installs and uses a published npm package, with a named script to run it. After this you can install anything from the registry by the same flow.

css
package.json{
  "name": "pkg-demo",
  "version": "1.0.0",
  "type": "module",
  "scripts": {
    "start": "node index.mjs"
  },
  "dependencies": {
    "nanoid": "^5.0.0"
  }
}
css

console.log('id:', nanoid(10));
console.log('id:', nanoid(10));

Run:

  1. npm install — downloads nanoid into node_modules, writes package-lock.json.
  2. npm start — runs the start script, which runs node index.mjs.
Expected result (the IDs will be random — but each one different):
code

First installed package, first named script — the loop you will use on every Node project from here on.

HTTP & Building APIs

This is where Node turns from a runtime into a backend. Four chapters: the built-in http module first, so you see what frameworks abstract; then routing and middleware, which every framework formalises; then a modern framework (Hono) for actual production use; and finally validation, error handling, and REST design — the disciplines that keep an API honest.
