Module 4 · Chapter 4.1

The http Module — A Server from Scratch

Before any framework, Node has a built-in HTTP server. Writing one with node:http is twenty lines, and once you have done it the magic of every framework disappears — frameworks are just well-organised wrappers around exactly this.

Lesson 4.1HTTP Without a Framework

Concept Overview

The node:http module exposes createServer(handler), where handler is a function called with a req (incoming request) and res (response writer) for every HTTP transaction. The request gives you req.method, req.url, req.headers, and a stream of the body. The response lets you set res.statusCode, headers via res.setHeader, and write the body with res.write or finish with res.end. The server binds to a port with server.listen(port) and starts handling connections. That is the entire surface; every framework on npm is a nicer API over this.

Plain English Explanation

An HTTP server is a program that listens on a network port for incoming requests, reads each one, and writes a response. Node's createServer hands you the two ends of that conversation: req is the question someone asked you, res is the answer you write back. You read what the request wants (req.method like GET or POST, req.url like /users/42), and you write a response: a status code (200 for success, 404 for not found, 500 for broken), headers (telling the client about your response, like content-type: application/json), and a body (the actual data).

res.end(body) is the one call that must happen for every request — it finalises the response and sends it. Forget it and the client hangs forever waiting for an answer. Most beginner Node servers have one bug pattern: a code path that does not eventually call res.end.

Bodies — the data sent to your server — arrive as a stream of Buffers (Chapter 3.3). For a JSON POST request you collect the chunks, concatenate, and parse. Frameworks do this for you; doing it once by hand makes the abstraction obvious.

Why It Matters

Knowing the bare HTTP API makes everything afterwards make sense. When a framework says "middleware runs before your handler" — that is just a chain of function calls before the framework's req-handler invokes yours. When a library says "we support streaming responses" — that is res.write called multiple times before res.end. The frameworks have not invented anything; they have packaged. Knowing the package's contents removes one layer of magic from every later debugging session.

Real-World Example

A team's health-check endpoint times out occasionally under load. Adding more "framework features" does not help. The principal engineer drops to the raw req/res level: prints req.headers, watches when res.end is called relative to load, and notices the middleware chain is swallowing one out of every thousand requests without finishing. The cause is a forgotten return in an error branch that let the chain fall through and never reach res.end. The fix is one line; finding it required understanding what the framework was wrapping.

Enterprise Example

Some enterprise services skip frameworks entirely for tight performance or surface-control. Cloudflare Workers, Deno, and Bun all converged on the web-standard Request/Response API instead of Node's req/res — Hono (Chapter 4.3) speaks that vocabulary and runs on all of them. But understanding Node's original node:http remains essential because every Node framework still has it underneath, and operations problems (timeouts, TCP-level issues, keep-alive behaviour) live there. The raw API is the debugging floor.

Step-by-Step Breakdown

The shape of every Node HTTP server:

  1. createServer(handler) registers a function called for each incoming request.
  2. The handler reads req.method, req.url, headers; optionally reads the body as a stream.
  3. The handler sets res.statusCode and headers, then writes the body — res.end(body) finalises and sends.
  4. server.listen(port) binds and starts accepting connections. Port 0 means "any free port"; useful in tests.
  5. The server keeps the event loop alive until server.close() is called or the process exits.

Code Example

css
server.mjs — a server in fifteen linesimport { createServer } from 'node:http';

const server = createServer((req, res) => {
  // Each callback is one HTTP transaction.
  res.setHeader('content-type', 'application/json');
  res.statusCode = 200;
  res.end(JSON.stringify({
    method: req.method,
    url: req.url,
    message: 'hello from native http',
  }));
});

server.listen(3000, () => console.log('listening on 3000'));

Run with node server.mjs, then visit http://localhost:3000/anything in a browser or curl http://localhost:3000/anything in another terminal — the response is JSON describing the request. Every Node framework's "hello world" reduces to those fifteen lines underneath.

JS & TS Together

TypeScript types req as IncomingMessage and res as ServerResponse — both well-typed. Bodies-as-streams are typed as AsyncIterable<Buffer> so for await (const chunk of req) works with full types.

Common Mistakes

Common Mistake

Forgetting res.end(). The client hangs. Every code path must end with one res.end.

Common Mistake

Setting headers after res.end. Once the response has been sent, headers cannot change — Node throws "Cannot set headers after they are sent."

Common Mistake

Reading the body as a string. The body is a stream of Buffers. Concatenate Buffers, then convert with .toString('utf8'). Frameworks do this for you.

Principal Engineer Notes

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

A principal engineer can drop to node:http when a framework's abstraction is in the way — debugging timeouts, custom protocols, low-overhead microservices. Knowing the bare API is a capability, not a daily tool.

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

They build for the web-standard shape: Request/Response. Code that consumes web-standard objects (what Hono offers) runs unchanged on Cloudflare Workers, Deno, Bun, and Node. Node-specific req/res locks you in.

Exercises & Solutions

Practice — Native HTTP
  1. Write a server that returns plain text "hello" for GET /, and {"error": "method not allowed"} with status 405 for anything else.
  2. Read a POST body by collecting chunks with for await (const chunk of req). Echo it back as JSON.
  3. Deliberately forget res.end on one code path. Confirm curl hangs. Add the missing call.
Solution & What to Check
  1. if (req.method !== 'GET') { res.statusCode = 405; res.end(JSON.stringify({ error: 'method not allowed' })); } — every branch must call end.
  2. const chunks = []; for await (const c of req) chunks.push(c); const body = Buffer.concat(chunks).toString('utf8'); — standard pattern for collecting a request body.
  3. Without res.end, the client waits forever (or times out). The missing call is the first thing to check on any "request never returns" bug.

Architecture Thinking

req/res is a protocol-level interface — close to the wire, no opinions about routing or middleware. Higher abstractions (Chapter 4.2, 4.3) layer opinion on top. Knowing the bottom lets you evaluate any abstraction's cost: what is the framework doing that you do not need? What is it doing that you do? The bare API is your reference point.

Debugging Tips

  • "Cannot set headers after they are sent" — you called res.setHeader or res.writeHead after res.end or after a previous response. Trace which path returns twice.
  • Hanging requests — almost always a missing res.end. Log at every branch.
  • Use curl -v to see exactly what status, headers, and body the server returned.

Performance Considerations

Performance

Native node:http is the fastest layer Node ships — every framework adds overhead. For most apps the framework's ergonomics are worth a microsecond per request; for very high- throughput services or edge functions, the bare API or a thinly-wrapped one (Hono, the Fetch-based servers) is the right choice. Module 9 covers profiling.

Build This — Mini-Project
A Self-Testing HTTP Server

A native HTTP server that, on startup, fetches itself and prints the response — proving end-to-end that the server is up. This pattern (server + self-check in one script) is the smallest verifiable HTTP build.

css
server.mjsimport { createServer } from 'node:http';

const server = createServer((req, res) => {
  res.setHeader('content-type', 'application/json');
  res.statusCode = 200;
  res.end(JSON.stringify({
    method: req.method,
    url: req.url,
    message: 'hello from native http',
  }));
});

server.listen(0, () => {
  const port = server.address().port;
  console.log('listening on', port);
  fetch(`http://127.0.0.1:${port}/health`)
    .then(r => r.json())
    .then(body => {
      console.log('response:', body);
      server.close();
    });
});
Expected result:
code
listening on 56303
response: { method: 'GET', url: '/health', message: 'hello from native http' }
Server up, request in, response out — fifteen lines, no framework.
Module 4 · Chapter 4.2

Routing, Middleware & Request Handling

A real API has dozens of routes and shared concerns — logging, auth, parsing. Two patterns organise that: routing (matching incoming requests to specific handlers) and middleware (functions that run for every request). Build them once by hand and every framework after looks transparent.

Lesson 4.2Dispatch and Layered Behaviour

Concept Overview

Routing is matching a request's method + URL to a handler — GET /users/:id maps to a function that returns a user. Patterns like :id capture URL params. Middleware is a function that receives the request, does something (log, authenticate, parse), and either responds or calls next() to pass control to the next middleware or the route handler. Middleware is the standard way to add shared concerns without repeating code in every handler.

Plain English Explanation

A route is a (method, URL pattern, handler) triple. When a request comes in, the router walks its list, finds the first triple that matches, extracts URL parameters (the :id in /users/:id becomes params.id), and calls the handler. If nothing matches, the router returns 404.

Middleware is a pipeline. Each middleware function takes (req, res, next) and either ends the response early (e.g., "not authenticated" → 401) or calls next() to continue. Typical middleware: a logger that records every request, an authenticator that attaches the user to the request, a body parser that turns the raw stream into JSON. The route handler runs last, after every middleware has had its say.

This is the abstraction every Node framework provides. Build it once and you see what frameworks are; pick one in the next chapter and you stop building it yourself.

Why It Matters

Without routing, an HTTP server is a giant if chain on req.url. Without middleware, every handler repeats logging, auth, parsing. Both make a small API workable; together they scale to dozens or hundreds of routes. Even when you pick a framework, the mental model is this.

Real-World Example

A team's API needs request IDs for log correlation. Without middleware, every handler generates an ID — repetitive, easily forgotten. With middleware: one function generates the ID, attaches it to req, every handler logs it. Adding a new cross-cutting concern (rate limiting, auth, timing) is one middleware, applied automatically to every route.

Enterprise Example

Enterprise APIs use middleware for: structured logging, request IDs, authentication, authorisation, rate limiting, body parsing, CORS, compression, request timing, response shaping, error handling. Each is a small function; the pipeline composes them. The discipline of "shared concerns become middleware" keeps handlers focused on their actual job.

Step-by-Step Breakdown

  1. Define a routes array: each entry is method, pattern, handler.
  2. Define a middleware array of (req, res, next) functions.
  3. For each incoming request, run middleware in order; each one calls next() to advance.
  4. After middleware, walk routes; find the first matching one; extract URL params; call its handler.
  5. If no route matches, respond 404.
  6. If any middleware responds (without calling next), the route handler is never reached.

Code Example

css
router.mjs — a tiny router + middleware on native httpimport { createServer } from 'node:http';

const routes = [];
const route = (method, pattern, handler) => routes.push({ method, pattern, handler });

const middleware = [];
const use = fn => middleware.push(fn);

use((req, res, next) => { console.log(req.method, req.url); next(); });
use((req, res, next) => { req.id = 'req_' + Math.random().toString(36).slice(2, 8); next(); });

route('GET', '/health', (req, res) => json(res, 200, { status: 'ok', id: req.id }));
route('GET', '/users/:id', (req, res, params) =>
  json(res, 200, { user: { id: params.id, name: 'User ' + params.id } }));

const json = (res, status, body) => {
  res.statusCode = status;
  res.setHeader('content-type', 'application/json');
  res.end(JSON.stringify(body));
};

function match(pattern, url) {
  const keys = [];
  const re = new RegExp('^' + pattern.replace(/:(\w+)/g, (_, k) => (keys.push(k), '([^/]+)')) + '$');
  const m = url.match(re);
  if (!m) return null;
  return Object.fromEntries(keys.map((k, i) => [k, m[i + 1]]));
}

const server = createServer((req, res) => {
  let i = 0;
  const next = () => {
    if (i < middleware.length) return middleware[i++](req, res, next);
    for (const r of routes) {
      if (r.method !== req.method) continue;
      const params = match(r.pattern, req.url);
      if (params) return r.handler(req, res, params);
    }
    json(res, 404, { error: 'not found' });
  };
  next();
});

server.listen(3000);

Eighty lines, full middleware + routing + URL params. Every framework in Chapter 4.3 wraps exactly this pattern with nicer syntax.

JS & TS Together

Typed equivalents annotate the handler signature: (req: IncomingMessage, res: ServerResponse, params: Record<string, string>) => void. Middleware is typed similarly with a next: () => void argument.

Common Mistakes

Common Mistake

Middleware that forgets to call next() or end the response. The chain hangs forever. Every middleware either responds or calls next — never both, never neither.

Common Mistake

Calling next() twice. Runs the rest of the chain twice — usually crashes when something tries to send a second response. return next() from every branch.

Common Mistake

Order-dependent middleware put in the wrong order. Auth must run before route handlers; body parsing before validation. The order of use() calls is the order of execution.

Principal Engineer Notes

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

A principal engineer keeps handlers small and pushes shared concerns into middleware deliberately. A handler that does logging and auth inline is a handler that has not been refactored.

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

They favour narrow middleware — one job each. A single "do everything" middleware is unrescuably tangled; ten small ones compose, test in isolation, and can be reordered.

Exercises & Solutions

Practice — Routing & Middleware
  1. Add a third route, POST /echo, that reads the body and echoes it back as JSON.
  2. Add a middleware that rejects any request without an x-token header with status 401.
  3. Reverse the order of the two middleware. Does anything change? Explain why for these particular middleware, but not always.
Solution & What to Check
  1. Read body with for await (const c of req); concat; JSON.parse; echo. Make sure to handle parse errors.
  2. If !req.headers['x-token'] respond 401 and return. Do not call next — the chain stops.
  3. Logger and request-ID are independent — order does not change visible behaviour. But auth-before-rate-limit vs rate-limit-before-auth would matter: rate limiting should probably run first so unauthenticated abusers are throttled cheaply.

Architecture Thinking

Middleware is a chain-of-responsibility pattern: a request flows through a sequence of handlers, each of which may handle, modify, or pass. Routing is dispatch: matching input to handler. Together they are the architectural shape of every HTTP framework ever built. Once you see the shape, the framework's API is just syntax over it.

Debugging Tips

  • Log at every middleware when debugging a mysterious chain — you can see exactly where it stops.
  • "Cannot set headers after they are sent" in the middle of a chain means two handlers tried to respond. Find the missing return.
  • 404 when you expected a match — typo in the pattern, wrong method, or middleware short-circuited before the router.

Performance Considerations

Performance

Each middleware adds a function call. Ten middleware functions per request is fine; a hundred can show up in profiles. The bigger lever is route matching — a linear walk through routes is fine for tens; for hundreds, use a tree-based router (Hono's is radix-tree-based, fast for any size).

Build This — Mini-Project
A Tiny Router With Middleware

Build the router shown above, run it, and hit it with curl: the health endpoint, a user with a parameter, and a missing path. You will have built — by hand — the abstraction every framework on npm wraps.

code
router.mjs (excerpt — same as Code Example above)(see Code Example for the full ~30 lines)

Run with node router.mjs, then in another terminal:

code
terminal$ curl http://localhost:3000/health
{"status":"ok","id":"req_..."}

$ curl http://localhost:3000/users/42
{"user":{"id":"42","name":"User 42"}}

$ curl http://localhost:3000/nope
{"error":"not found"}
Expected result: three requests, three correct responses, middleware ran on each (logger prints to the server's console). You have rebuilt the core of every Node web framework.
Module 4 · Chapter 4.3

Modern Frameworks (Hono / Express in 2026)

Now that you can build routing and middleware by hand, you stop. A framework gives you the same abstractions production-tested. Two options matter in 2026: Hono (fast, web-standard, runs everywhere) and Express (the long-standing Node standard). The course defaults to Hono; the patterns transfer.

Lesson 4.3Picking the Tool

Concept Overview

Hono is a small, fast framework built on the web-standard Request/Response API. The same Hono app runs on Node, Cloudflare Workers, Deno, and Bun — wherever modern JavaScript runs. It uses chainable routes (app.get('/x', handler)), middleware (app.use(fn)), and a context object c instead of separate req/res. Express is the long-standing Node-specific choice — massive ecosystem, callback-style API, slower but battle-tested. Fastify is a third option, Node-only, with strong schema support. This chapter uses Hono; everything except syntax transfers to Express or Fastify.

Plain English Explanation

Hono's API is what Chapter 4.2 built, polished. Create an app: const app = new Hono(). Add middleware: app.use('*', fn) — runs for every request. Add routes: app.get('/users/:id', handler). The handler takes a single c (context), reads with c.req.param('id') or c.req.json(), writes with c.json({...}) or c.text('...'). That is it.

Hono runs as a Fetch-style server in modern runtimes. On Node, the @hono/node-server package adapts the app to node:http: serve({ fetch: app.fetch, port: 3000 }).

Why It Matters

A framework saves you weeks of building the same pieces — routing, middleware, body parsing, response helpers — and gives you a community to ask when something is wrong. The trade-off is the framework's opinions; Hono's are minimal, modern, and portable, which is why it has become the default for new Node projects in 2026.

Real-World Example

A team starts a new API service. With Hono: one npm install, twenty lines of code, a working JSON server with logging middleware and three endpoints. With native http: an afternoon of writing routing, middleware glue, body parsing. Frameworks let you focus on the business problem, not the HTTP plumbing.

Enterprise Example

Enterprise services build internal frameworks on top of Hono: standardised middleware for auth, logging, tracing; a typed handler registration helper; integration with the company's observability stack. Hono is the substrate; the company's framework on top is the opinionated layer. The same approach works with Express or Fastify; the principle is "pick a small fast base, layer in-house conventions on top."

Step-by-Step Breakdown

  1. npm install hono @hono/node-server.
  2. const app = new Hono().
  3. Add middleware first with app.use('*', fn).
  4. Add routes with app.get/post/put/delete(path, handler).
  5. Start the server with serve({ fetch: app.fetch, port }).
  6. The handler reads from c.req and writes via c.json, c.text, or c.body.

Code Example

css
server.mjs — the router from 4.2, rewritten in Honoimport { Hono } from 'hono';
import { serve } from '@hono/node-server';

const app = new Hono();

app.use('*', async (c, next) => {
  c.set('id', 'req_' + Math.random().toString(36).slice(2, 8));
  console.log(c.req.method, c.req.path);
  await next();
});

app.get('/health', c => c.json({ status: 'ok', id: c.get('id') }));
app.get('/users/:id', c => c.json({
  user: { id: c.req.param('id'), name: 'User ' + c.req.param('id') },
}));

serve({ fetch: app.fetch, port: 3000 });

Same behaviour as Chapter 4.2's hand-rolled router. A third the code; routing, middleware, body handling all provided.

JS & TS Together

Hono is TypeScript-first — the context c has typed params, typed environment, typed variables. c.req.param('id') is typed string; c.get('user') can be typed by declaring an env type. Express has community types via @types/express; Fastify is also TS-first.

Common Mistakes

Common Mistake

Forgetting await next() in async middleware. Without await, the rest of the chain runs in parallel — surprising ordering and broken response handling.

Common Mistake

Picking a heavy framework for a small service. Express loads dozens of dependencies; for a microservice or edge function, Hono's tiny footprint is the right choice.

Common Mistake

Mixing frameworks. Pick one and use it throughout — middleware ecosystems do not cross.

Principal Engineer Notes

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

A principal engineer picks Hono for new projects in 2026 — modern, fast, web-standard, runs everywhere. They reach for Express only when an existing codebase or required middleware demands it.

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

They build a thin in-house layer on top of the framework for cross-cutting concerns — logging, error handling, request shape — so every team's handler looks the same and the framework choice could be swapped later with bounded effort.

Exercises & Solutions

Practice — Hono
  1. Build the Hono server above. Run it and curl the two endpoints.
  2. Add a POST /echo route that returns the JSON body as-is. Use await c.req.json().
  3. Add a timing middleware that records the elapsed time of each request and adds an x-elapsed-ms response header.
Solution & What to Check
  1. Two curls return matching JSON; the server's console shows the logger middleware running.
  2. app.post('/echo', async c => c.json(await c.req.json())) — three words, full round-trip.
  3. app.use('*', async (c, next) => { const start = Date.now(); await next(); c.header('x-elapsed-ms', String(Date.now() - start)); }). Confirm the header is present on the response.

Architecture Thinking

Framework choice is a long-lived decision; switching is expensive. Choose by the architectural fit: web-standard portability, ecosystem size, performance, or feature richness. Hono's design bet — "the web platform's Request/Response is the right abstraction" — is increasingly the industry consensus, which is why it has become the 2026 default.

Debugging Tips

  • 404 from your framework — pattern mismatch or method mismatch. Print c.req.method and c.req.path.
  • Middleware not running — check the path parameter ('*' for all, or a prefix).
  • Response not sent — handler did not return a response, or middleware did not await next().

Performance Considerations

Performance

Hono is among the fastest Node frameworks — a radix-tree router, minimal overhead, web-standard fetch. Express is 3-5x slower for equivalent work. For most apps neither is the bottleneck (the database is); for high-throughput services, the framework matters.

Build This — Mini-Project
A Hono API With Two Endpoints

Install Hono, write the routing example, and run it. After this, Chapter 4.4 layers validation and error handling on top.

css
package.json{
  "name": "hono-demo",
  "type": "module",
  "dependencies": {
    "hono": "^4.6.0",
    "@hono/node-server": "^1.13.0"
  }

code

Expected result: JSON responses from both endpoints, request-ID logging in the server console. Ten lines of app code, production-ready foundation.
Module 4 · Chapter 4.4

Validation, Error Handling & REST Design

The last 20% of an API is what separates "works on a sunny day" from "production-ready": validating every input, handling every error path deliberately, and shaping responses so consumers can rely on them. This chapter ties Chapter 1.5's error patterns to the HTTP layer.

Lesson 4.4Honest APIs

Concept Overview

Validation — checking that incoming data matches what the handler expects — is non-negotiable at the API boundary. Use a schema library (Zod is the 2026 default) that parses, validates, and types in one pass. Error handling builds on Module 1.5: define a small set of custom error classes (ValidationError, NotFoundError, UnauthorizedError) and a central error handler that maps each to its HTTP status. REST design is the convention: nouns in URLs (/users/:id), verbs as methods (GET/POST/PUT/DELETE), standard status codes (200, 201, 400, 401, 403, 404, 500).

Plain English Explanation

Validation: every request body, query parameter, and URL parameter is untrusted until proven otherwise. Zod expresses the expected shape declaratively — z.object({ email: z.string().email(), age: z.number().int().min(13) }) — and .safeParse(body) returns either a typed valid value or a list of issues. No hand-rolled checks.

Error handling: handlers throw typed errors (throw new NotFoundError('user')); a single app.onError handler maps each kind to the right HTTP status. The handler stays focused on its job; the error layer owns HTTP shape. This is Module 1.5's pattern wired into the framework.

REST design: resources are nouns, methods are verbs. GET /users lists, GET /users/:id reads, POST /users creates, PUT /users/:id replaces, PATCH /users/:id partially updates, DELETE /users/:id removes. Status codes have meaning: 201 for "created," 204 for "deleted with no body," 400 for "bad request," 401 for "no auth," 403 for "auth but not allowed," 404 for "missing," 500 for "we broke." Standard conventions; pick them.

Why It Matters

An API that does not validate inputs has security holes and confusing failure modes. An API without central error handling repeats try/catch in every handler and gets it slightly wrong each time. An API that ignores REST conventions makes every client engineer learn its quirks. The three disciplines together are what turn a working endpoint into a trustworthy one.

Real-World Example

A signup endpoint accepts an email, password, and age. Without validation, age might arrive as a string, the email might lack an @, the password might be one character — and the database insert might fail mysteriously. With Zod at the boundary, every request is parsed into a typed valid object or rejected with 400 and a list of issues. The downstream handler is small and confident. Same shape for every endpoint.

Enterprise Example

Enterprise APIs standardise the error response shape across all endpoints: { error: 'code', message: 'human readable', details: [...] }. Validation errors carry the offending field paths; not-found errors carry the missing resource; rate-limit errors carry the retry-after time. Clients (mobile, web, partners) write one error handler and it works for every endpoint. Module 8.2 covers logging and tracing the same way — uniform shape across the system is the win.

Step-by-Step Breakdown

  1. For every endpoint that accepts input, define a Zod schema.
  2. Inside the handler, schema.safeParse(input); if !parsed.success, throw a ValidationError(parsed.error.issues).
  3. For domain errors, throw typed subclasses of Error: NotFoundError, UnauthorizedError, ConflictError.
  4. Register a central app.onError that maps each kind to a status and a uniform JSON shape.
  5. Choose REST status codes deliberately: 201 for created, 204 for deleted, 400 for bad input, 401 for unauthenticated, 403 for forbidden, 404 for missing, 500 for unhandled.
  6. Document the API — OpenAPI / Swagger generated from the same schemas. Module 8 covers documentation.

Code Example

css
server.mjs — validation, typed errors, central handlerimport { Hono } from 'hono';
import { serve } from '@hono/node-server';
import { z } from 'zod';

class ValidationError extends Error {
  constructor(issues) { super('validation'); this.name='ValidationError'; this.issues=issues; }
}
class NotFoundError extends Error {
  constructor(what) { super(what + ' not found'); this.name='NotFoundError'; }
}

const app = new Hono();
const SignupSchema = z.object({
  email: z.string().email(),
  age: z.number().int().min(13),
});

app.post('/signup', async c => {
  const body = await c.req.json().catch(() => ({}));
  const parsed = SignupSchema.safeParse(body);
  if (!parsed.success) throw new ValidationError(parsed.error.issues);
  return c.json({ ok: true, user: parsed.data }, 201);
});

app.get('/users/:id', c => {
  const id = c.req.param('id');
  if (id !== '1') throw new NotFoundError('user');
  return c.json({ id, name: 'Billy' });
});

app.onError((err, c) => {
  if (err instanceof ValidationError) return c.json({ error: 'invalid input', issues: err.issues }, 400);
  if (err instanceof NotFoundError)   return c.json({ error: err.message }, 404);
  console.error('unhandled', err);
  return c.json({ error: 'internal' }, 500);
});

serve({ fetch: app.fetch, port: 3000 });

Handlers stay short — they validate, do their job, throw a typed error if needed. The central handler maps errors to status codes. Adding a new endpoint is the same shape every time.

JS & TS Together

In TypeScript, parsed.data from SignupSchema.safeParse is fully typed — { email: string; age: number } — derived from the schema. z.infer<typeof SignupSchema> gives you that type for reuse elsewhere. Schema is the single source of truth for both runtime validation and compile-time types.

Common Mistakes

Common Mistake

Validating in some endpoints but not others. Every input is untrusted. Validate at every boundary.

Common Mistake

Returning details of internal errors to clients. Stack traces in 500 responses leak information. Log internally; respond opaquely.

Common Mistake

Inconsistent status codes. Pick the right code for each case. 200 means success; 201 means created; 204 means deleted/no content. 400 vs 401 vs 403 vs 404 each mean different things — use them correctly.

Principal Engineer Notes

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

A principal engineer treats the schema as the source of truth for both runtime validation and types. One Zod schema, used as validator at the API boundary and inferred as TypeScript type everywhere downstream.

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

They standardise the error shape across the whole API and document it. Inconsistent error responses force every client to write per-endpoint handling; uniform errors are one error handler for all of them.

Exercises & Solutions

Practice — Validation & Errors
  1. Add a UnauthorizedError class and a middleware that throws it when a required x-token header is missing. Map it to 401 in the error handler.
  2. Add a Zod schema for query parameters on GET /users: limit (number, 1-100, default 20) and cursor (optional string).
  3. Make the error response shape uniform: { error: code, message, ...details } for every error type.
Solution & What to Check
  1. Class + middleware + onError branch. Request without the header returns 401; with it, 200.
  2. z.object({ limit: z.coerce.number().int().min(1).max(100).default(20), cursor: z.string().optional() }). coerce converts the string query param to a number.
  3. Every onError branch returns the same shape; clients can write one error handler.

Architecture Thinking

The API boundary is where untyped, untrusted bytes enter your typed, trusted world. Validation is the gate; types and invariants hold from there inward. Without validation, untyped chaos seeps into your domain. With it, every internal function can assume its inputs are well-formed — a huge simplification.

Debugging Tips

  • Log parsed.error.issues for validation failures — Zod gives precise field paths.
  • Set a breakpoint in app.onError to see every error in one place.
  • Check status codes with curl -i — most "the API responded weirdly" bugs are wrong status codes.

Performance Considerations

Performance

Zod validation is fast — microseconds per parse — and well worth the cost for the safety it brings. For very-high-throughput hot paths, an alternative like valibot (smaller bundle) or AJV (compiled JSON Schema) is faster, but Zod's ergonomics win for most teams.

Module 5 · Chapter 5.0

Setup — Running Real Postgres Locally with Docker

Before Chapter 5.1, you need a Postgres running on your machine. The fastest, cleanest way in 2026 is one docker-compose.yml file and one command. After this chapter you have a Postgres listening on port 5433, you have proven you can connect to it from Node, and every later chapter in Module 5 can be re-run against it by changing one environment variable.

Lesson 5.0Postgres in Docker, in One Command

Concept Overview

Docker runs a Postgres server inside a container — a lightweight isolated process that ships with its own filesystem and dependencies. A docker-compose.yml file declares the services your project needs; docker compose up -d starts them. The Postgres container exposes its port to your host, where your Node code connects as if to any remote database. When you are done, docker compose down stops and removes everything, leaving no host-installed Postgres to maintain. The same setup runs identically on macOS, Linux, and Windows.

Plain English Explanation

You do not install Postgres on your laptop. You run it in a container — a self-contained box that Docker manages. The box has Postgres inside it; you reach Postgres through the box at a port you choose. When you want to throw the database away, you stop the box. When you want a fresh one, you start it again. No system packages, no leftover databases from previous projects, no version conflicts.

You need three things on your laptop: Docker Desktop (or colima on macOS, or docker engine on Linux), the small docker-compose.yml file shown below, and one terminal window to run docker compose up -d. That is the whole story.

The course uses port 5433 for this container — one above Postgres's default 5432 — so it cannot clash with any Postgres you may already have running locally. The DATABASE_URL the rest of the course uses is therefore postgres://course:course@127.0.0.1:5433/course.

Why It Matters

Module 5.1–5.4 (and the SaaS capstone in 10.1) all expect Postgres. The earlier chapters' validated builds use an in-process Postgres-compatible mock (pg-mem) for portability, which is excellent for learning but not the same engine as production. Running real Postgres locally gives you the production engine, the real query planner, and the same SQL dialect your service will face under load. Twenty minutes of setup now means every later chapter can be run against the same Postgres a real production system uses.

It also unlocks Postgres-only features the SQLite-backed mocks cannot demonstrate: RETURNING, ON CONFLICT, JSONB, full-text search, window functions, row-level security. Those features appear in Module 5.3 (migrations), Module 6.1 (sessions), and the capstone in 10.1.

Real-World Example

A team's onboarding doc says: "Install Docker. Clone the repo. Run docker compose up -d. Run npm run dev." That is the modern shape. The new engineer has the whole local environment in under five minutes. The same compose file in CI starts the same Postgres for the test suite. No "Postgres installer version mismatch" tickets, ever.

Enterprise Example

Enterprise teams almost universally use docker compose (or devcontainer.json for codespace-style environments) for local dependencies — Postgres, Redis, S3-compatible storage (MinIO), Kafka, anything stateful. The compose file is committed; CI pipelines stand up the same stack; staging and production use the same images via Kubernetes. The same database engine runs in every environment, which is what makes "it works in dev" mean something.

Step-by-Step Approach

  1. Install Docker. Docker Desktop on macOS/Windows from docker.com; on Linux, docker engine via your package manager. Confirm with docker --version.
  2. Create the project folder for this chapter and save the docker-compose.yml below into it.
  3. Start Postgres in the background: docker compose up -d. Docker pulls the Postgres image (first run only, ~80 MB) and starts the container.
  4. Verify it is running: docker compose ps should show real-course-postgres with status Up.
  5. Test the connection with the db.mjs script below, exporting DATABASE_URL first.
  6. When done for the day: docker compose stop (keeps data) or docker compose down -v (deletes data too). The container is gone; nothing lingers on the host.

Code Example

code
docker-compose.ymlservices:
  postgres:
    image: postgres:17-alpine
    container_name: real-course-postgres
    environment:
      POSTGRES_USER: course
      POSTGRES_PASSWORD: course
      POSTGRES_DB: course
    ports:
      - "5433:5432"   # 5433 host -> 5432 container; avoids clashing with any local pg
    healthcheck:
      test: ["CMD-SHELL", "pg_isready -U course -d course"]
      interval: 1s
      timeout: 1s
      retries: 10
code
db.mjs — connect, query, prove it is real Postgresimport pg from 'pg';

const pool = new pg.Pool({
  connectionString: process.env.DATABASE_URL,
  max: 10,
});

await pool.query(`DROP TABLE IF EXISTS users`);
await pool.query(`
  CREATE TABLE users (
    id          SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    email       TEXT NOT NULL UNIQUE,
    created_at  TIMESTAMPTZ NOT NULL DEFAULT NOW()
  )
`);

const inserted = await pool.query(
  'INSERT INTO users (email) VALUES ($1) RETURNING id, email, created_at',
  ['billy@example.com'],
);
console.log('inserted:', inserted.rows[0]);

// Postgres-specific: RETURNING + ON CONFLICT — features you cannot get from SQLite.
const upsert = await pool.query(`
  INSERT INTO users (email) VALUES ($1)
  ON CONFLICT (email) DO UPDATE SET email = EXCLUDED.email
  RETURNING id, email
`, ['billy@example.com']);
console.log('upserted (same id):', upsert.rows[0]);

const version = await pool.query('SELECT version()');
console.log('engine:', version.rows[0].version.slice(0, 25) + '...');

await pool.end();

JS & TS Together

The pg driver ships its own types; in TypeScript you would write const inserted = await pool.query<{ id: number; email: string }>(...) to type the row. Chapter 5.1 covers this in detail.

Common Mistakes

Common Mistake

Forgetting the -d on docker compose up. Without it, compose runs in the foreground and your terminal is taken hostage. -d runs detached.

Common Mistake

Connecting to localhost port 5432 instead of 5433. Our compose file maps to host port 5433 so it never collides with a system-installed Postgres. The DATABASE_URL must use 5433.

Common Mistake

Running docker compose down -v thinking it just stops things. The -v flag deletes the volume — every row in your database. down alone keeps the data between sessions; use -v only when you mean to reset.

Common Mistake

Forgetting Docker is not running. If docker compose up errors with "Cannot connect to the Docker daemon," start Docker Desktop (or colima start) first.

Principal Engineer Notes

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

The compose file lives in version control. Local Postgres versions match production within a major version. The team agrees on one image tag (postgres:17-alpine here, not just postgres) so everyone runs the same engine. Floating tags ("latest", or just "postgres") are how "the upgrade broke local dev" tickets are born.

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

They also use the same compose file in CI. A GitHub Actions workflow that runs docker compose up -d postgres before npm test gets the same database engine every developer has. Skipping this and using a different in-memory mock in tests is what produces the "passes in CI, fails in prod" pattern.

Exercises & Solutions

Practice — Stand up Real Postgres
  1. Save the docker-compose.yml above into a new folder. Run docker compose up -d; confirm docker compose ps shows the container as Up (healthy).
  2. Run npm install and create the db.mjs shown. Export DATABASE_URL=postgres://course:course@127.0.0.1:5433/course and run node db.mjs.
  3. Open a Postgres shell directly to inspect the row: docker compose exec postgres psql -U course -d course -c "SELECT * FROM users".
  4. Run docker compose down, then docker compose up -d again. Re-run db.mjs. The row is still there — the volume persisted.
  5. Run docker compose down -v to wipe the volume; verify the table is gone on next up.
Solution & What to Check
  1. docker compose ps shows real-course-postgres with status Up. If "unhealthy", the healthcheck in the compose file is failing — usually means Postgres has not finished starting; wait 5–10s.
  2. The script prints inserted: { id: 1, email: ... }, upserted (same id): { id: 1, ... }, and engine: PostgreSQL 17.... The engine line is the proof: this is real Postgres, not pg-mem.
  3. psql from inside the container is the same database your Node code is hitting. This is the production debugging tool you will use forever.
  4. Persistence-by-default is the docker compose down behaviour; -v is the destructive variant. Most workflows use plain down at end of day.
  5. A clean wipe with -v is how you reset to a known-good state when migrations get into a tangle during development.

Architecture Thinking

The compose-file-as-dependency-spec pattern is the same shape as package.json for Node libraries. One declarative file lists everything the project needs; one command stands it up; one command tears it down. As projects grow, the same file scales to add Redis, MinIO for object storage, Mailpit for SMTP — the same shape, more services. In production, the compose file is usually replaced by Kubernetes manifests or a managed database service, but the abstraction is identical: declare what you need, the platform provides it.

Debugging Tips

  • Container won't start: docker compose logs postgres shows the startup log. Usually a port conflict (another Postgres on 5433) or a corrupted volume from a prior crash.
  • Connection refused from Node: the healthcheck may still be running. Wait a few seconds, or check docker compose ps for (healthy).
  • Slow queries during development: docker compose exec postgres psql -U course -d course -c "EXPLAIN ANALYZE SELECT ..." shows the real query plan against real data.
  • Out of disk: docker system prune -a --volumes reclaims everything Docker is holding. Use carefully.

Performance Considerations

Performance

On macOS, Docker runs inside a Linux VM, so file I/O between host and container is slower than native — fine for development databases, noticeable for very-large volume mounts. The Postgres container's own storage is isolated in a Docker volume (not a host bind-mount), so this cost does not apply to query performance. Local Postgres in this setup serves thousands of queries per second comfortably — more than enough for any development workload in the rest of the course.

A Local Postgres, Connected and Queried

A note on validation honesty

Unlike the other "Build This" projects in this course, this chapter's build cannot be validated in our automated harness — the harness does not have access to a Docker daemon. The validated builds in chapters 5.1–5.4 run against an in-process Postgres-compatible mock (pg-mem) so they execute everywhere. The build above runs against real Postgres on your machine, and the expected output is what real Postgres 17 actually prints. If yours matches, your setup works.

Module 5 · Chapter 5.1

Connecting to Postgres

Postgres is the default database for Node backends in 2026 — open source, rock-solid, feature-rich. The pg driver is the canonical way to talk to it. Connect, query, parameterise — this chapter is the foundation everything later in the module sits on.

Lesson 5.1Talking to Postgres

Concept Overview

The pg package is Node's standard Postgres client. You configure a Pool with a connection string and call pool.query(sql, params) for each query. The pool manages multiple TCP connections to the database, reusing them across requests. Parameterised queries — passing values as the second argument, never interpolated into the SQL string — are non-negotiable: they prevent SQL injection and let Postgres cache the query plan. Results come back as { rows, rowCount, fields }; rows is an array of objects keyed by column name.

Plain English Explanation

Connecting to Postgres has three pieces. A connection string — usually postgres://user:password@host:port/database — tells the driver where the database is. A pool is a small set of pre-opened connections kept ready (typically 10-20); opening a fresh TCP connection per query would be too slow. A query is SQL plus parameters — and the parameters always go through the driver's safe substitution, never into the string.

The parameterised-query rule is the most important security rule in backend development. Wrong: `SELECT * FROM users WHERE email = '${email}'` — if email is "' OR '1'='1" the query returns every user. Right: 'SELECT * FROM users WHERE email = $1', [email] — the driver sends the SQL and the value separately; Postgres treats the value as data, never as code. Every database library in 2026 supports this; use it always.

On configuration: store the connection string in process.env.DATABASE_URL (twelve-factor app), never in code. The same code runs against a local dev database, a staging database, and production by changing one env var.

Why It Matters

Talking to a database is what most backend code spends its time doing. Getting the basics right — pool sizing, parameterised queries, error handling — is the difference between a fast, secure service and a slow, vulnerable one. Beginners frequently make two mistakes that ruin services: opening a connection per query (slow), and concatenating SQL strings (insecure). Avoid both from day one.

Real-World Example

A handler needs to load a user by email. The right shape is six lines: const { rows } = await pool.query('SELECT id, email, created_at FROM users WHERE email = $1', [email]); if (rows.length === 0) throw new NotFoundError('user'); const user = rows[0];. Parameterised, single round-trip, typed columns. The Module 4 typed- error handler maps the NotFoundError to a 404. The handler is short and correct.

Enterprise Example

Enterprise services typically wrap the pool in a small data-access layer: one module exports the pool, route handlers import typed repository functions (findUserByEmail, not raw SQL). The repository module is where SQL lives and where it gets tested. Connection strings come from a managed secret store, not env files. TLS is required for production connections. Read replicas serve heavy SELECTs while the primary handles writes. These layers add up but the core remains the same: a pool, parameterised queries, results back as rows.

Step-by-Step Breakdown

  1. npm install pg; for TypeScript add npm install --save-dev @types/pg.
  2. Read DATABASE_URL from the environment.
  3. Create one Pool per service: const pool = new Pool({ connectionString: process.env.DATABASE_URL, max: 20 }).
  4. Use pool.query(sql, params) for individual queries.
  5. For multi-query transactions, check out a client with pool.connect() (Chapter 5.4).
  6. On graceful shutdown, call pool.end() to close connections cleanly.

Code Example

code
db.mjs — production connection setupimport pg from 'pg';

export const pool = new pg.Pool({
  connectionString: process.env.DATABASE_URL,
  max: 20,            // upper bound on concurrent connections
  idleTimeoutMillis: 30000,
  connectionTimeoutMillis: 2000,
});
css
index.mjs — using the poolimport { pool } from './db.mjs';

await pool.query(`
  CREATE TABLE users (
    id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    email TEXT NOT NULL UNIQUE,
    created_at TIMESTAMPTZ NOT NULL DEFAULT NOW()
  )
`);

// PARAMETERISED — the email value goes via the params array, never the string.
const inserted = await pool.query(
  'INSERT INTO users (email) VALUES ($1) RETURNING id, email',
  ['billy@example.com'],
);
console.log('inserted:', inserted.rows[0]);

const lookup = await pool.query('SELECT id, email FROM users WHERE email = $1', ['billy@example.com']);
console.log('found:', lookup.rows[0]);
console.log('rowCount:', lookup.rowCount);
await pool.end();

JS & TS Together

TypeScript lets you type the row shape with a generic: const { rows } = await pool.query<User>('SELECT ... '). Even better, derive the type from your schema (Chapter 5.2 — Drizzle generates row types automatically from table definitions).

Common Mistakes

Common Mistake

String concatenation in SQL. `WHERE email = '${email}'` is a SQL injection waiting to happen. Always parameterise.

Common Mistake

Opening a Pool per request. One Pool per service, created at startup, used everywhere.

Common Mistake

Not closing connections during graceful shutdown. On SIGTERM, call pool.end() so in-flight queries finish.

Principal Engineer Notes

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

A principal engineer treats SQL parameterisation as muscle memory. Concatenation in a query is a code-review red flag every time.

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

They centralise SQL behind small repository functions — a route handler calls findUserByEmail(email), not raw SQL. SQL lives where it can be tested and audited.

Exercises & Solutions

Practice — Postgres
  1. Create the users table, insert two users, fetch them back ordered by email. Use the param array for each query.
  2. Try a string-concatenated query and a parameterised query against an input like "' OR 1=1 --". Confirm the concatenated version returns all users.
  3. Wrap the pool in a findUserByEmail(email) function and call it from a Hono handler.
Solution & What to Check
  1. Two INSERT INTO users (email) VALUES ($1) calls with different emails; one SELECT ... ORDER BY email returns both rows.
  2. The concatenated version is a security disaster; the parameterised one returns nothing (the literal string is not a valid email).
  3. One module exports findUserByEmail; the route handler imports it, calls it, returns the result. SQL is centralised.

Architecture Thinking

The pool is your service's contract with the database: how many connections, how long they idle, how queries are dispatched. Treating it as an architectural concern, not boilerplate, is what keeps a service stable under load.

Debugging Tips

  • "connection refused" — Postgres is not running, or the host/port is wrong. psql with the same URL confirms.
  • "too many connections" — your pool max is too high, or you have many service replicas. Check the database's max_connections.
  • Slow queries — log query timing (const start = Date.now(); log on slow); use EXPLAIN ANALYZE in psql.

Performance Considerations

Performance

Pool size matters: too small and queries queue; too large and the database struggles. Start at 20 per service replica, tune from observed wait times. Indexes are the other lever — Chapter 5.4 covers query performance.

Module 5 · Chapter 5.2

Query Builders & ORMs — Drizzle, Prisma

Raw SQL is fine for small projects. As tables and queries multiply, a query builder or ORM saves repetition, types your results, and refactors safely. The 2026 default is Drizzle — lightweight, typed, SQL-like. Prisma is the heavier alternative with a full schema language. Either is dramatically better than hand-rolling SQL in dozens of repositories.

Lesson 5.2Typed Query Layers

Concept Overview

A query builder exposes the database's verbs as chainable JS calls: db.select().from(users).where(eq(users.email, 'x')). The shape is SQL-like; the output is fully typed from a schema you define in TypeScript. An ORM goes further — modelling tables as classes, relations as nested objects, sometimes generating a migration language of its own. Drizzle is a builder (lightweight, generates SQL); Prisma is an ORM (heavier, owns more of the stack). Both produce typed results derived from your schema, which is the big win over raw SQL: change a column name and the compiler shows every place that breaks.

Plain English Explanation

Raw SQL has two problems at scale. First, it is untyped — a query returns any rows, and the compiler cannot help when a column name changes. Second, common patterns (insert().returning(), joins, conditional WHEREs) get verbose. A query builder solves both: you declare your tables once as TypeScript objects, and from then on every query is typed.

Drizzle is a small library that maps closely to SQL. You write db.select().from(users).where(eq(users.email, x)); Drizzle generates the SQL, runs it, returns rows typed by your schema. Prisma is more opinionated — you write schema in its own .prisma language, run prisma generate to produce a typed client, and call prisma.user.findUnique({ where: { email: x } }). Both are dramatically better than raw SQL once you have more than a handful of queries.

The course defaults to Drizzle because it stays close to SQL and adds no extra build step — you write TS, you get a typed query layer, you can drop to raw SQL when needed. Prisma is worth knowing for teams that prefer its ergonomics.

Why It Matters

Once you have twenty tables and a hundred queries, the difference between typed and untyped is the difference between safe refactors and weeks of grep. ORMs and query builders pay back their learning cost within a small project.

Real-World Example

A team renames the column email to email_address in the users table. With raw SQL: grep the codebase for 'email', find every query, hope you got them all, deploy, watch production for "column email does not exist." With Drizzle: change the column name in one schema file; the compiler shows every query that referenced it; fix them all; ship with confidence. Same change, two completely different stakes.

Enterprise Example

Enterprise database layers combine: Drizzle for typed queries, a small repository module per aggregate (users.repository.ts), Zod schemas at the API boundary validating into the same shapes Drizzle returns. The result is a type-safe path from HTTP request to database row and back — refactor anywhere and the compiler flags everywhere else.

Step-by-Step Breakdown

  1. npm install drizzle-orm plus the driver (pg for Postgres, better-sqlite3 for SQLite).
  2. Declare tables as TypeScript objects using pgTable() or sqliteTable(): column name, type, constraints.
  3. Create the Drizzle instance: const db = drizzle(pool).
  4. Write queries: db.select().from(users).where(eq(users.email, x)) or db.insert(users).values({...}).returning().
  5. Use the row types: InferSelectModel<typeof users> gives you the row shape for use in handlers.

Code Example

code
db.mjs — schema + Drizzle instanceimport Database from 'better-sqlite3';
import { drizzle } from 'drizzle-orm/better-sqlite3';
import { sqliteTable, integer, text } from 'drizzle-orm/sqlite-core';
import { eq } from 'drizzle-orm';

const sqlite = new Database(':memory:');
sqlite.exec(`
  CREATE TABLE users (
    id INTEGER PRIMARY KEY AUTOINCREMENT,
    email TEXT NOT NULL UNIQUE,
    created_at TEXT NOT NULL DEFAULT (datetime('now'))
  )
`);

export const users = sqliteTable('users', {
  id: integer('id').primaryKey({ autoIncrement: true }),
  email: text('email').notNull().unique(),
  createdAt: text('created_at').notNull(),
});
export const db = drizzle(sqlite);
export { eq };
css
index.mjs — typed insert + selectimport { db, users, eq } from './db.mjs';

const [inserted] = await db.insert(users).values({
  email: 'billy@example.com',
  createdAt: new Date().toISOString(),
}).returning();
console.log('inserted:', { id: inserted.id, email: inserted.email });

const found = await db.select().from(users).where(eq(users.email, 'billy@example.com'));
console.log('found:', { id: found[0].id, email: found[0].email });
console.log('count:', found.length);

Same operations as Chapter 5.1, with two big improvements: types flow from schema to results, and queries are method chains instead of strings. Drizzle generates the SQL underneath; you can log it for debugging.

JS & TS Together

Drizzle is TypeScript-first. InferSelectModel<typeof users> is the row type; InferInsertModel<typeof users> is the insert shape. Export those alongside the table for use in handlers and validators.

Common Mistakes

Common Mistake

Bypassing the builder for "one quick raw query." Becomes the source of all bugs at refactor time. Use builder for everything; sql\`...\` template for the rare cases where you need raw SQL — still parameterised.

Common Mistake

N+1 queries. Loading parent rows and querying children one at a time in a loop. Use a JOIN or a batched IN clause.

Common Mistake

Over-using ORM relations. Eager-loading everything pulls more data than handlers need. Select explicit columns.

Principal Engineer Notes

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

A principal engineer treats the schema file as the contract. Add columns there first; let the compiler show every query that depends. Never edit production schemas through ad-hoc SQL.

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

They keep queries close to SQL. Drizzle's SQL-like shape is the right level — abstract enough to type, concrete enough to predict the generated query and its performance.

Exercises & Solutions

Practice — Drizzle
  1. Add a posts table with a foreign key to users. Insert a post and select all posts by user.
  2. Add an index on posts.user_id in the schema. Confirm Drizzle generates it.
  3. Write the same query with raw SQL via Drizzle's sql\`...\` tag — confirm it is still parameterised.
Solution & What to Check
  1. Define posts with userId: integer().references(() => users.id). Select with db.select().from(posts).where(eq(posts.userId, userId)).
  2. Drizzle has index('idx_posts_user_id').on(posts.userId) in its schema DSL. The generated SQL includes CREATE INDEX.
  3. sql\`SELECT * FROM posts WHERE user_id = ${userId}\` — the template tag parameterises automatically.

Architecture Thinking

A typed query layer is one of the highest-leverage productivity investments in a backend. The schema is the single source of truth; types propagate to every handler, validator, and test. The result is a system where renaming a column is a one-file edit followed by a compile.

Debugging Tips

  • Log generated SQL. Drizzle's logger: true option prints every query.
  • Type errors at query sites are usually the most actionable — column missing, wrong type, missing required.
  • Slow queries — explain the generated SQL with EXPLAIN ANALYZE; add indexes; verify in Drizzle's output.

Performance Considerations

Performance

Drizzle has near-zero overhead vs raw SQL — it generates SQL, sends it, parses results. The expensive part is always the query itself; chapter 5.4 covers indexing and query performance.

Module 5 · Chapter 5.3

Migrations — Versioned Schema Change

The term you originally asked about. A migration is a small, versioned script that changes the database schema in a tracked, repeatable way. Each one applies once, in order, and the database remembers which have already been applied. This is how schemas evolve safely across every developer's machine and every production deploy.

Lesson 5.3Schema as Tracked, Reversible Code

Concept Overview

A migration is a numbered file (or SQL script) describing one schema change — create a table, add a column, add an index. A migration runner applies pending migrations in order and records which have run in a migrations table inside the database itself. Up migrations apply the change; down migrations reverse it. Migrations are committed to git, run in CI against test databases, and applied to production as part of deploy. Modern tooling (Drizzle Kit, Prisma Migrate, Alembic for Python) automates the generation; the principle is universal.

Plain English Explanation

Imagine a database that started with two tables. Over six months, engineers add a column, create a new table, add an index, rename a field. How does production stay in sync with the code? Migrations. Each schema change is a small script — "add this column," "create this table" — saved as a file with a number prefix (001_create_users.sql, 002_add_user_email_verified.sql). The runner reads every file in order, checks the migrations table to see which have already been applied here, and applies the new ones. Same scripts, same order, every database — local dev, staging, production — ends up at the same schema.

The discipline has three rules. Once applied, never edit. Migration 003 was applied to production; editing it means production has version 003 but staging has 003-fixed. Add a new migration instead. Every migration has an up and a down. The up applies the change; the down undoes it. Even if you never roll back in production, the down is useful for development and CI. Migrations run before code that depends on them. Deploy order: apply migrations, then deploy the new code that uses them — never the other way around.

Tooling: Drizzle Kit generates SQL migrations from schema changes (drizzle-kit generate); Prisma Migrate has its own format. For a tiny project a hand-rolled runner (we will build one below) is fine; for anything serious, use a tested tool. The shape is the same in all of them.

Why It Matters

Without migrations, schema changes are an oral tradition — somebody runs "I think we need to ALTER" in production and hopes. With migrations, every change is a reviewed file in git, applied identically everywhere. The difference is the difference between "production schema drifts mysteriously" and "schema is code."

Real-World Example

A team needs to add an email_verified column to users. The engineer creates 002_add_user_email_verified.sql with ALTER TABLE users ADD COLUMN email_verified BOOLEAN NOT NULL DEFAULT FALSE. They commit it. CI applies it against a test database before running tests; staging applies it on next deploy; production applies it on production deploy. Every database, same order. The code that reads the new column ships in the same deploy, after the migration step.

Enterprise Example

Enterprise schemas evolve constantly across hundreds of migrations. Discipline: migrations are append-only (you do not edit a shipped one); each is small and focused (one logical change per file); each has a down for reversibility. Zero-downtime deploys require forward-compatible migrations: add the column as nullable, deploy code that reads-but-does-not- require it, backfill in a separate migration, then a final migration makes it required. Three migrations across three deploys — slower than one big change, but never breaks production.

Step-by-Step Breakdown

  1. Create the migration file with a numbered prefix: migrations/003_add_post_slug.sql.
  2. Write the up: the SQL that applies the change.
  3. Write the down (optional but recommended): the SQL that reverses it.
  4. Run the migration runner: it checks the migrations table, applies the new file, and records it as applied.
  5. Commit the migration file alongside the code that uses the new schema.
  6. In CI and production deploy, run the migration command before starting the new service version.
  7. Never edit an applied migration; add a new one instead.

Code Example

code
migrations/001_create_users.sqlCREATE TABLE users (
  id INTEGER PRIMARY KEY AUTOINCREMENT,
  email TEXT NOT NULL UNIQUE
);
code
migrations/002_add_user_email_verified.sqlALTER TABLE users ADD COLUMN email_verified INTEGER NOT NULL DEFAULT 0;
code
migrate.mjs — a tiny runner (production: use a tested tool)import Database from 'better-sqlite3';
import { readdirSync, readFileSync } from 'node:fs';
import { join } from 'node:path';

const db = new Database(':memory:');
db.exec(`CREATE TABLE IF NOT EXISTS migrations (
  name TEXT PRIMARY KEY,
  applied_at TEXT NOT NULL
)`);

const files = readdirSync('./migrations').filter(f => f.endsWith('.sql')).sort();
const applied = new Set(db.prepare('SELECT name FROM migrations').all().map(r => r.name));

for (const file of files) {
  if (applied.has(file)) { console.log('skip:', file); continue; }
  const sql = readFileSync(join('./migrations', file), 'utf8');
  db.exec(sql);
  db.prepare('INSERT INTO migrations (name, applied_at) VALUES (?, ?)')
    .run(file, new Date().toISOString());
  console.log('applied:', file);
}

const cols = db.prepare("PRAGMA table_info(users)").all().map(c => c.name);
console.log('columns:', cols);

Running it the first time applies both migrations; running it again prints "skip" for each. The schema is now at version 2. Add a third file and rerun — only 003 applies.

JS & TS Together

Drizzle Kit's CLI generates and runs typed migrations: drizzle-kit generate diffs your schema file against the previous version and emits SQL; drizzle-kit migrate applies pending migrations. The shape is identical to the manual runner above; the tool just automates generation and adds safety checks.

Common Mistakes

Common Mistake

Editing an applied migration. Production has the old version; your edited file is a different one. Add a new migration that does the correction.

Common Mistake

Big breaking migrations. Renaming a column in one migration breaks the running code. Split: add new column, copy data, deploy code reading both, deploy code reading only new, drop old column.

Common Mistake

Hand-edited prod schemas. An engineer runs ALTER directly in production; the migration history no longer matches the schema. Reapplying migrations from scratch on a clean DB now fails.

Principal Engineer Notes

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

A principal engineer treats migrations as a one-way ledger. Append-only, reviewed in PRs, tested in CI. Production schema changes happen only through migrations, never manually.

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

They write forward-compatible migrations for zero-downtime deploys. Three small migrations across three deploys are far safer than one big migration with downtime.

Exercises & Solutions

Practice — Migrations
  1. Create three migration files: create a posts table, add a slug column, add an index on slug. Run the runner.
  2. Run the runner again. Confirm it skips all three.
  3. Try to rename a column safely in two migrations: add the new name, copy data, leave the old column. (A real third migration would drop the old column after the code is updated.)
Solution & What to Check
  1. Three SQL files; the runner applies each once; PRAGMA table_info(posts) shows all three changes.
  2. Second run prints "skip" for all three; the migrations table is the source of truth.
  3. Migration 1: ALTER TABLE users ADD COLUMN email_address TEXT. Migration 2: UPDATE users SET email_address = email. Both columns coexist; safe deploy.

Architecture Thinking

Migrations are schema as code. The same principle that keeps application code under version control — reviewed, tested, deployed deterministically — applied to the database. Schema drift between environments is impossible when the schema is the result of applying a known list of migrations.

Debugging Tips

  • "migration already applied" but the change is not there — the migrations table is out of sync. Mark the migration as not-applied and rerun (carefully).
  • Migration fails halfway — fix the cause, undo the partial change manually, mark the migration as not applied, rerun.
  • CI fails on a migration — usually a syntax error or a dependency on previous data. Test migrations against a fresh empty database in CI.

Performance Considerations

Performance

Long migrations on large tables can lock the table for the duration. Postgres-specific techniques: CREATE INDEX CONCURRENTLY (no lock), batched data updates, online schema change tools (pg_repack). For zero-downtime, decompose into many small migrations rather than one big one.

Build This — Mini-Project
A Migration Runner From Scratch

Build the two SQL migrations and the runner. Apply them; confirm the schema; rerun and watch the skip behaviour. You have built — in 25 lines — the core of every migration tool.

code
migrations/001_create_users.sql + 002_add_user_email_verified.sql(see Code Example)
code
migrate.mjs(see Code Example)
Expected result (first run):
code
applied: 001_create_users.sql
applied: 002_add_user_email_verified.sql
columns: [ 'id', 'email', 'email_verified' ]
Second run:
code
skip: 001_create_users.sql
skip: 002_add_user_email_verified.sql
columns: [ 'id', 'email', 'email_verified' ]
Migrations applied exactly once, idempotent, recorded in the database. Schema as code.
Module 5 · Chapter 5.4

Transactions, Pools & Query Performance

A transaction groups several queries so they succeed or fail together. A pool keeps a small set of connections ready. Indexes turn a slow query into a fast one. These three are the production database disciplines every backend engineer must hold.

Lesson 5.4Atomicity, Concurrency, Speed

Concept Overview

A transaction wraps multiple queries in BEGIN ... COMMIT so they apply atomically — either all succeed or all roll back. Use them whenever a single business operation requires multiple writes (transfer money, create order with line items). A connection pool keeps a small number of open connections to the database, reused across requests — Chapter 5.1 introduced it; this chapter tunes it. An index is a separate data structure that speeds certain queries; the most-used index is on the primary key (automatic) and on every foreign key. Use EXPLAIN ANALYZE to see whether a query uses an index or scans the whole table.

Plain English Explanation

Transactions: when one user action requires two database writes, you need both to happen or neither. Money transfer: debit Alice, credit Bob — if the second write fails, you must undo the first or you have lost money. A transaction is the database's promise that you can do that: BEGIN, run your queries, COMMIT on success, ROLLBACK (automatic on error) on failure.

Pools: every database connection is a network socket plus a server-side process. Opening one per query would be painfully slow. A pool keeps, say, 20 connections open and hands one out per query. The pool size is one of the most important production tuning knobs: too small and queries queue waiting for a connection; too large and the database server's connection limit breaks. Typical: 20 per service replica; tune from observed waits.

Indexes: without an index, finding a user by email scans every row in the users table — fast for ten users, slow for ten million. An index on email is a sorted lookup structure that makes the same query log-time. Always index: primary keys (automatic), foreign keys, columns you filter on. EXPLAIN ANALYZE in Postgres shows the query plan — "Index Scan" is good, "Seq Scan" on a big table is a problem.

Why It Matters

Transactions are how you keep data consistent under partial-failure. Pools are how you handle traffic without overwhelming the database. Indexes are how queries stay fast as data grows. Together they are the difference between a service that survives production load and one that does not.

Real-World Example

A signup handler creates a user, a default workspace, and a membership row. Three inserts. Without a transaction, a crash between the second and third leaves an orphan workspace. With a transaction, the three writes are atomic. Drizzle exposes this as db.transaction(async tx => { await tx.insert(...); await tx.insert(...); ... }); pg uses pool.connect() and explicit BEGIN/COMMIT.

Enterprise Example

Enterprise patterns add layers. Long-running transactions are bad — they hold locks; keep transactions short. Repeatable-read isolation prevents some classes of race conditions. SELECT FOR UPDATE locks rows you intend to update. Read replicas serve SELECTs that do not need the latest write; the primary handles all writes. Index design is iterative: add what slow queries need, remove what is unused (indexes cost write performance).

Step-by-Step Breakdown

  1. Wrap multi-write operations in a transaction. Either it all happens, or none of it does.
  2. Tune pool size against the database's connection limit and your service replica count.
  3. Index every column you filter or join on. Foreign keys, status columns, lookup columns.
  4. Use EXPLAIN ANALYZE on slow queries; look for Seq Scans on large tables.
  5. Limit returned rows. Always cap with LIMIT; never let an endpoint return a million rows.
  6. Watch p99 query latency in production. Spikes are usually missing indexes or lock contention.

Code Example

js
tx.mjs — atomic money transfer with rollbackimport Database from 'better-sqlite3';

const db = new Database(':memory:');
db.exec(`
  CREATE TABLE accounts (id INTEGER PRIMARY KEY, name TEXT, balance INTEGER NOT NULL);
  INSERT INTO accounts (name, balance) VALUES ('Alice', 100), ('Bob', 50);
`);

// db.transaction wraps the function in BEGIN/COMMIT/ROLLBACK.
const transfer = db.transaction((from, to, amount) => {
  const fromAcc = db.prepare('SELECT balance FROM accounts WHERE id = ?').get(from);
  if (fromAcc.balance < amount) throw new Error('insufficient funds');
  db.prepare('UPDATE accounts SET balance = balance - ? WHERE id = ?').run(amount, from);
  db.prepare('UPDATE accounts SET balance = balance + ? WHERE id = ?').run(amount, to);
});

// 1. Successful transfer
transfer(1, 2, 30);
console.log('after ok:', db.prepare('SELECT name, balance FROM accounts ORDER BY id').all());

// 2. Failed transfer — both updates roll back together
try {
  transfer(1, 2, 9999);
} catch (e) {
  console.log('rolled back:', e.message);
}
console.log('after fail:', db.prepare('SELECT name, balance FROM accounts ORDER BY id').all());

Read the output: after the failed transfer, balances are unchanged — the BEGIN/UPDATE/UPDATE was rolled back as one unit. The balance check inside the transaction means the funds-insufficient case throws, which the transaction handler turns into a ROLLBACK.

JS & TS Together

Drizzle's db.transaction(async tx => ...) takes a typed function — every query inside uses tx, not the outer db. TypeScript ensures the right one is used. Errors inside the callback automatically roll back.

Common Mistakes

Common Mistake

Long-running transactions. A transaction holding a row lock for 30 seconds blocks every other writer. Keep transactions short; do slow work (HTTP calls, file I/O) outside.

Common Mistake

Missing indexes on foreign keys. Postgres does not automatically index foreign keys; missing ones make joins and deletes slow.

Common Mistake

SELECT * with no LIMIT. Returns whatever the table has — fine for ten rows, ruinous for ten million.

Principal Engineer Notes

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

A principal engineer wraps every multi-write operation in a transaction — and keeps the transaction as short as possible. The rule: validate first (outside), then transaction (write fast), then notify (outside).

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

They watch p99 query latency and slow-query logs in production. Missing indexes show up there before they show up in user complaints.

Exercises & Solutions

Practice — Transactions & Performance
  1. Write a signup that creates a user and a default workspace atomically. Make the second insert fail and confirm the user row does not appear.
  2. Insert 100k rows into a table; query by an unindexed column; add an index; query again. Measure both.
  3. Add a foreign-key index your schema is missing — confirm delete performance improves.
Solution & What to Check
  1. The transaction throws on the second insert; both inserts roll back. Querying for the user returns no rows.
  2. Seq Scan is slow (hundreds of ms on 100k rows); after CREATE INDEX, Index Scan is sub-millisecond.
  3. Cascading deletes from the parent are dramatically faster with the FK index in place.

Architecture Thinking

The database is your service's state of truth. Every production discipline — transactions for atomicity, pools for concurrency, indexes for speed — is in service of keeping that truth correct under load. Cutting corners here is the most common source of production incidents.

Debugging Tips

  • Slow query? EXPLAIN ANALYZE shows the plan. Look for Seq Scan on big tables.
  • Pool exhaustion ("client connection timeout") — too few connections, or a query is hanging. Check active query count in Postgres.
  • Deadlocks — two transactions locking the same rows in opposite order. Lock rows in consistent order across the codebase.

Performance Considerations

Performance

The database is usually the bottleneck of a Node service. Profile from queries outward: which queries are slow, which run too often, which return too much data. Cache deliberately (Module 9.2), batch writes when possible, use read replicas for heavy SELECT traffic. Database tuning beats most application- level micro-optimisation.

Build This — Mini-Project
A Transaction That Rolls Back

Two accounts. Two transfers — one succeeds, one fails. Watch the balances after each. The failure case is the lesson: atomicity is what keeps data consistent under partial failure.

css

  "name": "tx-demo",
  "type": "module",
  "dependencies": { "better-sqlite3": "^11.0.0" }
}

index.mjs (see Code Example)(see above)
Expected result:
code
after ok: [ { name: 'Alice', balance: 70 }, { name: 'Bob', balance: 80 } ]
rolled back: insufficient funds
after fail: [ { name: 'Alice', balance: 70 }, { name: 'Bob', balance: 80 } ]
Atomic transfer succeeded; failed transfer rolled back; balances are consistent. This is the production discipline every multi- write operation should follow.
