Module Six

Authentication & Security

Now your service has data; users need to authenticate to it. This module is the security floor: how passwords are stored, what JWTs and sessions are for, how OAuth's authorization-code-with-PKCE flow works, and the OWASP Top 10 vulnerabilities a Node backend must defend against. Getting these wrong is how breaches happen; getting them right is mostly using known- good primitives correctly.

6.1Sessions, JWTs & Password Hashing
6.2OAuth, OpenID Connect & Third-Party Login
6.3The OWASP Top 10 in Node Terms
By the end of this module You will have a working signup/login flow with Argon2id password hashing, a JWT issued and verified across requests, a full PKCE OAuth handshake (replay-rejected and interception-rejected), and four OWASP defences (parameterised SQL, escaped HTML, mass-assignment guard, timing-safe compare) each demonstrated by a passing assertion.
Module 6 · Chapter 6.1

Sessions, JWTs & Password Hashing

Three primitives every authenticated Node service uses: password hashing for storing credentials, sessions or JWTs for tracking who is logged in, and secure cookies for delivering them. The 2026 defaults are Argon2id for passwords and JOSE for JWTs; both are baseline-stable.

Lesson 6.1Identity Without Mistakes

Concept Overview

Password hashing is a one-way transformation — Argon2id (or scrypt) hashes the password with a per-user salt so the original cannot be recovered. You store the hash; on login you re-hash the attempt and compare. Sessions are server-side records of "this opaque cookie ID belongs to this user," typically in Redis or a database. JWTs (JSON Web Tokens) are self-contained signed tokens: the user's identity, role, and expiry are encoded in the token itself; verifying the signature is enough to trust the contents. Sessions are simpler and easier to revoke; JWTs scale across services without a shared session store. Production systems usually mix both — short-lived JWTs for service- to-service calls, server-side sessions for user-facing browsers.

Plain English Explanation

Three jobs need to be done. Store the password so an attacker who steals the database cannot recover it. Track who is logged in across requests. Deliver that identity to the client without it being stealable.

Password hashing. Never store passwords. Use Argon2id — a deliberately slow hash designed for passwords. The output is a string that includes the algorithm, the parameters, the salt, and the hash: $argon2id$v=19$m=65536,t=3,p=4$<salt>$<hash>. On verify, the library reads the parameters from the string itself — you do not have to remember which version produced which hash.

Sessions vs JWTs. A session is a record in your store: ID abc123 maps to user 42. The cookie holds abc123; the server looks it up to know who is logged in. Revoking is deleting the row. A JWT is a signed message: { sub: 42, role: 'admin', exp: ... } encoded into a token whose signature you verify with a secret. Nothing to look up — the token itself is the identity. The trade: JWTs are stateless and scale; but you cannot revoke a still-valid JWT without keeping a deny list somewhere (defeating the stateless property).

Cookie delivery. Always HttpOnly (JS cannot read it), Secure (HTTPS only), SameSite=Lax or Strict (CSRF defence). Short lifetimes; refresh-tokens for longer sessions. The cookie is the most attacked surface of any authenticated service.

Why It Matters

Every credential leak, session-hijack, and "why is the admin account compromised" incident traces to one of these primitives being used wrong. Use the libraries, not your own crypto; use the conventions, not clever tricks.

Real-World Example

A new project's first auth implementation: argon2.hash(password) on signup, argon2.verify(stored, attempt) on login, a random 32-byte session ID stored in Redis with a 30-day TTL, delivered in an HttpOnly Secure SameSite=Lax cookie. Logout deletes the Redis row. That is a complete, production-grade auth layer in about fifty lines. Anything fancier (rolling sessions, refresh tokens, impersonation) is an addition to this.

Enterprise Example

Enterprise systems combine: short-lived JWTs for service-to-service calls (15-minute expiry), refresh tokens stored server-side for re-issuing JWTs without re-login, server-side sessions for browser users. Password hashes use Argon2id with tuned parameters (calibrated to take ~250ms per verify on production hardware). Rate limiting on login endpoints. Account lockout after N failures. MFA for sensitive operations. Layered defences; each one is a small, understood primitive.

Step-by-Step Breakdown

  1. Signup: hash the password with argon2.hash(password); store the hash.
  2. Login: verify with argon2.verify(stored, attempt); if valid, issue a session cookie or JWT.
  3. Session route: HttpOnly, Secure, SameSite=Lax cookie; server-side row in Redis or DB.
  4. JWT route: sign with a strong secret, set short expiry, verify on every request.
  5. Logout: delete the session row, or add the JWT's jti to a deny list.
  6. Never rely on client-side fields for identity. Always validate the cookie/JWT server-side.

Code Example

code
auth.mjs — hashing + JWTimport * as argon2 from 'argon2';
import { SignJWT, jwtVerify } from 'jose';
import { randomUUID } from 'node:crypto';

// 1. password hashing — argon2id is the 2026 default
const password = 'correct horse battery staple';
const hash = await argon2.hash(password);
console.log('hash starts with:', hash.slice(0, 9));

const ok = await argon2.verify(hash, password);
console.log('verify good:', ok);
const bad = await argon2.verify(hash, 'wrong-password');
console.log('verify bad:', bad);

// 2. JWT — sign + verify a short-lived token
const secret = new TextEncoder().encode(process.env.JWT_SECRET ?? 'a-test-secret-at-least-32-bytes-long');
const token = await new SignJWT({ sub: 'user_1', role: 'admin' })
  .setProtectedHeader({ alg: 'HS256' })
  .setIssuedAt()
  .setExpirationTime('1h')
  .setJti(randomUUID())
  .sign(secret);
console.log('jwt parts:', token.split('.').length);

const { payload } = await jwtVerify(token, secret);
console.log('payload sub:', payload.sub);
console.log('payload role:', payload.role);

// Tampering test: change one char of the token, verify must fail.
try {
  const tampered = token.slice(0, -1) + (token.endsWith('A') ? 'B' : 'A');
  await jwtVerify(tampered, secret);
} catch {
  console.log('tampered token rejected: ok');
}

Three demonstrations in one file: a hash that you can verify good and bad attempts against, a signed JWT with payload, and rejection of a tampered token. The library handles the cryptography; your code uses it correctly.

JS & TS Together

JOSE is fully typed; jwtVerify returns a typed payload you narrow with your own z.object schema (or z.infer<typeof MyJWTSchema>). Argon2's API is small and clean in both flavours.

Common Mistakes

Common Mistake

Hashing with SHA-256 or MD5. Both are general- purpose hashes, fast on GPUs. Attackers crack billions per second. Use Argon2id or scrypt — slow by design.

Common Mistake

JWTs as long-lived "cookies." A JWT cannot be revoked without infrastructure. Use short expiries (15-60 minutes) and refresh tokens for longer sessions.

Common Mistake

Missing cookie flags. An auth cookie without HttpOnly can be stolen by XSS; without Secure it leaks on HTTP; without SameSite CSRF is easy. All three, every time.

Principal Engineer Notes

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

A principal engineer reaches for the library, never the algorithm. Argon2, JOSE, the standard cookie middleware — these are battle-tested. Hand-rolling crypto is the universal sign of inexperience.

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

They tune Argon2 parameters to take ~250ms on production hardware — slow enough to make brute force expensive, fast enough to keep login responsive. Calibrate, do not guess.

Exercises & Solutions

Practice — Auth Primitives
  1. Hash three different passwords and verify each. Confirm the hashes all start with $argon2id and contain different salts.
  2. Sign a JWT, decode the middle segment (base64), see the payload. Confirm changing one character of the signature makes verify fail.
  3. Add a refresh-token flow: short JWT (1m), long refresh token (1d), endpoint that exchanges a valid refresh token for a new JWT.
Solution & What to Check
  1. Each hash is unique because argon2 generates a fresh salt per call — even hashing the same password twice gives different output.
  2. The middle segment decodes to JSON {"sub":"user_1","role":"admin",...}. Any modification anywhere fails verify.
  3. Refresh endpoint validates the refresh token (server-side record), issues a new JWT, optionally rotates the refresh token. Logout invalidates the refresh row.

Architecture Thinking

Authentication is one of the most attacked layers of any service. The discipline is "use known-good primitives, follow conventions, layer defences" — never originality. Boring crypto is correct crypto.

Debugging Tips

  • "verify always fails" — wrong secret, wrong algorithm, or the token was issued with different parameters than verify expects.
  • "verify never fails" — you forgot to use jwtVerify and decoded without checking the signature. Always verify.
  • "cookie not sent" — browser doesn't send the cookie because SameSite blocked it, or the cookie domain is wrong.

Performance Considerations

Performance

Argon2 verify costs hundreds of milliseconds by design — that is the security. To handle high login throughput, scale horizontally; do not weaken the hash. JWT verify is sub- millisecond; verifying on every request is fine.

Build This — Mini-Project
Hashing & JWT in One Script

Run the code example; see the three lessons land: passwords are hashed and verified, JWTs sign and verify, a tampered token is rejected.

  "name": "auth",
  "type": "module",
  "dependencies": {
    "jose": "^5.9.0",
    "argon2": "^0.40.0"

Module 6 · Chapter 6.2

OAuth, OpenID Connect & Third-Party Login

"Log in with Google" is everywhere because OAuth 2.0 and its identity layer OpenID Connect make it standard. This chapter walks the modern authorisation-code-with-PKCE flow end to end — the exact dance Google, Microsoft, GitHub, and Apple all expect.

Lesson 6.2Letting Other Providers Sign Users In

Concept Overview

OAuth 2.0 is a delegated-authorisation protocol: your app asks a provider (Google, GitHub) for permission to act on a user's behalf, and gets an access token in return. OpenID Connect (OIDC) sits on top: in addition to the access token, it returns an ID token (a JWT identifying the user). The 2026 default flow is authorisation code with PKCE — the client sends a cryptographic challenge with the request and proves possession when exchanging the code for the token. The flow has four steps: redirect to provider, user authorises, provider redirects back with a code + state, exchange code for tokens. State protects against CSRF; PKCE protects against code interception.

Plain English Explanation

OAuth is "asking your bank if you have an account, without giving the asker your bank password." Your app does not see the user's Google password; it gets a token from Google saying "this user is valid." Four steps.

1. Build the authorisation URL. Your app generates a random state (a CSRF token) and a code_verifier + code_challenge pair (PKCE). Redirect the user to https://provider/authorize?...client_id...redirect_uri...state...code_challenge.

2. User authorises at the provider. They log in, approve the requested permissions. The provider redirects back to your redirect_uri with ?code=...&state=....

3. Verify state, exchange code for tokens. Your callback handler checks the returned state matches what you sent (CSRF defence). Then it POSTs the code + code_verifier to the provider's token endpoint; the provider verifies the verifier matches the challenge (PKCE defence) and returns an access token (and ID token if OIDC).

4. Use the tokens. ID token is a JWT — verify and read claims (sub, email, name). Access token is what you send to the provider's API. Most apps then create their own session for the user.

Why It Matters

"Sign in with X" is a baseline feature. Implementing it without understanding the flow is how leaked tokens, broken CSRF protection, and "we got hacked" stories happen. The flow is small; learn it once.

Real-World Example

A SaaS app's first auth method is "sign in with Google." The team registers an OAuth app with Google to get a client ID and secret. Their /login/google route redirects with state and PKCE; their /callback/google verifies state, exchanges code for tokens, verifies the ID token, looks up or creates a local user, issues their own session cookie. A library like arctic wraps this in ten lines per provider.

Enterprise Example

Enterprises use OIDC for single sign-on (SSO): the company's identity provider (Okta, Auth0, Microsoft Entra) is the OAuth provider for every internal app. Adding a new internal service means registering it as an OIDC client; users get one login across everything. SAML is the older equivalent still seen in legacy enterprise; OIDC is the modern default.

Step-by-Step Breakdown

  1. Register your app with the provider; get a client_id and a client_secret.
  2. For each login attempt, generate a fresh state and code_verifier; derive code_challenge = base64url(sha256(code_verifier)); store the verifier in a short-lived session.
  3. Redirect the user to the provider's /authorize endpoint with all the parameters.
  4. On callback, verify state matches the stored one; if not, reject (CSRF).
  5. POST the code to the provider's token endpoint with code_verifier; receive {access_token, id_token, refresh_token, ...}.
  6. Verify the ID token (a JWT) using the provider's public keys (JWKS endpoint); read the user's identity from the claims.
  7. Issue your own session for the user. The provider's tokens are only used for provider APIs after this.

Code Example

css
oauth.mjs — simulated authorisation-code-with-PKCE flowimport { randomBytes, createHash } from 'node:crypto';

const base64url = b => b.toString('base64')
  .replace(/=/g, '').replace(/\+/g, '-').replace(/\//g, '_');

// === Client side: build the authorization URL ===
const codeVerifier = base64url(randomBytes(32));
const codeChallenge = base64url(createHash('sha256').update(codeVerifier).digest());
const state = base64url(randomBytes(16));

const authUrl = new URL('https://provider.example/authorize');
authUrl.searchParams.set('response_type', 'code');
authUrl.searchParams.set('client_id', 'my-app');
authUrl.searchParams.set('redirect_uri', 'https://myapp.example/callback');
authUrl.searchParams.set('state', state);
authUrl.searchParams.set('code_challenge', codeChallenge);
authUrl.searchParams.set('code_challenge_method', 'S256');
console.log('redirect to:', authUrl.toString().slice(0, 60) + '...');

// === Provider side (simulated): user signs in, code issued ===
const fakeCodes = new Map();
const code = 'code_' + randomBytes(8).toString('hex');
fakeCodes.set(code, { challenge: codeChallenge });

const callbackUrl = new URL('https://myapp.example/callback');
callbackUrl.searchParams.set('code', code);
callbackUrl.searchParams.set('state', state);

// === Client side: verify state, exchange code for token ===
const cbParams = callbackUrl.searchParams;
if (cbParams.get('state') !== state) throw new Error('state mismatch — CSRF!');
console.log('state matches: ok');

// Exchange (simulated). PKCE: provider recomputes challenge from verifier.
function exchange(code, verifier) {
  const stored = fakeCodes.get(code);
  const recomputed = base64url(createHash('sha256').update(verifier).digest());
  if (stored.challenge !== recomputed) throw new Error('pkce mismatch');
  fakeCodes.delete(code);  // one-time use
  return { access_token: 'at_' + randomBytes(8).toString('hex'), token_type: 'Bearer' };
}
const token = exchange(cbParams.get('code'), codeVerifier);
console.log('got access_token:', token.access_token.slice(0, 12) + '...');

// Replay protection: the code is one-time-use.
try { exchange(code, codeVerifier); }
catch (e) { console.log('replay rejected:', e.message); }

Read the output: state matched, PKCE verified, token issued, replay rejected. In production, the provider-side functions are real HTTP endpoints — but the client-side code looks exactly like this.

JS & TS Together

Production: use a library — arctic (modern, tiny, per-provider modules) or openid-client (full OIDC). Both are TypeScript-first.

Common Mistakes

Common Mistake

Skipping state verification. CSRF: attacker crafts an auth URL with their own code, victim authorises it, attacker now owns the session.

Common Mistake

Skipping PKCE in single-page apps. The client-side app cannot keep client_secret secret; PKCE is the replacement.

Common Mistake

Trusting the access token to identify the user. Access tokens authorise API calls. ID tokens (or a userinfo endpoint call) identify users.

Principal Engineer Notes

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

A principal engineer uses an OIDC library (arctic, openid-client) — they do not hand-roll the flow. The library is the contract; their code wires it to the app's sessions.

Exercises & Solutions

Practice — OAuth
  1. Build the authorisation URL for a fake provider with PKCE parameters. Print it.
  2. Simulate the callback: verify the state matches; exchange the code with the verifier; receive a token.
  3. Tamper with the state on the callback URL. Confirm the handler rejects it.
Solution & What to Check
  1. The URL contains response_type=code, client_id, state, code_challenge, code_challenge_method=S256.
  2. State matches, PKCE verifies, token returned. Trying to re-exchange the same code throws.
  3. Tampering with state fails the equality check immediately — CSRF defended.

Architecture Thinking

OAuth's design is the principal-engineer pattern of "trust a proven specification over your own creativity." Every well-known provider implements the same flow; learning it once means working with all of them.

Debugging Tips

  • "redirect_uri mismatch" — your registered URI and the one you sent must match exactly, including port and trailing slash.
  • "invalid_grant" — code expired, was already used, or PKCE verifier doesn't match.
  • Use the provider's debugger — Google, Auth0, Okta all have OAuth playgrounds you can compare against.

Performance Considerations

Performance

OAuth happens at login only — performance is not the concern. What matters: cache the provider's JWKS keys (used to verify ID tokens) with proper expiry; do not fetch them per request.

Build This — Mini-Project
A Simulated OAuth Flow

Run the simulated flow and watch each step succeed. Then introduce each error in turn — bad state, wrong verifier, replay — and confirm the defences trigger.

code
oauth.mjs (see Code Example above)(see above)
Expected result:
code
redirect to: https://provider.example/authorize?response_type=...
state matches: ok
got access_token: at_2860cf783...
replay rejected: ...
The exact shape of every OAuth flow in production. Pick a library for real implementations.
Module 6 · Chapter 6.3

The OWASP Top 10 in Node Terms

The OWASP Top 10 is the industry's list of the most common web vulnerabilities. This chapter is the Node-flavoured version: what each one looks like, what defends against it, and where in earlier chapters that defence was already taught. Most security bugs are not novel — they are one of these ten, repeated.

Lesson 6.3The Repeatable Defences

Concept Overview

The OWASP Top 10 categories (2021 list, still current): broken access control, cryptographic failures, injection (SQL, command), insecure design, security misconfiguration, vulnerable and outdated components, identification and authentication failures, software and data integrity failures, security logging and monitoring failures, server-side request forgery (SSRF). Each has a specific Node defence: parameterised queries, Argon2, validation at the boundary, dependency scanning, timing-safe comparisons, structured logging. None requires hand-rolled crypto.

Plain English Explanation

Rather than read all ten in the abstract, focus on the ones a Node service hits most.

Injection. The classic: user input concatenated into SQL, shell commands, or queries. Defence: parameterised queries (Chapter 5.1) for SQL; escape or avoid shell; Zod-validated input (Chapter 4.4) at the boundary.

Broken access control. "Forgot to check that the user owns the resource they're trying to read." Defence: every handler that touches per-user data checks ownership; central helpers, not per-route ad-hoc.

Mass-assignment / overposting. "Took the request body and shoved it into the database." Defence: explicit allowlist of editable fields — never blindly spread ...body into a SQL UPDATE.

Timing attacks. Comparing secrets with === leaks timing information that helps attackers guess them. Defence: crypto.timingSafeEqual for any constant-time comparison.

Vulnerable dependencies. Defence: npm audit in CI; renovate for automated updates; minimise transitive deps.

Server-side request forgery (SSRF). Your server fetches a URL the user supplied — pointing at internal services. Defence: allowlist hosts; block private IP ranges; never let users control the host part of a server-side fetch.

Why It Matters

Most production security incidents are one of the OWASP Top 10 — not novel attacks. A team that defends against all ten by default rarely makes the news.

Real-World Example

An attacker sends POST /users/me { role: "admin" } to a "profile update" endpoint that copies request body fields into the database. The user is now admin. Defence: an explicit allowlist — const allowed = ['email', 'displayName'] — and the handler updates only those. Mass-assignment defended in five lines.

Enterprise Example

Enterprise teams run static analysis (semgrep) for known patterns, dependency scanning (Snyk, Dependabot), runtime protection (rate limiting, WAF), and red-team exercises. The development discipline — defend against each category by default — comes first; tooling catches what was missed.

Step-by-Step Breakdown

The defence checklist for any handler:

  1. Validate input at the boundary (Module 4.4). Zod schema for body, query, params.
  2. Parameterise database queries (Module 5.1). Never concatenate into SQL.
  3. Check authorisation — not just authentication. "Is this user logged in?" is different from "is this user allowed to see this resource?"
  4. Allowlist editable fields — never blindly spread request body.
  5. Hash passwords with Argon2id; compare secrets with timingSafeEqual.
  6. Block SSRF — never fetch arbitrary user-supplied URLs.
  7. Set security headers — CSP, HSTS, X-Content-Type-Options, etc. Hono's secure-headers middleware does this in one line.
  8. Audit dependencies with npm audit in CI; update regularly.

Code Example

code
owasp.mjs — three defences, side by sideimport Database from 'better-sqlite3';
import { timingSafeEqual, randomBytes } from 'node:crypto';

const db = new Database(':memory:');
db.exec(`CREATE TABLE users (id INTEGER PRIMARY KEY, email TEXT, role TEXT);
         INSERT INTO users (email, role) VALUES ('alice@x.co','user'),('bob@x.co','admin')`);

// === 1. SQL injection ===
function findByEmailUnsafe(email) {
  return db.prepare(`SELECT * FROM users WHERE email = '${email}'`).all();
}
function findByEmailSafe(email) {
  return db.prepare('SELECT * FROM users WHERE email = ?').all(email);
}
const attack = "' OR 1=1 --";
console.log('unsafe count:', findByEmailUnsafe(attack).length);
console.log('safe count:  ', findByEmailSafe(attack).length);

// === 2. Mass-assignment / overposting ===
const ALLOWED = ['email'];
function updateUserSafe(id, body) {
  const updates = {};
  for (const k of ALLOWED) if (k in body) updates[k] = body[k];
  if (Object.keys(updates).length === 0) return;
  const keys = Object.keys(updates);
  const sets = keys.map(k => `${k} = ?`).join(', ');
  db.prepare(`UPDATE users SET ${sets} WHERE id = ?`).run(...keys.map(k => updates[k]), id);
}
updateUserSafe(1, { email: 'a@x.co', role: 'admin' });
console.log('after safe   — alice role:', db.prepare('SELECT role FROM users WHERE id=1').get().role);

// === 3. Timing-safe comparison ===
const apiKey = randomBytes(32);
function checkSafe(given) {
  const a = Buffer.from(given, 'hex');
  if (a.length !== apiKey.length) return false;
  return timingSafeEqual(a, apiKey);
}
console.log('timing-safe equal:', checkSafe(apiKey.toString('hex')));
console.log('timing-safe wrong:', checkSafe('00'.repeat(32)));

The unsafe SQL returns 2 rows (every user) for the attack string; the safe version returns 0. The allowlist update writes only the email; the role is not changed. The timing-safe comparison returns the correct boolean without leaking timing.

JS & TS Together

TypeScript helps catch some classes of bugs at compile time (forgetting null checks, wrong types) but it does not make the OWASP problems go away. The disciplines in this chapter are equally important in TS and JS.

Common Mistakes

Common Mistake

Trusting client-side validation. The front-end's "must be a valid email" check is helpful UX, not security. The server validates again, every time.

Common Mistake

Authorisation via "is this user authenticated." Authenticated ≠ authorised. Check resource ownership for every per-user resource.

Common Mistake

Stack traces in production responses. Leak information about your service. Log internally; respond with opaque error codes (Module 4.4).

Principal Engineer Notes

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

A principal engineer treats every handler as a potential vector for one of the OWASP Top 10 and walks the checklist. The defences become reflexes.

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

They favour secure defaults over manual discipline. ORMs that parameterise by default, frameworks with security headers built in, validators that fail closed.

Exercises & Solutions

Practice — OWASP
  1. Build the unsafe SQL function and the attack. Confirm it returns every row. Convert to parameterised and watch it return zero.
  2. Implement the explicit-allowlist update; try to set role: 'admin' through it. Confirm the role remains unchanged.
  3. Compare two random buffers with ===, then with timingSafeEqual. The first works for value but leaks timing; the second is constant-time.
Solution & What to Check
  1. Unsafe: every row (the OR-true tail). Safe: zero rows. The same SQL parameterisation prevents every attack of this shape.
  2. Only the email is updated. Allowlists are simpler than blocklists and fail closed.
  3. === works for correctness; for secrets, the timing-safe version is required to prevent leakage.

Architecture Thinking

Security is not a feature to add at the end — it is a discipline applied to every handler. The OWASP Top 10 is the standardised checklist; running through it for every new endpoint catches most issues before they ship.

Debugging Tips

  • Run npm audit often. Most alerts have known fixes; resolve them promptly.
  • Set up CSP early. Content Security Policy headers prevent most XSS classes; configure once.
  • Log security events — failed logins, auth changes, admin actions. Detection is half the battle.

Performance Considerations

Performance

None of the defences in this chapter are expensive. Parameterised queries are at least as fast as concatenated ones (often faster — Postgres caches the plan). Argon2 verify is deliberately slow (login throughput is rarely a bottleneck). Timing-safe compare is microseconds. Security is essentially free.

Build This — Mini-Project
Three OWASP Defences in One Script

Build the SQL-injection, mass-assignment, and timing-safe defences. Run them; watch the unsafe versions break and the safe versions hold.

css
package.json{
  "name": "owasp",
  "type": "module",
  "dependencies": { "better-sqlite3": "^11.0.0" }
}
code
index.mjs (see Code Example)(see above)
Expected result:
code
unsafe count: 2
safe count:   0
after safe   — alice role: user

timing-safe wrong: false
Three OWASP defences in one place. Carry the disciplines into every handler from here on.

Module Seven

Real-Time & Background Work

Real systems do work outside the request-response cycle: live updates pushed to the browser, emails sent in the background, scheduled jobs, retries when something fails. This module covers the three shapes: real-time channels (WebSockets, SSE), background job queues, and the reliability disciplines (idempotency, retries) that keep systems honest when networks misbehave.

7.1WebSockets & Server-Sent Events
7.2Job Queues, Workers & Cron
7.3Idempotency, Retries & Reliability
By the end of this module You will have a working WebSocket server with echo and broadcast, a job queue with bounded concurrency, retry, and a dead-letter destination for failed jobs, and an idempotency-keyed endpoint that returns the cached response on retry without doing the work twice. The three primitives every production service eventually needs.
Module 7 · Chapter 7.1

WebSockets & Server-Sent Events

HTTP is request-response: client asks, server answers. Real-time systems need the server to push too — a new message arrived, the price changed, the upload finished. Two technologies do this in 2026: WebSockets (bidirectional) and Server-Sent Events (server-to-client only). Pick the right one and the rest is straightforward.

Lesson 7.1Pushing Data to Clients

Concept Overview

WebSockets are a persistent bidirectional connection between client and server — both can send messages at any time, framed as text or binary. The ws package is the Node default; Hono and Express both support it via adapters. Server-Sent Events (SSE) are simpler: an HTTP response that streams text events from the server to the client, one-way. SSE re-uses HTTP — no new protocol, no special infrastructure — and is enough for most "push notifications" use cases. WebSockets are required when the client also needs to push (chat, multiplayer games, collaborative editing).

Plain English Explanation

Imagine your client and server are on a phone call. With normal HTTP, the client speaks, waits, hears the server's reply, hangs up. With a WebSocket, they keep the line open and either side can say something whenever. With SSE, the server has a permanent radio broadcast going to the client; the client cannot speak back on that channel but can hear updates immediately.

WebSockets start as an HTTP request that "upgrades" to the WebSocket protocol. After the upgrade, both ends send and receive framed messages over the same TCP connection. On the server you typically track all open sockets and broadcast a message to all (chat room) or to one specific user (private notification). The protocol does not define what the messages mean — that is your application's job. Most teams use JSON envelopes: { type: 'message', text: '...' }.

SSE is the simpler choice when only the server needs to push. It is an HTTP response with content type text/event-stream that the server keeps open and writes events into. The browser's EventSource API consumes it with automatic reconnection on failure. For dashboards, notifications, log streams, and progress updates, SSE is often the right answer — simpler than WebSockets and plays nicely with HTTP infrastructure.

Why It Matters

Real-time features — live chat, live dashboards, collaborative editing — are increasingly expected. Polling (repeatedly fetching to check for updates) is wasteful and slow; WebSockets and SSE deliver updates with no polling overhead. Knowing both, and when to pick which, is part of the modern backend toolbox.

Real-World Example

A SaaS product needs to show a "build finished" notification when a background build completes. Polling would mean every dashboard hitting the server every few seconds — wasteful. With SSE, each dashboard opens one stream; when the build finishes, the server pushes one event; every connected dashboard updates immediately. Twenty lines of code; better UX; lower load.

Enterprise Example

Enterprise patterns scale this up. A chat product has a fleet of WebSocket servers behind a load balancer with sticky sessions; users connect to one server but messages broadcast across the fleet via Redis pub/sub. Cloudflare, AWS App Runner, and other modern hosts support WebSockets natively. Stateful connections complicate scale- out — that is the trade. SSE is easier here because each connection is "just an HTTP response."

Step-by-Step Breakdown

  1. Pick the channel. Bidirectional? WebSocket. Server-to-client only? SSE.
  2. For WebSockets, install ws (or use Hono's WS adapter); attach connection handlers; track open sockets; broadcast where needed.
  3. For SSE, set content-type: text/event-stream, keep the response open, write data: ...\n\n for each event.
  4. Use JSON envelopes with a type field so the client can dispatch on message kind.
  5. Handle reconnection. Networks drop. Clients retry; the server should treat a reconnection as a new connection with the same user identity.
  6. Authenticate. WebSocket upgrade includes cookies; verify them at the upgrade. SSE works the same way.

Code Example

css
server.mjs — WebSocket server with echo + broadcastimport { WebSocketServer, WebSocket } from 'ws';

const wss = new WebSocketServer({ port: 3000 });

wss.on('connection', socket => {
  socket.on('message', data => {
    const msg = data.toString();
    socket.send('echo:' + msg);
    // broadcast to everyone else
    for (const c of wss.clients) {
      if (c !== socket && c.readyState === WebSocket.OPEN) {
        c.send('broadcast:' + msg);
      }
    }
  });
});
css
sse.mjs — Server-Sent Events from Honoimport { Hono } from 'hono';
import { serve } from '@hono/node-server';
import { streamSSE } from 'hono/streaming';

const app = new Hono();
app.get('/events', c => streamSSE(c, async stream => {
  for (let i = 0; i < 5; i++) {
    await stream.writeSSE({ data: JSON.stringify({ tick: i }) });
    await stream.sleep(1000);
  }
}));
serve({ fetch: app.fetch, port: 3000 });

Two real-time patterns. The WebSocket version is bidirectional — clients send and receive. The SSE version is simpler when you only need server-to-client.

JS & TS Together

The ws package is fully typed. The browser WebSocket and EventSource APIs are typed in lib.dom. For typed message envelopes, define a Zod schema and parse every incoming message.

Common Mistakes

Common Mistake

Skipping authentication at upgrade. The WebSocket upgrade still includes cookies/headers. Verify them before accepting; do not accept anonymous connections by default.

Common Mistake

Broadcasting to all clients with no scoping. Users see each other's messages. Track which user owns which socket; broadcast within rooms/channels.

Common Mistake

Not handling disconnect. Browsers close sockets on tab close, network change, sleep. Clean up server-side state on close events.

Principal Engineer Notes

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

A principal engineer picks SSE by default and reaches for WebSockets only when bidirectional is needed. SSE re-uses HTTP infrastructure (load balancers, proxies, retries) and is operationally simpler.

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

For multi-server WebSocket fleets they use Redis pub/sub (or a managed equivalent) to broadcast across servers. Sticky sessions plus a pub/sub broadcaster is the standard shape.

Exercises & Solutions

Practice — Real-Time
  1. Build the WebSocket echo server. Use two terminal wscat clients; confirm one client's message appears in the other's terminal.
  2. Build the SSE counter. Connect with curl -N http://localhost:3000/events; watch ticks arrive once per second.
  3. Define a Zod schema for incoming WebSocket messages. Reject and log malformed messages instead of crashing.
Solution & What to Check
  1. One client sends "hi"; the same client sees echo:hi; the other sees broadcast:hi. The broadcast skipped the sender.
  2. Five SSE events arrive, one per second, with JSON {tick:0} through {tick:4}, then the response ends.
  3. The schema rejects messages that are not the right shape; the server logs the error and stays up. Never crash on bad input.

Architecture Thinking

Real-time channels are stateful connections — the opposite of HTTP's stateless model. State complicates scaling (which server holds this user's connection?), monitoring (open connections are a resource), and operations (rolling deploys drop connections). Choose stateful only when stateless cannot do the job.

Debugging Tips

  • "WebSocket connection failed" — wrong protocol (ws vs wss), wrong path, or middle infrastructure stripped the upgrade. Browser DevTools' Network tab shows the upgrade attempt.
  • "SSE events not arriving" — a proxy buffering. Add X-Accel-Buffering: no for nginx; set the correct content-type.
  • Memory growth over time — leaked connections. Track open count; ensure close handlers fire.

Performance Considerations

Performance

A single Node process can hold thousands of idle WebSocket connections — each is mostly an open socket file descriptor. Tens of thousands per process is achievable with tuning. SSE is similar: one HTTP response per client, kept open.

Build This — Mini-Project
A Self-Testing WebSocket Echo

WebSocket server plus a same-process client that connects, sends a message, receives the echo, and exits. End-to-end verification in one script.

css
package.json{
  "name": "ws",
  "type": "module",
  "dependencies": { "ws": "^8.18.0" }
}
code
server.mjs (see Code Example)(see above)
Expected result:
code
ws listening on 59300
connect
client got: echo:hello
Bidirectional channel up, message round-tripped. The shape every real-time feature builds on.
Module 7 · Chapter 7.2

Job Queues, Workers & Cron

Some work belongs outside the request: sending emails, processing uploads, generating reports, syncing with external APIs. A job queue is the standard pattern — the request enqueues a job, a worker picks it up and runs it, the result is recorded.

Lesson 7.2Work That Outlives the Request

Concept Overview

A job queue stores work items waiting to be processed. Workers are processes that pull jobs from the queue and execute them, with controlled concurrency. Each job has a unique ID, a payload, and metadata: attempts (how many times it has been tried), maxAttempts (how many times to retry on failure), backoff (how long to wait between attempts). For Node, BullMQ is the production default (backed by Redis); for in-process work, a small custom queue is fine. Cron jobs are scheduled — "run this at 02:00 daily" — and typically use node-cron or BullMQ's repeatable jobs.

Plain English Explanation

Imagine a restaurant. Orders arrive from waitstaff (HTTP handlers). Each order is written on a ticket and put on a rail in the kitchen — the queue. Cooks (workers) take the next ticket, prepare the dish, and mark it done. If the cook drops the dish, the ticket can be re-tried (retries). The cooks work in parallel up to the kitchen's capacity (concurrency).

The benefits over doing the work inline in the handler: the request returns immediately (the user is not waiting); workers process at their own pace (no backpressure on the request thread); failures retry automatically (network blips do not lose work); scaling is easy (add more workers).

In production, queues are typically backed by Redis (BullMQ's default) — the queue survives a server restart. For development or smaller services, an in-memory queue is fine, and writing one is a small project that makes BullMQ's API transparent.

Why It Matters

Inline work in HTTP handlers is the most common cause of slow APIs. Sending an email synchronously: the user waits 800ms for SMTP. Generating a report inline: the user waits 30 seconds. Pushing the work to a queue: the handler returns in 5ms, the worker runs the slow work in the background.

Real-World Example

Signup needs to send a welcome email. Inline: await sendgrid.send(...) in the handler — 800ms latency on signup. With a queue: the handler enqueues { type: 'welcome-email', userId } and responds 201 immediately. A worker pulls the job, sends the email, retries on SMTP failure. User experience improves; reliability improves.

Enterprise Example

Enterprise queues handle millions of jobs per day across categories: emails, webhooks to partners, sync jobs to external systems, report generation, image processing. BullMQ in production has typed jobs, configurable retry strategies (exponential backoff, capped), dead-letter queues for jobs that keep failing, dashboards for monitoring backlog. Cron-style scheduled jobs are first-class.

Step-by-Step Breakdown

  1. Identify the work to move out: anything slow, flaky, or that can fail without blocking the user.
  2. Define the job: a type and a payload. Keep payloads small (IDs, not full objects).
  3. Enqueue from the handler: queue.add(type, payload) — fast, returns immediately.
  4. Run workers with controlled concurrency. One worker per job type, or one worker that switches on type.
  5. Configure retries: max attempts, backoff strategy. Most transient failures resolve on retry.
  6. Handle permanent failures: after max attempts, move to a dead-letter queue for investigation.
  7. For scheduled work: cron syntax with BullMQ's repeatable jobs or node-cron for simple cases.

Code Example

css
queue.mjs — a tiny in-process queue with retriesclass JobQueue {
  constructor(concurrency = 2) {
    this.queue = [];
    this.concurrency = concurrency;
    this.active = 0;
    this.results = [];
  }
  add(job) { this.queue.push(job); this.tick(); }
  tick() {
    while (this.active < this.concurrency && this.queue.length) {
      const job = this.queue.shift();
      this.active++;
      this.run(job);
    }
  }
  async run(job) {
    let attempt = 0;
    while (attempt < (job.maxAttempts ?? 3)) {
      attempt++;
      try {
        const value = await job.fn();
        this.results.push({ id: job.id, ok: true, attempt, value });
        break;
      } catch (err) {
        if (attempt >= (job.maxAttempts ?? 3)) {
          this.results.push({ id: job.id, ok: false, attempt, error: err.message });
        } else {
          await new Promise(r => setTimeout(r, 2 ** attempt * 10)); // exp backoff
        }
      }
    }
    this.active--;
    if (this.queue.length) this.tick();
    if (this.active === 0 && this.queue.length === 0 && this.done) this.done();
  }
  async drain() { return new Promise(r => (this.done = r)); }
}

const q = new JobQueue(2);
let attempts = { c: 0 };
q.add({ id: 'a', fn: async () => 'ok-a' });
q.add({ id: 'b', fn: async () => 'ok-b' });
q.add({ id: 'c', fn: async () => { attempts.c++; if (attempts.c < 3) throw new Error('try again'); return 'ok-c'; } });
q.add({ id: 'd', fn: async () => { throw new Error('permanent'); }, maxAttempts: 2 });
await q.drain();
for (const r of q.results) console.log(r.id, '->', r.ok ? `ok in ${r.attempt}` : `fail after ${r.attempt}: ${r.error}`);

a and b succeed on first attempt; c succeeds on third attempt (the retry logic recovered it); d fails permanently after 2 attempts. The shape is what BullMQ generalises: concurrency, retries with backoff, success/failure tracking.

JS & TS Together

BullMQ is TypeScript-first; you declare a typed payload per job name and the worker receives the correct type. The mental model is identical to the in-process version above.

Common Mistakes

Common Mistake

Putting large data in the payload. Pass IDs; the worker fetches the data fresh. Big payloads bloat the queue and risk stale data.

Common Mistake

Workers without graceful shutdown. On SIGTERM the worker should finish its current job and stop pulling new ones. Module 8 covers shutdown.

Common Mistake

Retrying non-idempotent operations. A retried "charge the card" job double-charges. Make jobs idempotent (Chapter 7.3).

Principal Engineer Notes

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

A principal engineer designs every job to be safe to retry. The worker may be killed mid-execution and the job picked up again; outcomes must converge. Chapter 7.3 covers the idempotency discipline.

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

They monitor queue depth as a leading indicator. Backlog building = workers can't keep up = something is wrong upstream or downstream.

Exercises & Solutions

Practice — Queues
  1. Build the in-process queue. Add five jobs; observe two run in parallel.
  2. Add a job that fails the first two attempts and succeeds the third. Confirm the retry succeeds.
  3. Add a job that fails permanently. Confirm it ends up in the failed results with the expected error.
Solution & What to Check
  1. Concurrency 2: two jobs run at once; the queue drains in waves.
  2. The job succeeds on attempt 3; the result records attempt: 3.
  3. Permanent failure records {ok: false, attempt: maxAttempts, error}.

Architecture Thinking

Job queues are the architectural shape for "work that should not block the request and that must survive crashes." Once you have one, much of what used to be "background scripts" becomes typed jobs with retries and observability.

Debugging Tips

  • "Jobs not running" — worker not started, or stuck on a long job. Track concurrency vs queue length.
  • "Job retrying forever" — the job's error is transient-looking but the cause is permanent. Add a max attempts and dead-letter.
  • Queue depth metric in monitoring. Spikes mean something downstream is slow.

Performance Considerations

Performance

The queue itself is cheap; the work it runs is what matters. Concurrency is the tuning knob — too low and the queue backs up; too high and workers overwhelm the downstream. Match worker concurrency to downstream capacity.

Build This — Mini-Project
A Job Queue With Retries

Build the in-process queue, add four jobs (two succeed, one retries to success, one fails permanently). Watch the retry succeed and the permanent fail land in the failed results.

code
queue.mjs (see Code Example)(see above)
Expected result:
code
a -> ok in 1
b -> ok in 1
d -> fail after 2: permanent
c -> ok in 3
Two parallel workers; concurrency, retries, and permanent failures all visible. The same shape scales to BullMQ on Redis.
Module 7 · Chapter 7.3

Idempotency, Retries & Reliability

Networks fail, requests retry, workers restart. The distinguishing discipline of a reliable backend is idempotency — the same operation, repeated, produces the same result without duplication. Get this right and retries become safe; get it wrong and one network blip charges a credit card twice.

Lesson 7.3Designing for Repetition

Concept Overview

An operation is idempotent if calling it twice has the same effect as calling it once. Most read operations are naturally idempotent. Writes are not — a naive "insert a transaction" called twice creates two transactions. The standard pattern is the idempotency key: the client sends a unique key with each operation; the server records the key alongside the result; a repeated key returns the same result without re-doing the work. This is how Stripe, AWS, and every modern payments API handle "what if my request times out?" — the client retries with the same key, the server detects the duplicate.

Plain English Explanation

Imagine you press the elevator button. Pressing it again does nothing — the button is idempotent. Now imagine your bank's "transfer money" form. Pressing submit twice should not move the money twice. But if the network ate your first request and you press it again, you genuinely want it to succeed. How does the bank distinguish "this is a retry of my first attempt" from "I meant to transfer twice"?

The answer is the idempotency key. Before you submit, the client generates a UUID and includes it in the request. The server records it. On retry — same UUID, same request — the server finds the previously recorded result and returns it without doing the work again. On a genuinely new operation — different UUID — the server does the work for real.

The discipline applies to job queues too. A worker may be killed between "did the work" and "marked the job done"; on restart the job is picked up again. If the work is idempotent — the idempotency key is the job ID — the redo is harmless. If it is not — every retry double-charges.

Why It Matters

Networks are unreliable. Retries are unavoidable. Idempotency is the only way to retry safely. Skip it and you get classic incidents: duplicate charges, duplicate emails, duplicate webhook deliveries, all from "the customer clicked twice" or "the worker restarted."

Real-World Example

A payment endpoint accepts Idempotency-Key headers. Before charging, it checks a Redis (or DB) record keyed by the idempotency key. If found, return the recorded result; otherwise charge, record the result, return it. A network failure between "charge succeeded at Stripe" and "response reached the client" causes the client to retry with the same key — the server returns the recorded success without re-charging. Money safe.

Enterprise Example

Enterprise systems extend the pattern: idempotency keys have TTLs (24-48 hours); the recorded record includes both the response and the request hash so "same key, different request" can be detected as an error; webhooks delivered to partners include a delivery ID the partner uses for their own idempotency. Every external write has a key; every internal job has one too.

Step-by-Step Breakdown

  1. Identify side-effecting operations. Anything that writes, charges, sends, or otherwise changes external state.
  2. Decide on an idempotency key shape — usually a UUID. Clients generate it once per logical operation; retries reuse it.
  3. Store the key in a fast store (Redis, or DB with the operation's table) with a TTL.
  4. On request, look up the key first. Found: return the stored result. Not found: do the work, record the result under the key, return.
  5. For job queues: the job ID is the natural key. Use the database's row-level locking or a "claimed_at" pattern to ensure exactly-once-effective execution.
  6. For external calls (Stripe, etc.): use their idempotency-key feature too. The chain stays idempotent end to end.

Code Example

css
idempo.mjs — the idempotency key patternimport { randomUUID } from 'node:crypto';

const ledger = new Map();  // key -> { status, value }
let chargeCount = 0;

async function charge(idempotencyKey, amount) {
  if (ledger.has(idempotencyKey)) {
    return { ...ledger.get(idempotencyKey), replayed: true };
  }
  chargeCount++;
  const result = { status: 'ok', txId: 'tx_' + randomUUID().slice(0, 8), amount };
  ledger.set(idempotencyKey, result);
  return { ...result, replayed: false };
}

// 1. First call — does the work
const key = 'order_42';
const r1 = await charge(key, 1000);
console.log('first  :', r1);

// 2. Retry with same key — returns the same result, no duplicate charge
const r2 = await charge(key, 1000);
console.log('retry  :', r2);

// 3. Different key — new operation
const r3 = await charge('order_43', 500);
console.log('new    :', r3);

console.log('actual charges:', chargeCount);

Read the output: same key returns the same txId on retry (replayed: true); different key creates a new charge. Actual charges: 2 — even though charge was called three times. That is idempotency: safe to retry.

JS & TS Together

In TypeScript, declare the result type once and reuse it: type ChargeResult = { status: string; txId: string; amount: number; replayed: boolean }. Stripe's TypeScript SDK has built-in idempotency-key parameters; use them.

Common Mistakes

Common Mistake

Generating the key on the server. Defeats the point — every request gets a new key, no two are "the same." The client owns the key.

Common Mistake

Not storing the result with the key. Without the recorded response, a retry has nothing to return. Store the full response (or enough to reconstruct it).

Common Mistake

Keys with no TTL. The idempotency store grows forever. Set a TTL (24-48 hours is standard) so it self-cleans.

Principal Engineer Notes

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

A principal engineer assumes retries by default. Every write path gets a key; every external integration uses the partner's idempotency feature; every job is safe to retry.

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

They distinguish natural idempotency from enforced. Setting a status to "completed" is naturally idempotent — second call is a no-op. Charging a card is not — enforce with a key.

Exercises & Solutions

Practice — Idempotency
  1. Build the charge function. Call it twice with the same key; confirm only one charge happened.
  2. Detect "same key, different request" — record the request hash with the key, return an error on mismatch.
  3. Add a TTL: keys expire after N seconds. Confirm that after expiry, the same key creates a new charge.
Solution & What to Check
  1. Second call returns {...same result, replayed: true}; actual charge count stays at 1.
  2. Compare incoming body to recorded requestHash; mismatch yields HTTP 409 with an explanation.
  3. After TTL expiry, the key is forgotten; same key starts a fresh charge. Genuinely-retried-too-late requests become new operations.

Architecture Thinking

Idempotency is the contract that makes distributed systems workable. Once you have it, retries are no longer scary — they are the standard reliability mechanism. Without it, every network failure is a potential data corruption incident.

Debugging Tips

  • Duplicate operations? Either the client is not sending the key, or the server is not honouring it. Log both sides.
  • "Same key, different request" should be a loud error. Bugs that produce this surface immediately.
  • Audit external integrations for idempotency-key support — most modern APIs offer it; using it chains the safety through.

Performance Considerations

Performance

The idempotency lookup is one fast key-value read per request — sub-millisecond on Redis. The savings (no duplicate work, no incident recoveries) outweigh the cost by orders of magnitude.

Build This — Mini-Project
Idempotent Charges in One Script

Call the charge function three times — twice with the same key, once with a different one. Confirm only two actual charges happened despite three calls.

code
idempo.mjs (see Code Example)(see above)
Expected result:
code
first  : { status: 'ok', txId: 'tx_bb40e1dc', amount: 1000, replayed: false }
retry  : { status: 'ok', txId: 'tx_bb40e1dc', amount: 1000, replayed: true }
new    : { status: 'ok', txId: 'tx_c656e46b', amount: 500, replayed: false }
actual charges: 2
Three calls, two charges. The retry got the recorded result without re-running the work. This is the reliability contract every production write should obey.

Module Eight

Testing, Observability & Deployment

A service that does not ship safely is a hobby project. This module turns Node code into running production: testing (with Node's built-in node:test), structured logging and tracing for when things break, and the deployment disciplines — configs, secrets, graceful shutdown — that keep services correct under real-world operational pressure.

8.1Testing — node:test, Unit & Integration
8.2Logging, Metrics & Tracing
8.3Deployment, Configs & Graceful Shutdown
By the end of this module You will have a tested module with passing assertions via node --test, a service emitting JSON-line structured logs with bound per-request context, and a Hono server that handles SIGTERM by draining in-flight requests before exiting — every line of the four-line "graceful shutdown" sequence appearing on schedule.
Module 8 · Chapter 8.1

Testing — node:test, Unit & Integration

Node ships with a test runner. node:test is fast, zero-config, and good enough for everything from a small library to a large service. The disciplines are universal: unit tests for logic, integration tests for the wiring, contract tests for the seams.

Lesson 8.1Tests You Will Actually Write

Concept Overview

node:test is Node's built-in test runner — no external framework needed. test() and describe() from node:test declare cases; node:assert/strict provides assertions (assert.equal, assert.deepEqual, assert.throws). Run with node --test. Unit tests exercise pure functions or small modules in isolation. Integration tests exercise the wiring — your HTTP handler hitting a real (test) database. Mocking (Node's mock from node:test) lets you replace dependencies for unit tests; integration tests usually avoid mocks and use real ephemeral resources.

Plain English Explanation

A test is a small program that runs your code with known input and asserts the output is what you expect. A unit test tests one function with no external dependencies — given 2 and 3, sum returns 5. An integration test tests several pieces wired together — POST to the signup endpoint, check the database row appeared.

node:test is the modern default. Write test('description', () => { ... }); put assert.equal(actual, expected) inside; run node --test. The runner discovers *.test.mjs files automatically. describe() groups related tests. before() / after() / beforeEach() hooks set up and tear down.

For HTTP integration tests, hit your Hono app via its app.fetch directly — no real network. For database integration tests, point at an ephemeral Postgres (Testcontainers in production CI, or pg's default test database locally) and reset between tests.

Why It Matters

Untested code is code you cannot refactor safely. Tests are the guard-rail that lets a team change running systems without breaking them. The discipline of "every change comes with a test" sounds slow until you have lived without it; teams that have it ship faster, not slower, because they ship with confidence.

Real-World Example

A handler computes a tax amount. The unit test exercises the pure function with several inputs (zero, large, edge cases). The integration test posts an order to the API, looks up the saved record, asserts the saved tax matches the formula. Both tests catch different bugs: the unit test catches arithmetic mistakes; the integration test catches "we forgot to call the tax function from the handler." Both belong.

Enterprise Example

Enterprise testing layers: many fast unit tests, fewer slower integration tests, a small set of end-to-end tests. Pyramid by count and speed. CI runs unit tests on every PR (seconds), integration tests on merge to main (minutes), e2e against staging (longer). Coverage targets are tracked; flaky tests are killed ruthlessly. Snapshot tests for stable output shapes; contract tests for service boundaries (consumer and provider verify the same contract).

Step-by-Step Breakdown

  1. Files: *.test.mjs (or *.test.ts) next to the code under test.
  2. Imports: test, describe from node:test; assert from node:assert/strict.
  3. Write small focused tests: one logical assertion each. Tests with multiple unrelated asserts are harder to debug when one fails.
  4. Use describe for grouping: related tests under one heading.
  5. Run with node --test — fast, parallel by default.
  6. Integration tests: clean state. Set up fresh data in beforeEach; tear down in afterEach.

Code Example

code
sum.mjs — the code under testexport function sum(a, b) {
  if (typeof a !== 'number' || typeof b !== 'number')
    throw new TypeError('numbers only');
  return a + b;
}
css
sum.test.mjs — node:test in actionimport { test, describe } from 'node:test';
import assert from 'node:assert/strict';
import { sum } from './sum.mjs';

describe('sum', () => {
  test('adds two numbers', () => {
    assert.equal(sum(2, 3), 5);
  });
  test('rejects non-numbers', () => {
    assert.throws(() => sum('a', 1), TypeError);
  });
});
code
terminal$ node --test
✔ adds two numbers
✔ rejects non-numbers
ℹ tests 2
ℹ pass 2
ℹ fail 0

JS & TS Together

For TypeScript tests, run with node --experimental-strip-types --test or compile first with tsc. Most assertion patterns are identical; types help catch test bugs at compile time.

Common Mistakes

Common Mistake

Testing implementation details. Tests should verify behaviour, not internal mechanics. Internal refactors should not break tests.

Common Mistake

Shared state between tests. One test leaves a row in the DB, another assumes it. Reset between tests.

Common Mistake

Mocking what you should test. Mocking the database in an integration test means you tested the mock, not the database. Use a real ephemeral DB for integration tests.

Principal Engineer Notes

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

A principal engineer treats tests as documentation of expected behaviour. A new engineer reads the tests to learn what the code should do.

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

They write tests at the boundary — the public surface — rather than per private function. Behavioural tests survive refactors; mechanical ones do not.

Exercises & Solutions

Practice — Tests
  1. Write three tests for sum: addition, type-error case, and a fractional case.
  2. Write an integration test for a Hono handler — call app.fetch(new Request('/health')) and assert the JSON.
  3. Set up beforeEach to clear a test database table before each test that touches it.
Solution & What to Check
  1. Three test() blocks; all pass; the suite prints tests 3.
  2. app.fetch returns a Response; await res.json() for the body. No real network required.
  3. beforeEach(async () => await db.delete(users)) — every test starts clean.

Architecture Thinking

Tests are an investment in change tolerance. The same code becomes safe to refactor; the same team becomes faster at shipping changes. Skipping tests is borrowing speed from the future at high interest.

Debugging Tips

  • "Test passes locally, fails in CI" — shared state, environment differences, or timing. Make tests isolated.
  • Flaky tests — quarantine and fix immediately. A flaky test trains the team to ignore failures, which is worse than no tests.
  • Slow tests — split unit from integration; run unit on every PR, integration on merge.

Performance Considerations

Performance

node:test runs files in parallel by default — fast for unit tests. Integration tests sharing resources (database) need --test-concurrency=1 or per-test schemas. CI cost is real; keep tests fast.

Build This — Mini-Project
First Tests With node:test

Write sum.mjs and sum.test.mjs. Run node --test; see green. Break the function on purpose; see red.

code
sum.mjs + sum.test.mjs (see Code Example)(see above)
Expected result:
code
✔ adds two numbers
✔ rejects non-numbers
ℹ tests 2
ℹ pass 2
Two passing tests, no external framework, runs in milliseconds. From here, every module you write should ship with tests.
Module 8 · Chapter 8.2

Logging, Metrics & Tracing

When something breaks in production at 3 AM, your only friend is the log line you wrote three months ago. Structured logging, metrics, and distributed tracing are the three pillars of observability. Get them right and incidents become five-minute investigations instead of all-hands debugging sessions.

Lesson 8.2Knowing What Is Happening

Concept Overview

Structured logging emits JSON to stdout — one object per event, with consistent keys. Pino is the 2026 default for Node: fast, small, JSON-native. Metrics are numeric measurements aggregated over time — request counts, latencies, error rates — typically exposed via a /metrics endpoint scraped by Prometheus. Distributed tracing follows a single request across multiple services with a shared trace ID; OpenTelemetry is the standard. All three feed observability platforms (Grafana, Datadog, Honeycomb) where you query, alert, and dashboard.

Plain English Explanation

Logs are individual events: "this request started," "user authenticated," "error happened." Structured (JSON) logs are searchable: "show me every error with userId 42 in the last hour" is a one-line query. Plain text logs are not.

Pino writes one JSON object per log line. Each line has standard fields (level, time, msg) plus any extras you attach. A child logger binds context once (log.child({ requestId })) so every later line carries it. The result is a stream of self-describing events.

Metrics count things over time: http_requests_total{route="/users", status="200"} is a counter. Histograms bucket latencies: http_request_duration_seconds_bucket. Prometheus scrapes them; Grafana renders charts; alerting rules fire when a metric crosses a threshold.

Tracing connects related work across services. A single HTTP request flows through your API, a database call, and an external API; OpenTelemetry attaches a trace ID that follows each step. In the tracing UI you see the request as a flame chart of spans — which step took how long.

Why It Matters

A service without observability is one you can only debug by guessing. With it, every production question — "why did login slow down at 14:30?" "which user keeps getting 500s?" — has an answer in the logs and metrics.

Real-World Example

At 3 AM the on-call gets an alert: error rate on /checkout spiked. They open the dashboard: requests normal, latency up, error rate up. They open logs filtered by route=/checkout and level>=error: every error has err.code: 'ECONNREFUSED' and service: 'payments'. The payment service is down. Page the payments team. Total time from alert to diagnosis: five minutes. Without observability that takes hours.

Enterprise Example

Enterprise observability is layered: every log line carries service, environment, version, trace_id; metrics are pre- aggregated and exported per minute; traces are sampled (1 in N) and stored. Dashboards show SLO compliance (e.g. "99.9% of requests under 200ms"). Alerts page on SLO violation, not individual errors. Module 9's performance work uses these signals.

Step-by-Step Breakdown

  1. Use Pino as the logger. Configure once with a base level and service name.
  2. Log JSON to stdout. Cloud platforms parse JSON natively; never use console.log in production code paths.
  3. Attach request context with child loggers. A middleware creates a per-request logger (requestId, userId) and attaches it to the request.
  4. Standardise levels: debug, info, warn, error. info for events; warn for unexpected-but-recoverable; error for failures.
  5. Add metrics with prom-client: counters, histograms, gauges. Expose /metrics for Prometheus.
  6. Add tracing with OpenTelemetry SDK — auto- instrumentation covers HTTP, DB, common libraries.

Code Example

code
log.mjs — Pino with service context and child loggersimport pino from 'pino';

// Production: JSON to stdout — observability tools ingest it.
const log = pino({ level: 'info', base: { service: 'orders-api' } });

log.info({ userId: 42, route: '/health' }, 'request started');
log.warn({ retry: 1, reason: 'timeout' }, 'retrying');
log.error({ err: { message: 'db connection lost' } }, 'failure');

// child logger: per-request context attached automatically
const reqLog = log.child({ requestId: 'req_abc123' });
reqLog.info({ userId: 42 }, 'authenticated');
reqLog.info('completed');

Each line is a self-contained JSON object: service, level, timestamp, structured fields, message. A log aggregator turns these into a queryable database — "every authenticated event in the last 5 minutes" is one search.

JS & TS Together

Pino is fully typed; log.info(obj, msg) takes a typed structured object plus a message string. OpenTelemetry's Node SDK is typed; auto-instrumentation covers most workflows with no code changes.

Common Mistakes

Common Mistake

String-template log messages. log.info('user ' + id + ' logged in') defeats structured logging. Use a structured field: log.info({ userId: id }, 'login').

Common Mistake

Logging secrets. Passwords, tokens, API keys must never appear in logs. Redact at the source.

Common Mistake

Over-logging at info. Noise drowns signal. Reserve info for meaningful events; use debug for verbose diagnostics.

Principal Engineer Notes

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

A principal engineer writes logs for the future on-call. Each line should give a stranger debugging at 3 AM enough context to act. Vague messages are operational debt.

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

They alert on symptoms (user-visible) not causes (internal). "Error rate > 1%" pages; "CPU > 80%" only matters if it causes a symptom.

Exercises & Solutions

Practice — Observability
  1. Add a Hono middleware that creates a child logger per request with requestId and logs start + end with elapsed time.
  2. Add a prom-client counter for HTTP requests with route + status labels. Expose /metrics.
  3. Pipe Pino output to a file with node app.mjs | pino-pretty during development for human-readable logs.
Solution & What to Check
  1. Middleware: c.set('log', log.child({ requestId: randomUUID() })); log start; await next; log end with elapsedMs.
  2. Counter: new Counter({ name: 'http_requests_total', labelNames: ['route', 'status'] }). /metrics returns Prometheus text format.
  3. pino-pretty turns JSON into colourised, human-readable lines for local development.

Architecture Thinking

Observability is the system observing itself. Without it, every production question is guesswork; with it, you have evidence. The investment compounds: every event you log today is a tool when you need it.

Debugging Tips

  • If a bug only happens in production, the logs and traces are your only window. Make them rich.
  • Correlate by trace ID. One request across services share an ID; filter logs by it to see the whole flow.
  • Watch latency percentiles, not averages. A p99 of 5s means 1% of users wait 5 seconds — invisible in the mean.

Performance Considerations

Performance

Pino is one of the fastest Node loggers — sub-microsecond per line. Metrics overhead is similarly low. Tracing has overhead per span; auto-instrumentation lets you control sample rate to balance cost.

Build This — Mini-Project
Structured Logs With Pino

Run a script that emits info, warn, error, and child-logger lines. Confirm each is a JSON object with the service name, level, and your structured fields.

css
package.json{
  "name": "log",
  "type": "module",
  "dependencies": { "pino": "^9.5.0" }
}

Five JSON events; the last two carry the requestId from the child logger. This is the shape every production service should emit.
Module 8 · Chapter 8.3

Deployment, Configs & Graceful Shutdown

Shipping is a discipline. Twelve-factor configs via environment variables, secrets management, health endpoints, and graceful shutdown are the operational primitives every production Node service implements — the same way, every time.

Lesson 8.3From Code to Production

Concept Overview

Configuration lives in process.env — the twelve-factor convention. Validate on startup; refuse to boot if required values are missing. Secrets (DB passwords, API keys) come from a secret store (Vault, AWS Secrets Manager, GCP Secret Manager), not from files in the repo. Health endpoints (/health, /ready) let orchestrators (Kubernetes, load balancers) know whether the service is alive and ready for traffic. Graceful shutdown: on SIGTERM, stop accepting new connections, finish in-flight requests, close database pools, then exit. Done right, deploys cause no dropped requests.

Plain English Explanation

Config from env. The same binary runs in dev, staging, and production by changing environment variables. Validate them at startup — if DATABASE_URL is missing, fail loudly before any HTTP listener starts.

Secrets stored separately. Never commit a password to git. The secret store provides values at runtime (often via env vars injected by the deployment platform). For local dev, a .env file kept out of git is fine.

Health endpoints. /health answers "is the process alive?" (always true once running). /ready answers "is it ready for traffic?" (true only after DB connection, cache warmup, etc.). The load balancer probes these; failing health → restart; failing ready → remove from rotation but do not restart.

Graceful shutdown. When the orchestrator sends SIGTERM (deploy, scale-down), your service has a grace period (typically 30 seconds) before SIGKILL. Use it to: stop accepting new connections; finish in-flight requests; flush logs; close DB pool; close other resources; exit. Done right, users see no errors during deploys.

Why It Matters

Deploys are the most common production operation. A service that handles them poorly produces visible errors every time you ship. A service that handles them well makes a deploy invisible to users.

Real-World Example

A service rolls out a new version. Kubernetes starts a new pod; when its /ready returns 200, it joins the load balancer. Kubernetes sends SIGTERM to the old pod, which stops accepting new connections, finishes its 200 in-flight requests (typical: ~5 seconds), closes its DB pool, exits. Zero dropped requests; users see nothing. Without graceful shutdown, the old pod is killed mid-request and 200 users see 502s.

Enterprise Example

Enterprise deployment combines: containerised builds (Docker), declarative orchestration (Kubernetes), secret rotation (Vault/Secrets Manager), config validation (zod on env vars at boot), blue-green or canary rollout, and observability checks gating each step. The Node service itself supplies: /health, /ready, SIGTERM handler, structured logs, metrics. The rest is the platform.

Step-by-Step Breakdown

  1. Read config from env at startup. Use a schema (Zod) to validate; fail boot on missing required.
  2. Add /health and /ready endpoints. Health: trivially yes. Ready: actual checks (DB ping).
  3. Register a SIGTERM handler: stop new connections, wait for in-flight to finish, close resources.
  4. Track in-flight with a counter middleware so shutdown knows when to proceed.
  5. Build a container image (node:22-alpine; multi-stage to minimise size).
  6. Deploy behind a load balancer that respects the health/ready probes.

Code Example

css
server.mjs — graceful shutdown sketchimport { Hono } from 'hono';
import { serve } from '@hono/node-server';

const app = new Hono();
app.get('/health', c => c.json({ ok: true }));

// Track in-flight requests so shutdown waits for them.
let inFlight = 0;
app.use('*', async (c, next) => {
  inFlight++;
  try { await next(); } finally { inFlight--; }
});

const server = serve({ fetch: app.fetch, port: 3000 });

async function shutdown() {
  console.log('refusing new connections');
  server.close();
  while (inFlight > 0) {
    console.log('waiting:', inFlight, 'in flight');
    await new Promise(r => setTimeout(r, 50));
  }
  console.log('shutdown complete');
  process.exit(0);
}
process.on('SIGTERM', shutdown);
process.on('SIGINT', shutdown);

SIGTERM stops new connections, waits for in-flight to drain, then exits cleanly. Deploys cause zero dropped requests.

JS & TS Together

Validate env vars with Zod: const env = z.object({ DATABASE_URL: z.string().url(), PORT: z.coerce.number() }).parse(process.env);. TypeScript now knows the env shape; missing values fail boot loudly.

Common Mistakes

Common Mistake

No SIGTERM handler. The orchestrator kills your process mid-request; users see errors during every deploy.

Common Mistake

Reading env vars throughout the codebase. Validate once at boot into a typed config object; pass the object around.

Common Mistake

Committing .env. The .env file holds secrets and goes in .gitignore. A .env.example in the repo documents required variables without their values.

Principal Engineer Notes

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

A principal engineer treats startup as a checklist: validate config, connect dependencies, warm caches, mark ready. Refuse to start if anything required is missing. Loud failure beats silent corruption.

How Principal Engineers (L7+) Think

They make deploys boring. A boring deploy is a successful deploy; an exciting deploy is an incident.

Exercises & Solutions

Practice — Production Operations
  1. Add a Zod schema for env vars. Validate at startup; fail loudly with a missing variable.
  2. Add /health (always 200) and /ready (200 only when DB pings).
  3. Add the graceful shutdown handler. Simulate SIGTERM with process.kill(process.pid, 'SIGTERM') in a test.
Solution & What to Check
  1. Missing DATABASE_URL causes a clear error and exit. With it present, the service boots.
  2. /health is trivial; /ready tries a database query and returns 503 if it fails.
  3. The handler logs "refusing new connections", waits for in-flight, then exits. Test sees the sequence before process.exit.

Architecture Thinking

Production-readiness is not a final coat of paint — it is the contract your service has with the platform. Health, readiness, graceful shutdown, validated config, structured logs: these are the protocol the orchestrator speaks. Speak it correctly and your service is portable across every modern platform.

Debugging Tips

  • "Dropped requests during deploy" — almost always a missing SIGTERM handler.
  • "Service won't start" — usually missing env var. Validate at boot and the error message points right at it.
  • "Container restarts in a loop" — health check is failing or the process is crashing. Logs at boot tell you which.

Performance Considerations

Performance

Startup time matters for serverless and for fast restarts. Eager loading at boot vs lazy on first request: eager makes startup slower but the first request fast; lazy is the opposite. Module 9 covers startup performance.

Build This — Mini-Project

    "@hono/node-server": "^1.13.0"
  }

Graceful shutdown is a deploy that does not drop requests. Every production service should print this sequence on every rolling deploy.
